In the nearest field, where\r\nthe cotton was young and green, with no show of ripening, the overseer\r\nrode slowly between the rows, sprinkling plentifully the dry powder of\r\nparis green from two muslin bags attached to the ends of a short pole\r\nthat lay before him across the saddle.\r\n\r\nGr\xe9goire\'s presence would be needed later in the day, when the cotton\r\nwas hauled to gin to be weighed; when the mules were brought to\r\nstable, to see them properly fed and cared for, and the gearing all\r\nput in place. In the meanwhile he was deliciously idle with Melicent.\r\n\r\nThey retreated into the woods, soon losing sight of everything but the\r\ntrees that surrounded them and the underbrush, that was scant and\r\nscattered over the turf which the height of the trees permitted to\r\ngrow green and luxuriant.\r\n\r\nThere, on the far slope of the hill they found McFarlane\'s grave,\r\nwhich they knew to be such only by the battered and weather-worn cross\r\nof wood, that lurched disreputably to one side--there being no hand in\r\nall the world that cared enough to make it straight--and from which\r\nall lettering had long since been washed away. This cross was all that\r\nmarked the abiding place of that mist-like form, so often seen at dark\r\nto stalk down the hill with threatening stride, or of moonlight nights\r\nto cross the lake in a pirogue, whose substance though visible was\r\nnought; with sound of dipping oars that made no ripple on the lake\'s\r\nsmooth surface. On stormy nights, some more gifted with spiritual\r\ninsight than their neighbors, and with hearing better sharpened to\r\ndelicate intonations of the supernatural, had not only seen the mist\r\nfigure mounted and flying across the hills, but had heard the panting\r\nof the blood-hounds, as the invisible pack swept by in hot pursuit of\r\nthe slave so long ago at rest.\r\n\r\nBut it was "broad day," and here was nothing sinister to cause\r\nMelicent the least little thrill of awe. No owl, no bat, no ill-omened\r\ncreature hovering near; only a mocking bird high up in the branches of\r\na tall pine tree, gushing forth his shrill staccatoes as blithely as\r\nthough he sang paeans to a translated soul in paradise.\r\n\r\n"Poor old McFarlane," said Melicent, "I\'ll pay a little tribute to his\r\nmemory; I dare say his spirit has listened to nothing but abuse of\r\nhimself there in the other world, since it left his body here on the\r\nhill;" and she took one of the long-stemmed blood-red flowers and laid\r\nit beside the toppling cross.\r\n\r\n"I reckon he\'s in a place w\'ere flowers don\'t git much waterin\', if\r\nthey got any there."\r\n\r\n"Shame to talk so cruelly; I don\'t believe in such places."\r\n\r\n"You don\'t believe in hell?" he asked in blank surprise.\r\n\r\n"Certainly not. I\'m a Unitarian."\r\n\r\n"Well, that\'s new to me," was his only comment.\r\n\r\n"Do you believe in spirits, Gr\xe9goire? I don\'t--in day time."\r\n\r\n"Neva mine \'bout spirits," he answered, taking her arm and leading her\r\noff, "let\'s git away f\'om yere."\r\n\r\nThey soon found a smooth and gentle slope where Melicent sat herself\r\ncomfortably down, her back against the broad support of a tree trunk,\r\nand Gr\xe9goire lay prone upon the ground with--his head in Melicent\'s\r\nlap.\r\n\r\nWhen Melicent first met Gr\xe9goire, his peculiarities of speech, so\r\nunfamiliar to her, seemed to remove him at once from the possibility\r\nof her consideration. She was not then awake to certain fine\r\npsychological differences distinguishing man from man; precluding the\r\npossibility of naming and classifying him in the moral as one might in\r\nthe animal kingdom. But short-comings of language, which finally\r\nseemed not to detract from a definite inheritance of good breeding,\r\ntouched his personality as a physical deformation might, adding to it\r\ncertainly no charm, yet from its pathological aspect not without a\r\nspecies of fascination, for a certain order of misregulated mind.\r\n\r\nShe bore with him, and then she liked him. Finally, whilst indulging\r\nin a little introspection; making a diagnosis of various symptoms,\r\nindicative by no means of a deep-seated malady, she decided that she\r\nwas in love with Gr\xe9goire. But the admission embraced the\r\nunderstanding with herself, that nothing could come of it. She\r\naccepted it as a phase of that relentless fate which in pessimistic\r\nmoments she was inclined to believe pursued her.\r\n\r\nIt could not be thought of, that she should marry a man whose\r\neccentricity of speech would certainly not adapt itself to the\r\nrequirements of polite society.\r\n\r\nHe had kissed her one day. Whatever there was about the kiss--possibly\r\nan over exuberance--it was not to her liking, and she forbade that he\r\never repeat it, under pain of losing her affection. Indeed, on the few\r\noccasions when Melicent had been engaged, kissing had been excluded as\r\nsuperfluous to the relationship, except in the case of the young\r\nlieutenant out at Fort Leavenworth who read Tennyson to her, as an\r\nangel might be supposed to read, and who in moments of rapturous\r\nself-forgetfulness, was permitted to kiss her under the ear: a\r\nproceeding not positively distasteful to Melicent, except in so much\r\nas it tickled her.\r\n\r\nGr\xe9goire\'s hair was soft, not so dark as her own, and possessed an\r\ninclination to curl about her slender fingers.\r\n\r\n"Gr\xe9goire," she said, "you told me once that the Santien boys were a\r\nhard lot; what did you mean by that?"\r\n\r\n"Oh no," he answered, laughing good-humoredly up into her eyes, "you\r\ndid\'n year me right. W\'at I said was that we had a hard name in the\r\ncountry. I don\' see w\'y eitha, excep\' we all\'ays done putty much like\r\nwe wanted. But my! a man can live like a saint yere at Place-du-Bois,\r\nthey ain\'t no temptations o\' no kine."\r\n\r\n"There\'s little merit in your right doing, if you have no temptations\r\nto withstand," delivering the time worn aphorism with the air and tone\r\nof a pretty sage, giving utterance to an inspired truth.\r\n\r\nMelicent felt that she did not fully know Gr\xe9goire; that he had always\r\nbeen more or less under restraint with her, and she was troubled by\r\nsomething other than curiosity to get at the truth concerning him. One\r\nday when she was arranging a vase of flowers at a table on the back\r\nporch, Aunt Belindy, who was scouring knives at the same table, had\r\nfollowed Gr\xe9goire with her glance, when he walked away after\r\nexchanging a few words with Melicent.\r\n\r\n"God! but dats a diffunt man sence you come heah."\r\n\r\n"Different?" questioned the girl eagerly, and casting a quick sideward\r\nlook at Aunt Belindy.\r\n\r\n"Lord yas honey, \'f you warn\'t heah dat same Mista Gr\xe9gor \'d be in\r\nCentaville ev\'y Sunday, a raisin\' Cain. Humph--I knows \'im."\r\n\r\nMelicent would not permit herself to ask more, but picked up her vase\r\nof flowers and walked with it into the house; her comprehension of\r\nGr\xe9goire in no wise advanced by the newly acquired knowledge that he\r\nwas liable to "raise Cain" during her absence--a proceeding which she\r\ncould not too hastily condemn, considering her imperfect apprehension\r\nof what it might imply.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile she would not allow her doubts to interfere with the\r\nkindness which she lavished on him, seeing that he loved her to\r\ndesperation. Was he not at this very moment looking up into her eyes,\r\nand talking of his misery and her cruelty? turning his face downward\r\nin her lap--as she knew to cry--for had she not already seen him lie\r\non the ground in an agony of tears, when she had told him he should\r\nnever kiss her again?\r\n\r\nAnd so they lingered in the woods, these two curious lovers, till the\r\nshadows grew so deep about old McFarlane\'s grave that they passed it\r\nby with hurried step and averted glance.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX\r\n\r\nFace to Face.\r\n\r\n\r\nAfter a day of close and intense September heat, it had rained during\r\nthe night. And now the morning had followed chill and crisp, yet with\r\npossibilities of a genial sunshine breaking through the mist that had\r\nrisen at dawn from the great sluggish river and spread itself through\r\nthe mazes of the city.\r\n\r\nThe change was one to send invigorating thrills through the blood, and\r\nto quicken the step; to make one like the push and jostle of the\r\nmultitude that thronged the streets; to make one in love with\r\nintoxicating life, and impatient with the grudging dispensation that\r\nhad given to mankind no wings wherewith to fly.\r\n\r\nBut with no reacting warmth in his heart, the change had only made\r\nHosmer shiver and draw his coat closer about his chest, as he pushed\r\nhis way through the hurrying crowd.\r\n\r\nThe St. Louis Exposition was in progress with all its many allurements\r\nthat had been heralded for months through the journals of the State.\r\n\r\nHence, the unusual press of people on the streets this bright\r\nSeptember morning. Home people, whose air of ownership to the\r\nsurroundings classified them at once, moving unobservantly about their\r\naffairs. Women and children from the near and rich country towns, in\r\nfor the Exposition and their fall shopping; wearing gowns of ultra\r\nfashionable tendencies; leaving in their toilets nothing to\r\nexpediency; taking no chances of so much as a ribbon or a loop set in\r\ndisaccordance with the book.\r\n\r\nThere were whole families from across the bridge, hurrying towards the\r\nExposition. Fathers and mothers, babies and grandmothers, with baskets\r\nof lunch and bundles of provisional necessities, in for the day.\r\n\r\nNothing would escape their observation nor elude their criticism, from\r\nthe creations in color lining the walls of the art gallery, to the\r\nmost intricate mechanism of inventive genius in the basement. All\r\nwould pass inspection, with drawing of comparison between the present,\r\nthe past year and the "year before," likely in a nasal drawl with the\r\nR\'s brought sharply out, leaving no doubt as to their utterance.\r\n\r\nThe newly married couple walking serenely through the crowd, young,\r\nsmiling, up-country, hand-in-hand; well pleased with themselves, with\r\ntheir new attire and newer jewelry, would likely have answered\r\nHosmer\'s "beg pardon" with amiability if he had knocked them down. But\r\nhe had only thrust them rather violently to one side in his eagerness\r\nto board the cable car that was dashing by, with no seeming\r\nwillingness to stay its mad flight. He still possessed the agility in\r\nhis unpracticed limbs to swing himself on the grip, where he took a\r\nfront seat, well buttoned up as to top-coat, and glad of the bodily\r\nrest that his half hour\'s ride would bring him.\r\n\r\nThe locality in which he descended presented some noticeable changes\r\nsince he had last been there. Formerly, it had been rather a quiet\r\nstreet, with a leisurely horse car depositing its passengers two\r\nblocks away to the north from it; awaking somewhat of afternoons when\r\nhordes of children held possession. But now the cable had come to\r\ndisturb its long repose, adding in the office, nothing to its\r\nattractiveness.\r\n\r\nThere was the drug store still at the corner, with the same\r\nproprietor, tilted back in his chair as of old, and as of old reading\r\nhis newspaper with only the change which a newly acquired pair of\r\nspectacles gave to his appearance. The "drug store boy" had unfolded\r\ninto manhood, plainly indicated by the mustache that in adding\r\nadornment and dignity to his person, had lifted him above the menial\r\noffice of window washing. A task relegated to a mustacheless urchin\r\nwith a leaning towards the surreptitious abstraction of caramels and\r\nchewing gum in the intervals of such manual engagements as did not\r\nrequire the co-operation of a strategic mind.\r\n\r\nWhere formerly had been the vacant lot "across the street," the Sunday\r\nafternoon elysium of the youthful base ball fiend from Biddle Street,\r\nnow stood a row of brand new pressed-brick "flats." Marvelous must\r\nhave been the architectural ingenuity which had contrived to unite so\r\nmany dwellings into so small a space. Before each spread a length of\r\nclosely clipped grass plot, and every miniature front door wore its\r\nfantastic window furnishing; each set of decorations having seemingly\r\nfired the next with efforts of surpassing elaboration.\r\n\r\nThe house at which Hosmer rang--a plain two-storied red brick,\r\nstanding close to the street--was very old-fashioned in face of its\r\nmodern opposite neighbors, and the recently metamorphosed dwelling\r\nnext door, that with added porches and appendages to tax man\'s faculty\r\nof conjecture, was no longer recognizable for what it had been. Even\r\nthe bell which he pulled was old-fashioned and its tingle might be\r\nheard throughout the house long after the servant had opened the door,\r\nif she were only reasonably alert to the summons. Its reverberations\r\nwere but dying away when Hosmer asked if Mrs. Larimore were in. Mrs.\r\nLarimore was in; an admission which seemed to hold in reserve a\r\ndefiant "And what if she is, sir."\r\n\r\nHosmer was relieved to find the little parlor into which he was\r\nushered, with its adjoining dining-room, much changed. The carpets\r\nwhich he and Fanny had gone out together to buy during the early days\r\nof their housekeeping, were replaced by rugs that lay upon the bare,\r\nwell polished floors. The wall paper was different; so were the\r\nhangings. The furniture had been newly re-covered. Only the small\r\nhousehold gods were as of old: things--trifles--that had never much\r\noccupied or impressed him, and that now, amid their altered\r\nsurroundings stirred no sentiment in him of either pleased or sad\r\nremembrance.\r\n\r\nIt had not been his wish to take his wife unawares, and he had\r\npreviously written her of his intended coming, yet without giving her\r\na clue for the reason of it.\r\n\r\nThere was an element of the bull-dog in Hosmer. Having made up his\r\nmind, he indulged in no regrets, in no nursing of if\'s and and\'s, but\r\nstood like a brave soldier to his post, not a post of danger,\r\ntrue--but one well supplied with discomfiting possibilities.\r\n\r\nAnd what had Homeyer said of it? He had railed of course as usual, at\r\nthe submission of a human destiny to the exacting and ignorant rule of\r\nwhat he termed moral conventionalities. He had startled and angered\r\nHosmer with his denunciation of Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s sophistical guidance.\r\nRather--he proposed--let Hosmer and Th\xe9r\xe8se marry, and if Fanny were\r\nto be redeemed--though he pooh-poohed the notion as untenable with\r\ncertain views of what he called the rights to existence: the existence\r\nof wrongs--sorrows--diseases--death--let them all go to make up the\r\nconglomerate whole--and let the individual man hold on to his\r\npersonality. But if she must be redeemed--granting this point to their\r\nlittleness, let the redemption come by different ways than those of\r\nsacrifice: let it be an outcome from the capability of their united\r\nhappiness.\r\n\r\nHosmer did not listen to his friend Homeyer. Love was his god now, and\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se was Love\'s prophet.\r\n\r\nSo he was sitting in this little parlor waiting for Fanny to come.\r\n\r\nShe came after an interval that had been given over to the indulgence\r\nof a little feminine nervousness. Through the open doors Hosmer could\r\nhear her coming down the back stairs; could hear that she halted\r\nmid-way. Then she passed through the dining-room, and he arose and\r\nwent to meet her, holding out his hand, which she was not at once\r\nready to accept, being flustered and unprepared for his manner in\r\nwhichever way it might direct itself.\r\n\r\nThey sat opposite each other and remained for a while silent; he with\r\nastonishment at sight of the "merry blue eyes" faded and sunken into\r\ndeep, dark round sockets; at the net-work of little lines all traced\r\nabout the mouth and eyes, and spreading over the once rounded cheeks\r\nthat were now hollow and evidently pale or sallow, beneath a layer of\r\nrouge that had been laid on with an unsparing hand. Yet was she still\r\npretty, or pleasing, especially to a strong nature that would find an\r\nappeal in the pathetic weakness of her face. There was no guessing at\r\nwhat her figure might be, it was disguised under a very fashionable\r\ndress, and a worsted shawl covered her shoulders, which occasionally\r\nquivered as with an inward chill. She spoke first, twisting the end of\r\nthis shawl.\r\n\r\n"What did you come for, David? why did you come now?" with peevish\r\nresistance to the disturbance of his coming.\r\n\r\n"I know I have come without warrant," he said, answering her\r\nimplication. "I have been led to see--no matter how--that I made\r\nmistakes in the past, and what I want to do now is to right them, if\r\nyou will let me."\r\n\r\nThis was very unexpected to her, and it startled her, but neither with\r\npleasure nor pain; only with an uneasiness which showed itself in her\r\nface.\r\n\r\n"Have you been ill?" he asked suddenly as the details of change in her\r\nappearance commenced to unfold themselves to him.\r\n\r\n"Oh no, not since last winter, when I had pneumonia so bad. They\r\nthought I was going to die. Dr. Franklin said I would \'a died if Belle\r\nWorthington hadn\'t \'a took such good care of me. But I don\'t see what\r\nyou mean coming now. It\'ll be the same thing over again: I don\'t see\r\nwhat\'s the use, David."\r\n\r\n"We won\'t talk about the use, Fanny. I want to take care of you for\r\nthe rest of your life--or mine--as I promised to do ten years ago; and\r\nI want you to let me do it."\r\n\r\n"It would be the same thing over again," she reiterated, helplessly.\r\n\r\n"It will not be the same," he answered positively. "I will not be the\r\nsame, and that will make all the difference needful."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t see what you want to do it for, David. Why we\'d haf to get\r\nmarried over again and all that, wouldn\'t we?"\r\n\r\n"Certainly," he answered with a faint smile. "I\'m living in the South\r\nnow, in Louisiana, managing a sawmill down there."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I don\'t like the South. I went down to Memphis, let\'s see, it was\r\nlast spring, with Belle and Lou Dawson, after I\'d been sick; and I\r\ndon\'t see how a person can live down there."\r\n\r\n"You would like the place where I\'m living. It\'s a fine large\r\nplantation, and the lady who owns it would be the best of friends to\r\nyou. She knew why I was coming, and told me to say she would help to\r\nmake your life a happy one if she could."\r\n\r\n"It\'s her told you to come," she replied in quick resentment. "I don\'t\r\nsee what business it is of hers."\r\n\r\nFanny Larimore\'s strength of determination was not one to hold against\r\nHosmer\'s will set to a purpose, during the hour or more that they\r\ntalked, he proposing, she finally acquiescing. And when he left her,\r\nit was with a gathering peace in her heart to feel that his nearness\r\nwas something that would belong to her again; but differently as he\r\nassured her. And she believed him, knowing that he would stand to his\r\npromise.\r\n\r\nHer life was sometimes very blank in the intervals of street\r\nperambulations and matin\xe9es and reading of morbid literature. That\r\nelation which she had felt over her marriage with Hosmer ten years\r\nbefore, had soon died away, together with her weak love for him, when\r\nshe began to dread him and defy him. But now that he said he was ready\r\nto take care of her and be good to her, she felt great comfort in her\r\nknowledge of his honesty.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX\r\n\r\nFanny\'s Friends.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was on the day following Hosmer\'s visit, that Mrs. Lorenzo\r\nWorthington, familiarly known to her friends as Belle Worthington, was\r\noccupied in constructing a careful and extremely elaborate street\r\ntoilet before her dressing bureau which stood near the front window of\r\none of the "flats" opposite Mrs. Larimore\'s. The Nottingham curtain\r\nscreened her effectually from the view of passers-by without hindering\r\nher frequent observance of what transpired in the street.\r\n\r\nThe lower portion of this lady\'s figure was draped, or better,\r\nseemingly supported, by an abundance of stiffly starched white\r\npetticoats that rustled audibly at her slightest movement. Her neck\r\nwas bare, as were the well shaped arms that for the past five minutes\r\nhad been poised in mid-air, in the arrangement of a front of\r\nexquisitely soft blonde curls, which she had taken from her "top\r\ndrawer" and was adjusting, with the aid of a multitude of tiny\r\ninvisible hair-pins, to her own very smoothly brushed hair. Yellow\r\nhair it was, with a suspicious darkness about the roots, and a\r\nstreakiness about the back, that to an observant eye would have more\r\nthan hinted that art had assisted nature in coloring Mrs.\r\nWorthington\'s locks.\r\n\r\nDressed, and evidently waiting with forced patience for the\r\ntermination of these overhead maneuvers of her friend, sat Lou,--Mrs.\r\nJack Dawson,--a woman whom most people called handsome. If she were\r\nhandsome, no one could have told why, for her beauty was a thing which\r\ncould not be defined. She was tall and thin, with hair, eyes, and\r\ncomplexion of a brownish neutral tint, and bore in face and figure, a\r\nstamp of defiance which probably accounted for a certain eccentricity\r\nin eschewing hair dyes and cosmetics. Her face was full of little\r\nirregularities; a hardly perceptible cast in one eye; the nose drawn a\r\nbit to one side, and the mouth twitched decidedly to the other when\r\nshe talked or laughed. It was this misproportion which gave a piquancy\r\nto her expression and which in charming people, no doubt made them\r\nbelieve her handsome.\r\n\r\nMrs. Worthington\'s coiffure being completed, she regaled herself with\r\na deliberate and comprehensive glance into the street, and the outcome\r\nof her observation was the sudden exclamation.\r\n\r\n"Well I\'ll be switched! come here quick Lou. If there ain\'t Fanny\r\nLarimore getting on the car with Dave Hosmer!"\r\n\r\nMrs. Dawson approached the window, but without haste; and in no wise\r\nsharing her friend\'s excitement, gave utterance to her calm opinion.\r\n\r\n"They\'ve made it up, I\'ll bet you what you want."\r\n\r\nSurprise seemed for the moment to have deprived Mrs. Worthington of\r\nfurther ability to proceed with her toilet, for she had fallen into a\r\nchair as limply as her starched condition would permit, her face full\r\nof speculation.\r\n\r\n"See here, Belle Worthington, if we\'ve got to be at the \'Lympic at two\r\no\'clock, you\'d better be getting a move on yourself."\r\n\r\n"Yes, I know; but I declare, you might knock me down with a feather."\r\n\r\nA highly overwrought figure of speech on the part of Mrs. Worthington,\r\nseeing that the feather which would have prostrated her must have met\r\na resistance of some one hundred and seventy-five pounds of solid\r\navoirdupois.\r\n\r\n"After all she said about him, too!" seeking to draw her friend into\r\nsome participation in her own dumbfoundedness.\r\n\r\n"Well, you ought to know Fanny Larimore\'s a fool, don\'t you?"\r\n\r\n"Well, but I just can\'t get over it; that\'s all there is about it."\r\nAnd Mrs. Worthington went about completing the adornment of her person\r\nin a state of voiceless stupefaction.\r\n\r\nIn full garb, she presented the figure of a splendid woman; trim and\r\ntight in a black silk gown of expensive quality, heavy with jets which\r\nhung and shone, and jangled from every available point of her person.\r\nNot a thread of her yellow hair was misplaced. She shone with\r\ncleanliness, and her broad expressionless face and meaningless blue\r\neyes were set to a good-humored readiness for laughter, which would be\r\nwholesome if not musical. She exhaled a fragrance of patchouly or\r\njockey-club, or something odorous and "strong" that clung to every\r\narticle of her apparel, even to the yellow kid gloves which she would\r\nnow be forced to put on during her ride in the car. Mrs. Dawson,\r\nattired with equal richness and style, showed more of individuality in\r\nher toilet.\r\n\r\nAs they quitted the house she observed to her friend:\r\n\r\n"I wish you\'d let up on that smell; it\'s enough to sicken a body."\r\n\r\n"I know you don\'t like it, Lou," was Mrs. Worthington\'s apologetic and\r\nhalf disconcerted reply, "and I was careful as could be. Give you my\r\nword, I didn\'t think you could notice it."\r\n\r\n"Notice it? Gee!" responded Mrs. Dawson.\r\n\r\nThese were two ladies of elegant leisure, the conditions of whose\r\nlives, and the amiability of whose husbands, had enabled them to\r\ndevelop into finished and professional time-killers.\r\n\r\nTheir intimacy with each other, as also their close acquaintance with\r\nFanny Larimore, dated from a couple of years after that lady\'s\r\nmarriage, when they had met as occupants of the same big up-town\r\nboarding house. The intercourse had never since been permitted to die\r\nout. Once, when the two former ladies were on a visit to Mrs.\r\nLarimore, seeing the flats in course of construction, they were at\r\nonce assailed with the desire to abandon their hitherto nomadic life,\r\nand settle to the responsibilities of housekeeping; a scheme which\r\nthey carried into effect as soon as the houses became habitable.\r\n\r\nThere was a Mr. Lorenzo Worthington; a gentleman employed for many\r\nyears past in the custom house. Whether he had been overlooked, which\r\nhis small unobtrusive, narrow-chested person made possible--or whether\r\nhis many-sided usefulness had rendered him in a manner indispensable\r\nto his employers, does not appear; but he had remained at his post\r\nduring the various changes of administration that had gone by since\r\nhis first appointment.\r\n\r\nDuring intervals of his work--intervals often occurring of afternoon\r\nhours, when he had been given night work--he was fond of sitting at\r\nthe sunny kitchen window, with his long thin nose, and shortsighted\r\neyes plunged between the pages of one of his precious books: a small\r\nhoard of which he had collected at some cost and more self-denial.\r\n\r\nOne of the grievances of his life was the necessity under which he\r\nfound himself of protecting his treasure from the Philistine abuse and\r\ncontempt of his wife. When they moved into the flat, Mrs. Worthington,\r\nduring her husband\'s absence, had ranged them all, systematically\r\nenough, on the top shelf of the kitchen closet to "get them out of the\r\nway." But at this he had protested, and taken a positive stand, to\r\nwhich his wife had so far yielded as to permit that they be placed on\r\nthe top shelf of the bedroom closet; averring that to have them laying\r\naround was a thing that she would not do, for they spoilt the looks of\r\nany room.\r\n\r\nHe had not foreseen the possibility of their usefulness being a\r\ntemptation to his wife in so handy a receptacle.\r\n\r\nSeeking once a volume of Ruskin\'s Miscellanies, he discovered that it\r\nhad been employed to support the dismantled leg of a dressing bureau.\r\nOn another occasion, a volume of Schopenhauer, which he had been at\r\nmuch difficulty and expense to procure, Emerson\'s Essays, and two\r\nother volumes much prized, he found had served that lady as weights to\r\nhold down a piece of dry goods which she had sponged and spread to dry\r\non an available section of roof top.\r\n\r\nHe was glad enough to transport them all back to the safer refuge of\r\nthe kitchen closet, and pay the hired girl a secret stipend to guard\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nMr. Worthington regarded women as being of peculiar and unsuitable\r\nconformation to the various conditions of life amid which they are\r\nplaced; with strong moral proclivities, for the most part subservient\r\nto a weak and inadequate mentality.\r\n\r\nIt was not his office to remodel them; his r\xf4le was simply to endure\r\nwith patience the vagaries of an order of human beings, who after all,\r\noffered an interesting study to a man of speculative habit, apart from\r\ntheir usefulness as propagators of the species.\r\n\r\nAs regards this last qualification, Mrs. Worthington had done less\r\nthan her fair share, having but one child, a daughter of twelve, whose\r\ntraining and education had been assumed by an aunt of her father\'s, a\r\nnun of some standing in the Sacred Heart Convent.\r\n\r\nQuite a different type of man was Jack Dawson, Lou\'s husband. Short,\r\nround, young, blonde, good looking and bald--as what St. Louis man\r\npast thirty is not? he rejoiced in the agreeable calling of a\r\ntraveling salesman.\r\n\r\nOn the occasions when he was at home; once in two weeks--sometimes\r\nseldomer--never oftener--the small flat was turned inside out and\r\nupside down. He filled it with noise and merriment. If a theater party\r\nwere not on hand, it was a spin out to Forest park behind a fast team,\r\nclosing with a wine supper at a road-side restaurant. Or a card party\r\nwould be hastily gathered to which such neighbors as were congenial\r\nwere bid in hot haste; deficiencies being supplied from his large\r\ncircle of acquaintances who happened not to be on the road, and who at\r\nthe eleventh hour were rung up by telephone. On such occasions Jack\'s\r\nvoice would be heard loud in anecdote, introduced in some such wise as\r\n"When I was in Houston, Texas, the other day," or "Tell you what it\r\nis, sir, those fellers over in Albuquerque are up to a thing or two."\r\n\r\nOne of his standing witticisms was to inquire in a stage whisper of\r\nBelle or Lou--whether the little gal over the way had taken the pledge\r\nyet.\r\n\r\nThis gentleman and his wife were on the most amiable of terms\r\ntogether, barring the small grievance that he sometimes lost money at\r\npoker. But as losing was exceptional with him, and as he did not make\r\nit a matter of conscience to keep her at all times posted as to the\r\nfluctuations of his luck, this grievance had small occasion to show\r\nitself.\r\n\r\nWhat he thought of his wife, might best be told in his own language:\r\nthat Lou was up to the mark and game every time; feminine\r\ncharacteristics which he apparently held in high esteem.\r\n\r\nThe two ladies in question had almost reached the terminus of their\r\nride, when Mrs. Worthington remarked incidentally to her friend, "It\r\nwas nothing in the God\'s world but pure sass brought those two fellers\r\nto see you last night, Lou."\r\n\r\nMrs. Dawson bit her lip and the cast in her eye became more\r\naccentuated, as it was apt to do when she was ruffled.\r\n\r\n"I notice you didn\'t treat \'em any too cool yourself," she retorted.\r\n\r\n"Oh, they weren\'t my company, or I\'d a give \'em a piece of my mind\r\npretty quick. You know they\'re married, and they know you\'re married,\r\nand they hadn\'t a bit o\' business there."\r\n\r\n"They\'re perfect gentlemen, and I don\'t see what business \'tis of\r\nyours, anyway."\r\n\r\n"Oh that\'s a horse of another color," replied Mrs. Worthington,\r\nbridling and relapsing into injured silence for the period of ten\r\nseconds, when she resumed, "I hope they ain\'t going to poke themselves\r\nat the matin\xe9e."\r\n\r\n"Likely they will \'s long as they gave us the tickets."\r\n\r\nOne of the gentlemen was at the matin\xe9e: Mr. Bert Rodney, but he\r\ncertainly had not "poked" himself there. He never did any thing vulgar\r\nor in bad taste. He had only "dropped in!" Exquisite in dress and\r\nmanner, a swell of the upper circles, versed as was no one better in\r\nthe code of gentlemanly etiquette--he was for the moment awaiting\r\ndisconsolately the return of his wife and daughter from Narragansett.\r\n\r\nHe took a vacant seat behind the two ladies, and bending forward began\r\nto talk to them in his low and fascinating drawl.\r\n\r\nMrs. Worthington, who often failed to accomplish her fierce designs,\r\nwas as gracious towards him as if she had harbored no desire to give\r\nhim a piece of her mind; but she was resolute in her refusal to make\r\none of a proposed supper party.\r\n\r\nA quiet sideward look from Mrs. Dawson, told Mr. Rodney as plainly as\r\nwords, that in the event of his _partie-carr\xe9e_ failing him, he might\r\ncount upon her for a _t\xeate-\xe0-t\xeate_.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXI\r\n\r\nThe Self-Assumed Burden.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe wedding was over. Hosmer and Fanny had been married in the small\r\nlibrary of their Unitarian minister whom they had found intent upon\r\nthe shaping of his Sunday sermon.\r\n\r\nOut of deference, he had been briefly told the outward circumstances\r\nof the case, which he knew already; for these two had been formerly\r\nmembers of his congregation, and gossip had not been reluctant in\r\ntelling their story. Hosmer, of course, had drifted away from his\r\nknowledge, and in late years, he had seen little of Fanny, who when\r\nmoved to attend church at all usually went to the Redemptorist\'s Rock\r\nChurch with her friend Belle Worthington. This lady was a good\r\nCatholic to the necessary extent of hearing a mass on Sundays,\r\nabstaining from meat on Fridays and Ember days, and making her\r\n"Easters." Which concessions were not without their attendant\r\ndiscomforts, counterbalanced, however, by the soothing assurance which\r\nthey gave her of keeping on the safe side.\r\n\r\nThe minister had been much impressed with the significance of this\r\nre-marriage which he was called upon to perform, and had offered some\r\nfew and well chosen expressions of salutary advice as to its future\r\nguidance. The sexton and housekeeper had been called in as witnesses.\r\nThen Hosmer had taken Fanny back home in a cab as she requested,\r\nbecause of her eyes that were red and swollen.\r\n\r\nInside the little hall-way he took her in his arms and kissed her,\r\ncalling her "my child." He could not have told why, except that it\r\nexpressed the responsibility he accepted of bearing all things that a\r\nfather must bear from the child to whom he has given life.\r\n\r\n"I should like to go out for an hour, Fanny; but if you would rather\r\nnot, I shall stay."\r\n\r\n"No, David, I want to be alone," she said, turning into the little\r\nparlor, with eyes big and heavy from weariness and inward clashing\r\nemotions.\r\n\r\nAlong the length of Lindell avenue from Grand avenue west to Forest\r\npark, reaches for two miles on either side of the wide and well kept\r\ngravel drive a smooth stone walk, bordered its full extent with a\r\ndouble row of trees which were young and still uncertain, when Hosmer\r\nwalked between them.\r\n\r\nHad it been Sunday, he would have found himself making one of a\r\nfashionable throng of promenaders; it being at that time a fad with\r\nsociety people to walk to Forest park and back of a Sunday afternoon.\r\nDriving was then considered a respectable diversion only on the six\r\nwork days of the week.\r\n\r\nBut it was not Sunday and this inviting promenade was almost deserted.\r\nAn occasional laborer would walk clumsily by; apathetic; swinging his\r\ntin bucket and bearing some implement of toil with the yellow clay yet\r\nclinging to it. Or it might be a brace of strong-minded girls walking\r\nwith long and springing stride, which was then fashionable; looking\r\nnot to the right nor left; indulging in no exchange of friendly and\r\ngirlish chatter, but grimly intent upon the purpose of their walk.\r\n\r\nA steady line of vehicles was pushing on towards the park at the\r\nmoderate speed which the law required. On both sides the wide\r\nboulevard tasteful dwellings, many completed, but most of them in\r\ncourse of construction, were in constant view. Hosmer noted every\r\nthing, but absently; and yet he was not pre-occupied with thought. He\r\nfelt himself to be hurrying away from something that was fast\r\novertaking him, and his faculties for the moment were centered in the\r\nmere act of motion. It is said that motion is pleasurable to man. No\r\ndoubt, in connection with a healthy body and free mind, movement\r\nbrings to the normal human being a certain degree of enjoyment. But\r\nwhere the healthful conditions are only physical, rapid motion changes\r\nfrom a source of pleasure to one of mere expediency.\r\n\r\nSo long as Hosmer could walk he kept a certain pressing consciousness\r\nat bay. He would have liked to run if he had dared. Since he had\r\nentered the park there were constant trains of cars speeding somewhere\r\noverhead; he could hear them at near intervals clashing over the stone\r\nbridge. And there was not a train which passed that he did not long to\r\nbe at the front of it to measure and let out its speed. What a mad\r\nflight he would have given it, to make men hold their breath with\r\nterror! How he would have driven it till its end was death and\r\nchaos!--so much the better.\r\n\r\nThere suddenly formed in Hosmer\'s mind a sentence--sharp and distinct.\r\nWe are all conscious of such quick mental visions whether of words or\r\npictures, coming sometimes from a hidden and untraceable source,\r\nmaking us quiver with awe at this mysterious power of mind manifesting\r\nitself with the vividness of visible matter.\r\n\r\n"It was the act of a coward."\r\n\r\nThose were the words which checked him, and forbade him to go farther:\r\nwhich compelled him to turn about and face the reality of his\r\nconvictions.\r\n\r\nIt is no unusual sight, that of a man lying full length in the soft\r\ntender grass of some retired spot of Forest park--with his face hidden\r\nin his folded arms. To the few who may see him, if they speculate at\r\nall about him he sleeps or he rests his body after a day\'s fatigue.\r\n"Am I never to be the brave man?" thought Hosmer, "always the coward,\r\nflying even from my own thoughts?"\r\n\r\nHow hard to him was this unaccustomed task of dealing with moral\r\ndifficulties, which all through his life before, however lightly they\r\nhad come, he had shirked and avoided! He realized now, that there was\r\nto be no more of that. If he did not wish his life to end in\r\ndisgraceful shipwreck, he must take command and direction of it upon\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\nHe had felt himself capable of stolid endurance since love had\r\ndeclared itself his guide and helper. But now--only to-day--something\r\nbeside had crept into his heart. Not something to be endured, but a\r\nthing to be strangled and thrust away. It was the demon of hate; so\r\nnew, so awful, so loathsome, he doubted that he could look it in the\r\nface and live.\r\n\r\nHere was the problem of his new existence.\r\n\r\nThe woman who had formerly made his life colorless and empty he had\r\nquietly turned his back upon, carrying with him a pity that was not\r\nuntender. But the woman who had unwittingly robbed him of all\r\npossibility of earthly happiness--he hated her. The woman who for the\r\nremainder of a life-time was to be in all the world the nearest thing\r\nto him, he hated her. He hated this woman of whom he must be careful,\r\nto whom he must be tender, and loyal and generous. And to give no sign\r\nor word but of kindness; to do no action that was not considerate, was\r\nthe task which destiny had thrust upon his honor.\r\n\r\nHe did not ask himself if it were possible of accomplishment. He had\r\nflung hesitancy away, to make room for the all-powerful "Must be."\r\n\r\nHe walked slowly back to his home. There was no need to run now;\r\nnothing pursued him. Should he quicken his pace or drag himself ever\r\nso slowly, it could henceforth make no difference. The burden from\r\nwhich he had fled was now banded upon him and not to be loosed, unless\r\nhe fling himself with it into forgetfulness.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXII\r\n\r\nSevering Old Ties.\r\n\r\n\r\nReturning from the matin\xe9e, Belle and her friend Lou Dawson, before\r\nentering their house, crossed over to Fanny\'s. Mrs. Worthington tried\r\nthe door and finding it fastened, rang the bell, then commenced to\r\nbeat a tattoo upon the pane with her knuckles; an ingenuous manner\r\nwhich she had of announcing her identity. Fanny opened to them\r\nherself, and the three walked into the parlor.\r\n\r\n"I haven\'t seen you for a coon\'s age, Fanny," commenced Belle, "where\r\non earth have you been keeping yourself?"\r\n\r\n"You saw me yesterday breakfast time, when you came to borrow the\r\nwrapper pattern," returned Fanny, in serious resentment to her\r\nfriend\'s exaggeration.\r\n\r\n"And much good the old wrapper pattern did me: a mile too small every\r\nway, no matter how much I let out the seams. But see here--"\r\n\r\n"Belle\'s the biggest idiot about her size: there\'s no convincing her\r\nshe\'s not a sylph."\r\n\r\n"_Thank_ you, Mrs. Dawson."\r\n\r\n"Well, it\'s a fact. Didn\'t you think Furgeson\'s scales were all wrong\r\nthe other day because you weighed a hundred and eighty pounds?"\r\n\r\n"O that\'s the day I had that heavy rep on."\r\n\r\n"Heavy nothing. We were coming over last night, Fanny, but we had\r\ncompany," continued Mrs. Dawson.\r\n\r\n"Who d\'you have?" asked Fanny mechanically and glad of the respite.\r\n\r\n"Bert Rodney and Mr. Grant. They\'re so anxious to meet you. I\'d \'a\r\nsent over for you, but Belle--"\r\n\r\n"See here, Fanny, what the mischief was Dave Hosmer doing here to-day,\r\nand going down town with you and all that sort o\' thing?"\r\n\r\nFanny flushed uneasily. "Have you seen the evening paper?" she asked.\r\n\r\n"How d\'you want us to see the paper? we just come from the matin\xe9e."\r\n\r\n"David came yesterday," Fanny said working nervously at the window\r\nshade. "He\'d wrote me a note the postman brought right after you left\r\nwith the pattern. When you saw us getting on the car, we were going\r\ndown to Dr. Martin\'s, and we\'ve got married again."\r\n\r\nMrs. Dawson uttered a long, low whistle by way of comment. Mrs.\r\nWorthington gave vent to her usual "Well I\'ll be switched," which she\r\nwas capable of making expressive of every shade of astonishment, from\r\nthe lightest to the most pronounced; at the same time unfastening the\r\nbridle of her bonnet which plainly hindered her free respiration after\r\nsuch a shock.\r\n\r\n"Say that Fanny isn\'t sly, after that, Belle."\r\n\r\n"Sly? My God, she\'s a fool! If ever a woman had a snap! and to go to\r\nwork and let a man get around her like that."\r\n\r\nMrs. Worthington seemed powerless to express herself in anything but\r\ndisconnected exclamations.\r\n\r\n"What are you going to do, Fanny?" asked Lou, who having aired all the\r\nastonishment which she cared to show, in her whistle, was collected\r\nenough to want her natural curiosity satisfied.\r\n\r\n"David\'s living down South. I guess we\'ll go down there pretty soon.\r\nSoon\'s he can get things fixed up here."\r\n\r\n"Where--down South?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, I don\'t know. Somewheres in Louisiana."\r\n\r\n"It\'s to be hoped in New Orleans," spoke Belle didactically, "that\'s\r\nthe only decent place in Louisiana where a person could live."\r\n\r\n"No, \'tain\'t in New Orleans. He\'s got a saw mill somewheres down\r\nthere."\r\n\r\n"Heavens and earth! a saw mill?" shrieked Belle. Lou was looking\r\ncalmly resigned to the startling news.\r\n\r\n"Oh, I ain\'t going to live in a saw mill. I wisht you\'d all let me\r\nalone, any way," she returned pettishly. "There\'s a lady keeps a\r\nplantation, and that\'s where he lives."\r\n\r\n"Well of all the rigmaroles! a lady, and a saw mill and a plantation.\r\nIt\'s my opinion that man could make you believe black\'s white, Fanny\r\nLarimore."\r\n\r\nAs Hosmer approached his house, he felt mechanically in his pocket for\r\nhis latch key; so small a trick having come back to him with the old\r\nhabit of misery. Of course he found no key. His ring startled Fanny,\r\nwho at once sprang from her seat to open the door for him; but having\r\ntaken a few steps, she hesitated and irresolutely re-seated herself.\r\nIt was only his second ring that the servant unamiably condescended to\r\nanswer.\r\n\r\n"So you\'re going to take Fanny away from us, Mr. Hosmer," said Belle,\r\nwhen he had greeted them and seated himself beside Mrs. Dawson on the\r\nsmall sofa that stood between the door and window. Fanny sat at the\r\nadjoining window, and Mrs. Worthington in the center of the room;\r\nwhich was indeed so small a room that any one of them might have\r\nreached out and almost touched the hand of the others.\r\n\r\n"Yes, Fanny has agreed to go South with me," he answered briefly.\r\n"You\'re looking well, Mrs. Worthington."\r\n\r\n"Oh, Law yes, I\'m never sick. As I tell Mr. Worthington, he\'ll never\r\nget rid of me, unless he hires somebody to murder me. But I tell you\r\nwhat, you came pretty near not having any Fanny to take away with you.\r\nShe was the sickest woman! Did you tell him about it, Fanny? Come to\r\nthink of it, I guess the climate down there\'ll be the very thing to\r\nbring her round."\r\n\r\nMrs. Dawson without offering apology interrupted her friend to inquire\r\nof Hosmer if his life in the South were not of the most interesting,\r\nand begging that he detail them something of it; with a look to\r\nindicate that she felt the deepest concern in anything that touched\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nA masculine presence had always the effect of rousing Mrs. Dawson into\r\nan animation which was like the glow of a slumbering ember, when a\r\nstrong pressure of air is brought to bear upon it.\r\n\r\nHosmer had always considered her an amiable woman, with rather\r\ndelicate perceptions; frivolous, but without the vulgarisms of Mrs.\r\nWorthington, and consequently a less objectionable friend for Fanny.\r\nHe answered, looking down into her eyes, which were full of\r\nattentiveness.\r\n\r\n"My life in the South is not one that you would think interesting. I\r\nlive in the country where there are no distractions such as you ladies\r\ncall amusements--and I work pretty hard. But it\'s the sort of life\r\nthat one grows attached to and finds himself longing for again if he\r\nhave occasion to change it."\r\n\r\n"Yes, it must be very satisfying," she answered; for the moment\r\nperfectly sincere.\r\n\r\n"Oh very!" exclaimed Mrs. Worthington, with a loud and aggressive\r\nlaugh. "It would just suit you to a T, Lou, but how it\'s going to\r\nsatisfy Fanny! Well, I\'ve got nothing to say about it, thanks be; it\r\ndon\'t concern me."\r\n\r\n"If Fanny finds that she doesn\'t like it after a fair trial, she has\r\nthe privilege of saying so, and we shall come back again," he said\r\nlooking at his wife whose elevation of eyebrow, and droop of mouth\r\ngave her the expression of martyred resignation, which St. Lawrence\r\nmight have worn, when invited to make himself comfortable on the\r\ngridiron--so had Mrs. Worthington\'s words impressed her with the force\r\nof their prophetic meaning.\r\n\r\nMrs. Dawson politely hoped that Hosmer would not leave before Jack\r\ncame home; it would distress Jack beyond everything to return and find\r\nthat he had missed an old friend whom he thought so much of.\r\n\r\nHosmer could not say precisely when they would leave. He was in\r\npresent negotiation with a person who wanted to rent the house,\r\nfurnished; and just as soon as he could arrange a few business\r\ndetails, and Fanny could gather such belongings as she wished to take\r\nwith her they would go.\r\n\r\n"You seem mighty struck on Dave Hosmer, all of a sudden," remarked\r\nMrs. Worthington to her friend, as the two crossed over the street. "A\r\nfeller without any more feelings than a stick; it\'s what I always said\r\nabout him."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I always did like Hosmer," replied Mrs. Dawson. "But I thought he\r\nhad more sense than to tie himself to that little gump again, after\r\nhe\'d had the luck to get rid of her."\r\n\r\nA few days later Jack came home. His return was made palpable to the\r\nentire neighborhood; for no cab ever announced itself with quite the\r\ndash and clatter and bang of door that Jack\'s cabs did.\r\n\r\nThe driver had staggered behind him under the weight of the huge\r\nyellow valise, and had been liberally paid for the service.\r\n\r\nImmediately the windows were thrown wide open, and the lace curtains\r\ndrawn aside until no smallest vestige of them remained visible from\r\nthe street. A condition of things which Mrs. Worthington upstairs\r\nbitterly resented, and naturally, spoiling as it necessarily did, the\r\ngeneral _coup d\'oeil_ of the flat to passers-by. But Mrs. Dawson had\r\nwon her husband\'s esteem by just such acts as this one of amiable\r\npermission to ventilate the house according to methods of his own and\r\nessentially masculine; regardless of dust that might be flying, or sun\r\nthat might be shining with disastrous results to the parlor carpet.\r\n\r\nClouds of tobacco smoke were seen to issue from the open windows.\r\nThose neighbors whose openings commanded a view of the Dawson\'s\r\nalley-gate might have noted the hired girl starting for the grocery\r\nwith unusual animation of step, and returning with her basket well\r\nstocked with beer and soda bottles--a provision made against a need\r\nfor "dutch-cocktails," likely to assail Jack during his hours of\r\ndomesticity.\r\n\r\nIn the evening the same hired girl, breathless from the multiplicity\r\nof errands which she had accomplished during the day, appeared at the\r\nHosmers with a message that Mrs. Dawson wanted them to "come over."\r\n\r\nThey were preparing to leave on the morrow, but concluded that they\r\ncould spare a few moments in which to bid adieu to their friends.\r\n\r\nJack met them at the very threshold, with warm and hearty\r\nhand-shaking, and loud protest when he learned that they had not come\r\nto spend the evening and that they were going away next day.\r\n\r\n"Great Scott! you\'re not leaving to-morrow? And I ain\'t going to have\r\na chance to get even with Mrs. Hosmer on that last deal? By Jove, she\r\nknows how to do it," he said, addressing Hosmer and holding Fanny\r\nfamiliarly by the elbow. "Drew to the middle, sir, and hang me, if she\r\ndidn\'t fill. Takes a woman to do that sort o\' thing; and me a laying\r\nfor her with three aces. Hello there, girls! here\'s Hosmer and Fanny,"\r\nin response to which summons his wife and Mrs. Worthington issued from\r\nthe depths of the dining-room, where they had been engaged in\r\npreparing certain refreshments for the expected guests.\r\n\r\n"See here, Lou, we\'ll have to fix it up some way to go and see them\r\noff to-morrow. If you\'d manage to lay over till Thursday I could join\r\nyou as far as Little Rock. But no, that\'s a fact," he added\r\nreflectively, "I\'ve got to be in Cincinnati on Thursday."\r\n\r\nThey had all entered the parlor, and Mrs. Worthington suggested that\r\nHosmer go up and make a visit to her husband, whom he would find up\r\nthere "poring over those everlasting books."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know what\'s got into Mr. Worthington lately," she said, "he\'s\r\ngetting that religious. If it ain\'t the Bible he\'s poring over, well\r\nit\'s something or other just as bad."\r\n\r\nThe brightly burning light guided Hosmer to the kitchen, where he\r\nfound Lorenzo Worthington seated beside his student lamp at the table,\r\nwhich was covered with a neat red cloth. On the gas-stove was spread a\r\nsimilar cloth and the floor was covered with a shining oil-cloth.\r\n\r\nMr. Worthington was startled, having already forgotten that his wife\r\nhad told him of Hosmer\'s return to St. Louis.\r\n\r\n"Why, Mr. Hosmer, is this you? come, come into the parlor, this is no\r\nplace," shaking Hosmer\'s hand and motioning towards the parlor.\r\n\r\n"No, it\'s very nice and cozy here, and I have only a moment to stay,"\r\nsaid Hosmer, seating himself beside the table on which the other had\r\nlaid his book, with his spectacles between the pages to mark his\r\nplace. Mr. Worthington then did a little hemming and hawing\r\npreparatory to saying something fitting the occasion; not wishing to\r\nbe hasty in offering the old established form of congratulation, in a\r\ncase whose peculiarity afforded him no precedential guide. Hosmer came\r\nto his relief by observing quite naturally that he and his wife had\r\ncome over to say good-bye, before leaving for the South, adding "no\r\ndoubt Mrs. Worthington has told you."\r\n\r\n"Yes, yes, and I\'m sure we\'re very sorry to lose you; that is, Mrs.\r\nLarimore--I should say Mrs. Hosmer. Isabella will certainly regret her\r\ndeparture, I see them always together, you know."\r\n\r\n"You cling to your old habit, I see, Mr. Worthington," said Hosmer,\r\nindicating his meaning by a motion of the hand towards the book on the\r\ntable.\r\n\r\n"Yes, to a certain extent. Always within the forced limits, you\r\nunderstand. At this moment I am much interested in tracing the history\r\nof various religions which are known to us; those which have died out,\r\nas well as existing religions. It is curious, indeed, to note the\r\ncircumstances of their birth, their progress and inevitable death;\r\nseeming to follow the course of nations in such respect. And the\r\nsimilitude which stamps them all, is also a feature worthy of study.\r\nYou would perhaps be surprised, sir, to discover the points of\r\nresemblance which indicate in them a common origin. To observe the\r\nslight differences, indeed technical differences, distinguishing the\r\nIslam from the Hebrew, or both from the Christian religion. The creeds\r\nare obviously ramifications from the one deep-rooted trunk which we\r\ncall religion. Have you ever thought of this, Mr. Hosmer?"\r\n\r\n"No, I admit that I\'ve not gone into it. Homeyer would have me think\r\nthat all religions are but mythological creations invented to satisfy\r\na species of sentimentality--a morbid craving in man for the unknown\r\nand undemonstrable."\r\n\r\n"That is where he is wrong; where I must be permitted to differ from\r\nhim. As you would find, my dear sir, by following carefully the\r\nhistory of mankind, that the religious sentiment is implanted, a true\r\nand legitimate attribute of the human soul--with peremptory right to\r\nits existence. Whatever may be faulty in the creeds--that makes no\r\ndifference, the foundation is there and not to be dislodged. Homeyer,\r\nas I understand him from your former not infrequent references, is an\r\nIconoclast, who would tear down and leave devastation behind him;\r\nbuilding up nothing. He would deprive a clinging humanity of the\r\nsupports about which she twines herself, and leave her helpless and\r\nsprawling upon the earth."\r\n\r\n"No, no, he believes in a natural adjustment," interrupted Hosmer. "In\r\nan innate reserve force of accommodation. What we commonly call laws\r\nin nature, he styles accidents--in society, only arbitrary methods of\r\nexpediency, which, when they outlive their usefulness to an advancing\r\nand exacting civilization, should be set aside. He is a little\r\nimpatient to always wait for the inevitable natural adjustment."\r\n\r\n"Ah, my dear Mr. Hosmer, the world is certainly to-day not prepared to\r\nstand the lopping off and wrenching away of old traditions. She must\r\ntake her stand against such enemies of the conventional. Take religion\r\naway from the life of man--"\r\n\r\n"Well, I knew it--I was just as sure as preaching," burst out Mrs.\r\nWorthington, as she threw open the door and confronted the two\r\nmen--resplendent in "baby blue" and much steel ornamentation. "As I\r\ntell Mr. Worthington, he ought to turn Christian Brother or something\r\nand be done with it."\r\n\r\n"No, no, my dear; Mr. Hosmer and I have merely been interchanging a\r\nfew disjointed ideas."\r\n\r\n"I\'ll be bound they were disjointed. I guess Fanny wants you, Mr.\r\nHosmer. If you listen to Mr. Worthington he\'ll keep you here till\r\ndaylight with his ideas."\r\n\r\nHosmer followed Mrs. Worthington down-stairs and into Mrs. Dawson\'s.\r\nAs he entered the parlor he heard Fanny laughing gaily, and saw that\r\nshe stood near the sideboard in the dining-room, just clicking her\r\nglass of punch to Jack Dawson\'s, who was making a gay speech on the\r\noccasion of her new marriage.\r\n\r\nThey did not leave when they had intended. Need the misery of that one\r\nday be told?\r\n\r\nBut on the evening of the following day, Fanny peered with pale,\r\nhaggard face from the closed window of the Pullman car as it moved\r\nslowly out of Union dep\xf4t, to see Lou and Jack Dawson smiling and\r\nwaving good-bye, Belle wiping her eyes and Mr. Worthington looking\r\nblankly along the line of windows, unable to see them without his\r\nspectacles, which he had left between the pages of his Schopenhauer on\r\nthe kitchen table at home.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPART II\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\nFanny\'s First Night at Place-du-Bois.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe journey South had not been without attractions for Fanny. She had\r\nthat consciousness so pleasing to the feminine mind of being well\r\ndressed; for her husband had been exceedingly liberal in furnishing\r\nher the means to satisfy her fancy in that regard. Moreover the change\r\nholding out a promise of novelty, irritated her to a feeble\r\nexpectancy. The air, that came to her in puffs through the car window,\r\nwas deliciously soft and mild; steeped with the rich languor of the\r\nIndian summer, that had already touched the tree tops, the sloping\r\nhill-side, and the very air, with russet and gold.\r\n\r\nHosmer sat beside her, curiously inattentive to his newspaper;\r\nobservant of her small needs, and anticipating her timid half\r\nexpressed wishes. Was there some mysterious power that had so soon\r\ntaught the man such methods to a woman\'s heart, or was he not rather\r\non guard and schooling himself for the r\xf4le which was to be acted out\r\nto the end? But as the day was approaching its close, Fanny became\r\ntired and languid; a certain mistrust was creeping into her heart with\r\nthe nearing darkness. It had grown sultry and close, and the view from\r\nthe car window was no longer cheerful, as they whirled through\r\nforests, gloomy with trailing moss, or sped over an unfamiliar country\r\nwhose features were strange and held no promise of a welcome for her.\r\n\r\nThey were nearing Place-du-Bois, and Hosmer\'s spirits had risen almost\r\nto the point of gaiety as he began to recognize the faces of those who\r\nloitered about the stations at which they stopped. At the Centerville\r\nstation, five miles before reaching their own, he had even gone out on\r\nthe platform to shake hands with the rather mystified agent who had\r\nnot known of his absence. And he had waved a salute to the little\r\nFrench priest of Centerville who stood out in the open beside his\r\nhorse, booted, spurred and all equipped for bad weather, waiting for\r\ncertain consignments which were to come with the train, and who\r\nanswered Hosmer\'s greeting with a sober and uncompromising sweep of\r\nthe hand. When the whistle sounded for Place-du-Bois, it was nearly\r\ndark. Hosmer hurried Fanny on to the platform, where stood Henry, his\r\nclerk. There were a great many negroes loitering about, some of whom\r\noffered him a cordial "how\'dy Mr. Hosma," and pushing through was\r\nGr\xe9goire, meeting them with the ease of a courtier, and acknowledging\r\nHosmer\'s introduction of his wife, with a friendly hand shake.\r\n\r\n"Aunt Th\xe9r\xe8se sent the buggy down fur you," he said, "we had rain this\r\nmornin\' and the road\'s putty heavy. Come this way. Mine out fur that\r\nba\'el, Mrs. Hosma, it\'s got molasses in. Hiurm bring that buggy ova\r\nyere."\r\n\r\n"What\'s the news, Gr\xe9goire?" asked Hosmer, as they waited for Hiram to\r\nturn the horses about.\r\n\r\n"Jus\' about the same\'s ev\'a. Miss Melicent wasn\'t ver\' well a few days\r\nback; but she\'s some betta. I reckon you\'re all plum wore out," he\r\nadded, taking in Fanny\'s listless attitude, and thinking her very\r\npretty as far as he could discover in the dim light.\r\n\r\nThey drove directly to the cottage, and on the porch Th\xe9r\xe8se was\r\nwaiting for them. She took Fanny\'s two hands and pressed them warmly\r\nbetween her own; then led her into the house with an arm passed about\r\nher waist. She shook hands with Hosmer, and stood for a while in\r\ncheerful conversation, before leaving them.\r\n\r\nThe cottage was fully equipped for their reception, with Minervy in\r\npossession of the kitchen and the formerly reluctant Suze as\r\nhousemaid: though Th\xe9r\xe8se had been silent as to the methods which she\r\nhad employed to prevail with these unwilling damsels.\r\n\r\nHosmer then went out to look after their baggage, and when he\r\nreturned, Fanny sat with her head pillowed on the sofa, sobbing\r\nbitterly. He knelt beside her, putting his arm around her, and asked\r\nthe cause of her distress.\r\n\r\n"Oh it\'s so lonesome, and dreadful, I don\'t believe I can stand it,"\r\nshe answered haltingly through her tears.\r\n\r\nAnd here was he thinking it was so home-like and comforting, and\r\ntasting the first joy that he had known since he had gone away.\r\n\r\n"It\'s all strange and new to you, Fanny; try to bear up for a day or\r\ntwo. Come now, don\'t be a baby--take courage. It will all seem quite\r\ndifferent by and by, when the sun shines."\r\n\r\nA knock at the door was followed by the entrance of a young colored\r\nboy carrying an armful of wood.\r\n\r\n"Miss T\'r\xe8se sont me kin\'le fiar fu\' Miss Hosma; \'low he tu\'nin\'\r\ncole," he said depositing his load on the hearth; and Fanny, drying\r\nher eyes, turned to watch him at his work.\r\n\r\nHe went very deliberately about it, tearing off thin slathers from the\r\nfat pine, and arranging them into a light frame-work, beneath a\r\ntopping of kindling and logs that he placed on the massive brass\r\nandirons. He crawled about on hands and knees, picking up the stray\r\nbits of chips and moss that had fallen from his arms when he came in.\r\nThen sitting back on his heels he looked meditatively into the blaze\r\nwhich he had kindled and scratched his nose with a splinter of pine\r\nwood. When Hosmer presently left the room, he rolled his big black\r\neyes towards Fanny, without turning his head, and remarked in a tone\r\nplainly inviting conversation "yo\' all come f\'om way yonda?"\r\n\r\nHe was intensely black, and if Fanny had been a woman with the\r\nslightest sense of humor, she could not but have been amused at the\r\npicture which he presented in the revealing fire-light with his elfish\r\nand ape like body much too small to fill out the tattered and\r\nill-fitting garments that hung about it. But she only wondered at him\r\nand his rags, and at his motive for addressing her.\r\n\r\n"We\'re come from St. Louis," she replied, taking him with a\r\nseriousness which in no wise daunted him.\r\n\r\n"Yo\' all brung de rain," he went on sociably, leaving off the\r\nscratching of his nose, to pass his black yellow-palmed hand slowly\r\nthrough the now raging fire, a feat which filled her with\r\nconsternation. After prevailing upon him to desist from this\r\nsalamander like exhibition, she was moved to ask if he were not very\r\npoor to be thus shabbily clad.\r\n\r\n"No \'um," he laughed, "I got some sto\' close yonda home. Dis yere coat\r\nw\'at Mista Gr\xe9gor gi\'me," looking critically down at its length, which\r\nswept the floor as he remained on his knees. "He done all to\'e tu\r\npieces time he gi\' him tu me, whar he scuffle wid Jo\xe7int yonda tu de\r\nmill. Mammy \'low she gwine mek him de same like new w\'en she kin kotch\r\nde time."\r\n\r\nThe entrance of Minervy bearing a tray temptingly arranged with a\r\ndainty supper, served to silence the boy, who at seeing her, threw\r\nhimself upon all fours and appeared to be busy with the fire. The\r\nwoman, a big raw-boned field hand, set her burden awkwardly down on a\r\ntable, and after staring comprehensively around, addressed the boy in\r\na low rich voice, "Dar ain\'t no mo\' call to bodda wid dat fiar, you\r\nSampson; how come Miss T\'r\xe8se sont you lazy piece in yere tu buil\'\r\nfiar?"\r\n\r\n"Don\' know how come," he replied, vanishing with an air of the utmost\r\nself-depreciation.\r\n\r\nHosmer and Fanny took tea together before the cheerful fire and he\r\ntold her something of methods on the plantation, and made her further\r\nacquainted with the various people whom she had thus far encountered.\r\nShe listened apathetically; taking little interest in what he said,\r\nand asking few questions. She did express a little bewilderment at the\r\nservant problem. Mrs. Lafirme, during their short conversation, had\r\ndeplored her inability to procure more than two servants for her; and\r\nFanny could not understand why it should require so many to do the\r\nwork which at home was accomplished by one. But she was tired--very\r\ntired, and early sought her bed, and Hosmer went in quest of his\r\nsister whom he had not yet seen.\r\n\r\nMelicent had been told of his marriage some days previously, and had\r\nbeen thrown into such a state of nerves by the intelligence, as to\r\nseriously alarm those who surrounded her and whose experience with\r\nhysterical girls had been inadequate.\r\n\r\nPoor Gr\xe9goire had betaken himself with the speed of the wind to the\r\nstore to procure bromide, valerian, and whatever else should be\r\nthought available in prevailing with a malady of this distressing\r\nnature. But she was "some betta," as he told Hosmer, who found her\r\nwalking in the darkness of one of the long verandas, all enveloped in\r\nfilmy white wool. He was a little prepared for a cool reception from\r\nher, and ten minutes before she might have received him with a studied\r\nindifference. But her mood had veered about and touched the point\r\nwhich moved her to fall upon his neck, and in a manner, condole with\r\nhim; seasoning her sympathy with a few tears.\r\n\r\n"Whatever possessed you, David? I have been thinking, and thinking,\r\nand I can see no reason which should have driven you to do this thing.\r\nOf course I can\'t meet her; you surely don\'t expect it?"\r\n\r\nHe took her arm and joined her in her slow walk.\r\n\r\n"Yes, I do expect it, Melicent, and if you have the least regard for\r\nme, I expect more. I want you to be good to her, and patient, and show\r\nyourself her friend. No one can do such things more amiably than you,\r\nwhen you try."\r\n\r\n"But David, I had hoped for something so different."\r\n\r\n"You couldn\'t have expected me to marry Mrs. Lafirme, a Catholic," he\r\nsaid, making no pretense of misunderstanding her.\r\n\r\n"I think that woman would have given up religion--anything for you."\r\n\r\n"Then you don\'t know her, little sister."\r\n\r\nIt must have been far in the night when Fanny awoke suddenly. She\r\ncould not have told whether she had been awakened by the long, wailing\r\ncry of a traveler across the narrow river, vainly trying to rouse the\r\nferryman; or the creaking of a heavy wagon that labored slowly by in\r\nthe road and moved Hector to noisy enquiry. Was it not rather the\r\npattering rain that the wind was driving against the window panes? The\r\nlamp burned dimly upon the high old-fashioned mantel-piece and her\r\nhusband had thoughtfully placed an improvised screen before it, to\r\nprotect her against its disturbance. He himself was not beside her,\r\nnor was he in the room. She slid from her bed and moved softly on her\r\nbare feet over to the open sitting-room door.\r\n\r\nThe fire had all burned away. Only the embers lay in a glowing heap,\r\nand while she looked, the last stick that lay across the andirons,\r\nbroke through its tapering center and fell amongst them, stirring a\r\nfitful light by which she discovered her husband seated and bowed like\r\na man who has been stricken. Uncomprehending, she stood a moment\r\nspeechless, then crept back noiselessly to bed.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\n"Neva to See You!"\r\n\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se judged it best to leave Fanny a good deal to herself during\r\nher first days on the plantation, without relinquishing a certain\r\nwatchful supervision of her comfort, and looking in on her for a few\r\nmoments each day. The rain which had come with them continued fitfully\r\nand Fanny remained in doors, clad in a warm handsome gown, her small\r\nslippered feet cushioned before the fire, and reading the latest novel\r\nof one of those prolific female writers who turn out their unwholesome\r\nintellectual sweets so tirelessly, to be devoured by the girls and\r\nwomen of the age.\r\n\r\nMelicent, who always did the unexpected, crossed over early on the\r\nmorning after Fanny\'s arrival; penetrated to her sleeping room and\r\nembraced her effusively, even as she lay in bed, calling her "poor\r\ndear Fanny" and cautioning her against getting up on such a morning.\r\n\r\nThe tears which had come to Fanny on arriving, and which had dried on\r\nher cheek when she turned to gaze into the cheer of the great wood\r\nfire, did not return. Everybody seemed to be making much of her, which\r\nwas a new experience in her life; she having always felt herself as of\r\nlittle consequence, and in a manner, overlooked. The negroes were\r\noverawed at the splendor of her toilettes and showed a respect for her\r\nin proportion to the money value which these toilettes reflected. Each\r\nmorning Gr\xe9goire left at her door his compliments with a huge bouquet\r\nof brilliant and many colored crysanthemums, and enquiry if he could\r\nserve her in any way. And Hosmer\'s time, that was not given to work,\r\nwas passed at her side; not in brooding or pre-occupied silence, but\r\nin talk that invited her to friendly response.\r\n\r\nWith Th\xe9r\xe8se, she was at first shy and diffident, and over watchful of\r\nherself. She did not forget that Hosmer had told her "The lady knows\r\nwhy I have come" and she resented that knowledge which Th\xe9r\xe8se\r\npossessed of her past intimate married life.\r\n\r\nMelicent\'s attentions did not last in their ultra-effusiveness, but\r\nshe found Fanny less objectionable since removed from her St. Louis\r\nsurroundings; and the evident consideration with which she had been\r\naccepted at Place-du-Bois seemed to throw about her a halo of\r\nsufficient distinction to impel the girl to view her from a new and\r\ndifferent stand-point.\r\n\r\nBut the charm of plantation life was letting go its hold upon\r\nMelicent. Gr\xe9goire\'s adoration alone, and her feeble response to it\r\nwere all that kept her.\r\n\r\n"I neva felt anything like this befo\'," he said, as they stood\r\ntogether and their hands touched in reaching for a splendid rose that\r\nhung invitingly from its tall latticed support out in mid lawn. The\r\nsun had come again and dried the last drop of lingering moisture on\r\ngrass and shrubbery.\r\n\r\n"W\'en I\'m away f\'om you, even fur five minutes, \'t seems like I mus\'\r\nhurry quick, quick, to git back again; an\' w\'en I\'m with you,\r\neverything \'pears all right, even if you don\'t talk to me or look at\r\nme. Th\' otha day, down at the gin," he continued, "I was figurin\' on\r\nsome weights an\' wasn\'t thinkin\' about you at all, an\' all at once I\r\nremember\'d the one time I\'d kissed you. Goodness! I couldn\'t see the\r\nfigures any mo\', my head swum and the pencil mos\' fell out o\' my han\'.\r\nI neva felt anything like it: hones\', Miss Melicent, I thought I was\r\ngoin\' to faint fur a minute."\r\n\r\n"That\'s very unwise, Gr\xe9goire," she said, taking the roses that he\r\nhanded her to add to the already large bunch. "You must learn to think\r\nof me calmly: our love must be something like a sacred memory--a sweet\r\nrecollection to help us through life when we are apart."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know how I\'m goin\' to stan\' it. Neva to see you! neva--my\r\nGod!" he gasped, paling and crushing between his nervous fingers the\r\nflower she would have taken from him.\r\n\r\n"There is nothing in this world that one cannot grow accustomed to,\r\ndear," spoke the pretty philosopher, picking up her skirts daintily\r\nwith one hand and passing the other through his arm--the hand which\r\nheld the flowers, whose peculiar perfume ever afterwards made Gr\xe9goire\r\nshiver through a moment of pain that touched very close upon rapture.\r\n\r\nHe was more occupied than he liked during those busy days of\r\nharvesting and ginning, that left him only brief and snatched\r\nintervals of Melicent\'s society. If he could have rested in the\r\ncomfort of being sure of her, such moments of separation would have\r\nhad their compensation in reflective anticipation. But with his\r\nundisciplined desires and hot-blooded eagerness, her half-hearted\r\nacknowledgments and inadequate concessions, closed her about with a\r\nchilling barrier that staggered him with its problematic nature.\r\nFeeling himself her equal in the aristocracy of blood, and her master\r\nin the knowledge and strength of loving, he resented those half\r\nunderstood reasons which removed him from the possibility of being\r\nanything to her. And more, he was angry with himself for acquiescing\r\nin that self understood agreement. But it was only in her absence that\r\nthese thoughts disturbed him. When he was with her, his whole being\r\nrejoiced in her existence and there was no room for doubt or dread.\r\n\r\nHe felt himself regenerated through love, and as having no part in\r\nthat other Gr\xe9goire whom he only thought of to dismiss with\r\nunrecognition.\r\n\r\nThe time came when he could ill conceal his passion from others.\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se became conscious of it, through an unguarded glance. The\r\nunhappiness of the situation was plain to her; but to what degree she\r\ncould not guess. It was certainly so deplorable that it would have\r\nbeen worth while to have averted it. Yet, she felt great faith in the\r\npower of time and absence to heal such wounds even to the extent of\r\nleaving no tell-tale scar.\r\n\r\n"Gr\xe9goire, my boy," she said to him, speaking in French, and laying\r\nher hand on his, when they were alone together. "I hope that your\r\nheart is not too deep in this folly."\r\n\r\nHe reddened and asked, "What do you mean, aunt?"\r\n\r\n"I mean, that unfortunately, you are in love with Melicent. I do not\r\nknow how much longer she will remain here, but taking any possibility\r\nfor granted, let me advise you to leave the place for a while; go back\r\nto your home, or take a little trip to the city."\r\n\r\n"No, I could not."\r\n\r\n"Force yourself to it."\r\n\r\n"And lose days, perhaps weeks, of being near her? No, no, I could not\r\ndo that, aunt. There will be plenty time for that in the rest of my\r\nlife," he said, trying to speak calmly and forcing his voice to a\r\nharshness which the nearness of tears made needful.\r\n\r\n"Does she know? Have you told her?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, she knows how much I love her."\r\n\r\n"And she does not love you," said Th\xe9r\xe8se, seeming rather to assert\r\nthan to question.\r\n\r\n"No, she does not. No matter what she says--she does not. I can feel\r\nthat here," he answered, striking his breast. "Oh aunt, it is terrible\r\nto think of her going away; forever, perhaps; of never seeing her. I\r\ncould not stand it." And he stood the strain no longer, but sobbed and\r\nwept with his aunt\'s consoling arms around him.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se, knowing that Melicent would not tarry much longer with them,\r\nthought it not needful to approach her on the subject. Had it been\r\notherwise, she would not have hesitated to beg the girl to desist from\r\nthis unprofitable amusement of tormenting a human heart.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\nA Talk Under the Cedar Tree.\r\n\r\n\r\nDay by day, Fanny threw off somewhat of the homesickness which had\r\nweighted her at coming. Not by any determined effort of the will, nor\r\nby any resolve to make the best of things. Outside influences meeting\r\nhalf-way the workings of unconscious inward forces, were the agents\r\nthat by degrees were gently ridding her of the acute pressure of\r\ndissatisfaction, which up to the present, she had stolidly borne\r\nwithout any personal effort to cast it off.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se affected her forcibly. This woman so wholesome, so fair and\r\nstrong; so un-American as to be not ashamed to show tenderness and\r\nsympathy with eye and lip, moved Fanny like a new and pleasing\r\nexperience. When Th\xe9r\xe8se touched her caressingly, or gently stroked\r\nher limp hand, she started guiltily, and looked furtively around to\r\nmake sure that none had witnessed an exhibition of tenderness that\r\nmade her flush, and the first time found her unresponsive. A second\r\ntime, she awkwardly returned the hand pressure, and later, these\r\nmildly sensuous exchanges prefaced the outpouring of all Fanny\'s woes,\r\ngreat and small.\r\n\r\n"I don\'t say that I always done what was right, Mrs. Laferm, but I\r\nguess David\'s told you just what suited him about me. You got to\r\nremember there\'s always two sides to a story."\r\n\r\nShe had been to the poultry yard with Th\xe9r\xe8se, who had introduced her\r\nto its feathery tenants, making her acquainted with stately Brahmas\r\nand sleek Plymouth-Rocks and hardy little "Creole chickens"--not much\r\nto look at, but very palatable when converted into _fricass\xe9e_.\r\n\r\nReturning, they seated themselves on the bench that encircled a\r\nmassive cedar--spreading and conical. Hector, who had trotted\r\nattendance upon them during their visit of inspection, cast himself\r\nheavily down at his mistress\' feet and after glancing knowingly up\r\ninto her face, looked placidly forth at Sampson, gathering garden\r\ngreens on the other side of a low dividing fence.\r\n\r\n"You see if David\'d always been like he is now, I don\'t know but\r\nthings\'d been different. Do you suppose he ever went any wheres with\r\nme, or even so much as talked to me when he came home? There was\r\nalways that everlasting newspaper in his pocket, and he\'d haul it out\r\nthe first thing. Then I used to read the paper too sometimes, and when\r\nI\'d go to talk to him about what I read, he\'d never even looked at the\r\nsame things. Goodness knows what he read in the paper, I never could\r\nfind out; but here\'d be the edges all covered over with figuring. I\r\nbelieve it\'s the only thing he ever thought or dreamt about; that\r\neternal figuring on every bit of paper he could lay hold of, till I\r\nwas tired picking them up all over the house. Belle Worthington used\r\nto say it\'d of took an angel to stand him. I mean his throwing papers\r\naround that way. For as far as his never talking went, she couldn\'t\r\nfind any fault with that; Mr. Worthington was just as bad, if he\r\nwasn\'t worse. But Belle\'s not like me; I don\'t believe she\'d let poor\r\nMr. Worthington talk in the house if he wanted to."\r\n\r\nShe gradually drifted away from her starting point, and like most\r\npeople who have usually little to say, became very voluble, when once\r\nshe passed into the humor of talking. Th\xe9r\xe8se let her talk unchecked.\r\nIt seemed to do her good to chatter about Belle and Lou, and Jack\r\nDawson, and about her home life, of which she unknowingly made such a\r\npitiable picture to her listener.\r\n\r\n"I guess David never let on to you about himself," she said moodily,\r\nhaving come back to the sore that rankled: the dread that Th\xe9r\xe8se had\r\nlaid all the blame of the rupture on her shoulders.\r\n\r\n"You\'re mistaken, Mrs. Hosmer.
