It was a knowledge of his own\r\nshort-comings that prompted your husband to go back and ask your\r\nforgiveness. You must grant, there\'s nothing in his conduct now that\r\nyou could reproach him with. And," she added, laying her hand gently\r\non Fanny\'s arm, "I know you\'ll be strong, and do your share in this\r\nreconciliation--do what you can to please him."\r\n\r\nFanny flushed uneasily under Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s appealing glance.\r\n\r\n"I\'m willing to do anything that David wants," she replied, "I made up\r\nmy mind to that from the start. He\'s a mighty good husband now, Mrs.\r\nLaferm. Don\'t mind what I said about him. I was afraid you thought\r\nthat--"\r\n\r\n"Never mind," returned Th\xe9r\xe8se kindly, "I know all about it. Don\'t\r\nworry any farther over what I may think. I believe in you and in him,\r\nand I know you\'ll both be brave and do what\'s right."\r\n\r\n"There isn\'t anything so very hard for David to do," she said,\r\ndepressed with a sense of her inadequate strength to do the task which\r\nshe had set herself. "He\'s got no faults to give up. David never did\r\nhave any faults. He\'s a true, honest man; and I was a coward to say\r\nthose things about him."\r\n\r\nMelicent and Gr\xe9goire were coming across the lawn to join the two, and\r\nFanny, seeing them approach, suddenly chilled and wrapt herself about\r\nin her mantle of reserve.\r\n\r\n"I guess I better go," she said, offering to rise, but Th\xe9r\xe8se held\r\nout a detaining hand.\r\n\r\n"You don\'t want to go and sit alone in the cottage; stay here with me\r\ntill Mr. Hosmer comes back from the mill."\r\n\r\nGr\xe9goire\'s face was a study. Melicent, who did what she wanted with\r\nhim, had chosen this afternoon, for some inscrutable reason, to make\r\nhim happy. He carried her shawl and parasol; she herself bearing a\r\nveritable armful of flowers, leaves, red berried sprigs, a tangle of\r\nrichest color. They had been in the woods and she had bedecked him\r\nwith garlands and festoons of autumn leaves, till he looked a very\r\nSatyr; a character which his flushed, swarthy cheeks, and glittering\r\nanimal eyes did not belie.\r\n\r\nThey were laughing immoderately, and their whole bearing still\r\nreflected their exuberant gaiety as they joined Th\xe9r\xe8se and Fanny.\r\n\r\n"What a \'Mater Dolorosa\' Fanny looks!" exclaimed Melicent, throwing\r\nherself into a picturesque attitude on the bench beside Th\xe9r\xe8se, and\r\nresting her feet on Hector\'s broad back.\r\n\r\nFanny offered no reply, but to look helplessly resigned; an expression\r\nwhich Melicent knew of old, and which had always the effect of\r\nirritating her. Not now, however, for the curve of the bench around\r\nthe great cedar tree removed her from the possibility of contemplating\r\nFanny\'s doleful visage, unless she made an effort to that end, which\r\nshe was certainly not inclined to do.\r\n\r\n"No, Gr\xe9goire," she said, flinging a rose into his face when he would\r\nhave seated himself beside her, "go sit by Fanny and do something to\r\nmake her laugh; only don\'t tickle her; David mightn\'t like it. And\r\nhere\'s Mrs. Lafirme looking almost as glum. Now, if David would only\r\njoin us with that \'pale cast of thought\' that he bears about usually,\r\nwhat a merry go round we\'d have."\r\n\r\n"When Melicent looks at the world laughing, she wants it to laugh back\r\nat her," said Th\xe9r\xe8se, reflecting something of the girl\'s gaiety.\r\n\r\n"As in a looking-glass, well isn\'t that square?" she returned, falling\r\ninto slang, in her recklessness of spirit.\r\n\r\nEndeavoring to guard her treasure of flowers from Th\xe9r\xe8se, who was\r\nwithout ceremony making a critical selection among them of what\r\npleased her, Melicent slid around the bench, bringing herself close to\r\nGr\xe9goire and begging his protection against the Vandalism of his aunt.\r\nShe looked into his eyes for an instant as though asking him for love\r\ninstead of so slight a favor and he grasped her arm, pressing it till\r\nshe cried out from the pain: which act, on his side, served to drive\r\nher again around to Th\xe9r\xe8se.\r\n\r\n"Guess what we are going to do to-morrow: you and I and all of us;\r\nGr\xe9goire and David and Fanny and everybody?"\r\n\r\n"Going to Bedlam along with you?" Th\xe9r\xe8se asked.\r\n\r\n"Mrs. Lafirme is in need of a rebuke, which I shall proceed to\r\nadminister," thrusting a crumpled handful of rose leaves down the neck\r\nof Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s dress, and laughing joyously in her scuffle to accomplish\r\nthe punishment.\r\n\r\n"No, madam; I don\'t go to Bedlam; I drive others there. Ask Gr\xe9goire\r\nwhat we\'re going to do. Tell them, Gr\xe9goire."\r\n\r\n"They ain\'t much to tell. We\'a goin\' hoss back ridin\'."\r\n\r\n"Not me; I can\'t ride," wailed Fanny.\r\n\r\n"You can get up Torpedo for Mrs. Hosmer, can\'t you, Gr\xe9goire?" asked\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se.\r\n\r\n"Certainly. W\'y you could ride ole Torpedo, Mrs. Hosma, if you nova\r\nsaw a hoss in yo\' life. A li\'l chile could manage him."\r\n\r\nFanny turned to Th\xe9r\xe8se for further assurance and found all that she\r\nlooked for.\r\n\r\n"We\'ll go up on the hill and see that dear old Morico, and I shall\r\ntake along a comb, and comb out that exquisite white hair of his and\r\nthen I shall focus him, seated in his low chair and making one of\r\nthose cute turkey fans."\r\n\r\n"Ole Morico ain\'t goin\' to let you try no monkeyshines on him; I tell\r\nyou that befo\' han\'," said Gr\xe9goire, rising and coming to Melicent to\r\nrid him of his sylvan ornamentations, for it was time for him to leave\r\nthem. When he turned away, Melicent rose and flung all her flowery\r\nwealth into Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s lap, and following took his arm.\r\n\r\n"Where are you going?" asked Th\xe9r\xe8se.\r\n\r\n"Going to help Gr\xe9goire feed the mules," she called back looking over\r\nher shoulder; the sinking sun lighting her handsome mischievous face.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se proceeded to arrange the flowers with some regard to graceful\r\nsymmetry; and Fanny did not regain her talkative spirit that\r\nMelicent\'s coming had put to flight, but sat looking silent and\r\nlistlessly into the distance.\r\n\r\nAs Th\xe9r\xe8se glanced casually up into her face she saw it warmed by a\r\nsudden faint glow--an unusual animation, and following her gaze, she\r\nsaw that Hosmer had returned and was entering the cottage.\r\n\r\n"I guess I better be going," said Fanny rising, and this time Th\xe9r\xe8se\r\nno longer detained her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIV\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se Crosses the River.\r\n\r\n\r\nTo shirk any serious duties of life would have been entirely foreign\r\nto Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s methods or even instincts. But there did come to her\r\nmoments of rebellion--or repulsion, against the small demands that\r\npresented themselves with an unfailing recurrence; and from such, she\r\nat times indulged herself with the privilege of running away. When\r\nFanny left her alone--a pathetic little droop took possession of the\r\ncorners of her mouth that might not have come there if she had not\r\nbeen alone. She laid the flowers, only half arranged, on the bench\r\nbeside her, as a child would put aside a toy that no longer interested\r\nit. She looked towards the house and could see the servants going back\r\nand forth. She knew if she entered, she would be met by appeals from\r\none and the other. The overseer would soon be along, with his crib\r\nkeys, and stable keys; his account of the day\'s doings and\r\nconsultations for to-morrow\'s work, and for the moment, she would have\r\nnone of it.\r\n\r\n"Come, Hector--come, old boy," she said rising abruptly; and crossing\r\nthe lawn she soon gained the gravel path that led to the outer road.\r\nThis road brought her by a mild descent to the river bank. The water,\r\nseldom stationary for any long period, was at present running low and\r\nsluggishly between its red banks.\r\n\r\nTied to the landing was a huge flat-boat, that was managed by the aid\r\nof a stout cable reaching quite across the river; and beside it\r\nnestled a small light skiff. In this Th\xe9r\xe8se seated herself, and\r\nproceeded to row across the stream, Hector plunging into the water and\r\nswimming in advance of her.\r\n\r\nThe banks on the opposite shore were almost perpendicular; and their\r\nsummit to be reached only by the artificial road that had been cut\r\ninto them: broad and of easy ascent. This river front was a standing\r\nworry to Th\xe9r\xe8se, for when the water was high and rapid, the banks\r\ncaved constantly, carrying away great sections from the land. Almost\r\nevery year, the fences in places had to be moved back, not only for\r\nsecurity, but to allow a margin for the road that on this side\r\nfollowed the course of the small river.\r\n\r\nHigh up and perilously near the edge, stood a small cabin. It had once\r\nbeen far removed from the river, which had now, however, eaten its way\r\nclose up to it--leaving no space for the road-way. The house was\r\nsomewhat more pretentious than others of its class, being fashioned of\r\nplaned painted boards, and having a brick chimney that stood fully\r\nexposed at one end. A great rose tree climbed and spread generously\r\nover one side, and the big red roses grew by hundreds amid the dark\r\ngreen setting of their leaves.\r\n\r\nAt the gate of this cabin Th\xe9r\xe8se stopped, calling out, "_Grosse\r\ntante!--oh, Grosse tante!_"\r\n\r\nThe sound of her voice brought to the door a negress--coal black and\r\nso enormously fat that she moved about with evident difficulty. She\r\nwas dressed in a loosely hanging purple calico garment of the mother\r\nHubbard type--known as a _volante_ amongst Louisiana Creoles; and on\r\nher head was knotted and fantastically twisted a bright _tignon_. Her\r\nglistening good-natured countenance illumined at the sight of Th\xe9r\xe8se.\r\n\r\n"_Quo faire to pas woulez rentrer, Tite ma\xeetresse?_" and Th\xe9r\xe8se\r\nanswered in the same Creole dialect: "Not now, _Grosse tante_--I shall\r\nbe back in half an hour to drink a cup of coffee with you." No English\r\nwords can convey the soft music of that speech, seemingly made for\r\ntenderness and endearment.\r\n\r\nAs Th\xe9r\xe8se turned away from the gate, the black woman re-entered the\r\nhouse, and as briskly as her cumbersome size would permit, began\r\npreparations for her mistress\' visit. Milk and butter were taken from\r\nthe safe; eggs, from the India rush basket that hung against the wall;\r\nand flour, from the half barrel that stood in convenient readiness in\r\nthe corner: for _Tite ma\xeetresse_ was to be treated to a dish of\r\n_croquignoles_. Coffee was always an accomplished fact at hand in the\r\nchimney corner.\r\n\r\n_Grosse tante_, or more properly, Marie Louise, was a Creole--Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s\r\nnurse and attendant from infancy, and the only one of the family\r\nservants who had come with her mistress from New Orleans to\r\nPlace-du-Bois at that lady\'s marriage with J\xe9r\xf4me Lafirme. But her\r\never increasing weight had long since removed her from the possibility\r\nof usefulness, otherwise than in supervising her small farm yard. She\r\nhad little use for "_ces n\xe9ges Am\xe9ricains_," as she called the\r\nplantation hands--a restless lot forever shifting about and changing\r\nquarters.\r\n\r\nIt was seldom now that she crossed the river; only two occasions being\r\nconsidered of sufficient importance to induce her to such effort. One\r\nwas in the event of her mistress\' illness, when she would install\r\nherself at her bedside as a fixture, not to be dislodged by any less\r\ninducement than Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s full recovery. The other was when a dinner\r\nof importance was to be given: then Marie Louise consented to act as\r\n_chef de cuisine_, for there was no more famous cook than she in the\r\nState; her instructor having been no less a personage than old Lucien\r\nSantien--a _gourmet_ famed for his ultra Parisian tastes.\r\n\r\nSeated at the base of a great China-berry on whose gnarled protruding\r\nroots she rested an arm languidly, Th\xe9r\xe8se looked out over the river\r\nand gave herself up to doubts and misgivings. She first took exception\r\nwith herself for that constant interference in the concerns of other\r\npeople. Might not this propensity be carried too far at times? Did the\r\ngood accruing counterbalance the personal discomfort into which she\r\nwas often driven by her own agency? What reason had she to know that a\r\npolicy of non-interference in the affairs of others might not after\r\nall be the judicious one? As much as she tried to vaguely generalize,\r\nshe found her reasoning applying itself to her relation with Hosmer.\r\n\r\nThe look which she had surprised in Fanny\'s face had been a painful\r\nrevelation to her. Yet could she have expected other, and should she\r\nhave hoped for less, than that Fanny should love her husband and he in\r\nturn should come to love his wife?\r\n\r\nHad she married Hosmer herself! Here she smiled to think of the storm\r\nof indignation that such a marriage would have roused in the parish.\r\nYet, even facing the impossibility of such contingency, it pleased her\r\nto indulge in a short dream of what might have been.\r\n\r\nIf it were her right instead of another\'s to watch for his coming and\r\nrejoice at it! Hers to call him husband and lavish on him the love\r\nthat awoke so strongly when she permitted herself, as she was doing\r\nnow, to invoke it! She felt what capability lay within her of rousing\r\nthe man to new interests in life. She pictured the dawn of an\r\nunsuspected happiness coming to him: broadening; illuminating; growing\r\nin him to answer to her own big-heartedness.\r\n\r\nWere Fanny, and her own prejudices, worth the sacrifice which she and\r\nHosmer had made? This was the doubt that bade fair to unsettle her;\r\nthat called for a sharp, strong out-putting of the will before she\r\ncould bring herself to face the situation without its accessions of\r\npersonalities. Such communing with herself could not be condemned as a\r\nweakness with Th\xe9r\xe8se, for the effect which it left upon her strong\r\nnature was one of added courage and determination.\r\n\r\nWhen she reached Marie Louise\'s cabin again, twilight, which is so\r\nbrief in the South, was giving place to the night.\r\n\r\nWithin the cabin, the lamp had already been lighted, and Marie Louise\r\nwas growing restless at Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s long delay.\r\n\r\n"Ah _Grosse tante_, I\'m so tired," she said, falling into a chair near\r\nthe door; not relishing the warmth of the room after her quick walk,\r\nand wishing to delay as long as possible the necessity of sitting at\r\ntable. At another time she might have found the dish of golden brown\r\n_croquignoles_ very tempting with its accessory of fragrant coffee;\r\nbut not to-day.\r\n\r\n"Why do you run about so much, _Tite ma\xeetresse_? You are always going\r\nthis way and that way; on horseback, on foot--through the house. Make\r\nthose lazy niggers work more. You spoil them. I tell you if it was old\r\nmistress that had to deal with them, they would see something\r\ndifferent."\r\n\r\nShe had taken all the pins from Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s hair which fell in a\r\ngleaming, heavy mass; and with her big soft hands she was stroking her\r\nhead as gently as if those hands had been of the whitest and most\r\ndelicate.\r\n\r\n"I know that look in your eyes, it means headache. It\'s time for me to\r\nmake you some more _eau s\xe9dative_--I am sure you haven\'t any more;\r\nyou\'ve given it away as you give away every thing."\r\n\r\n"_Grosse tante_," said Th\xe9r\xe8se seated at table and sipping her coffee;\r\n_Grosse tante_ also drinking her cup--but seated apart, "I am going to\r\ninsist on having your cabin moved back; it is silly to be so stubborn\r\nabout such a small matter. Some day you will find yourself out in the\r\nmiddle of the river--and what am I going to do then?--no one to nurse\r\nme when I am sick--no one to scold me--nobody to love me."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t say that, _Tite ma\xeetresse_, all the world loves you--it isn\'t\r\nonly Marie Louise. But no. You must remember the last time poor\r\nMonsieur J\xe9r\xf4me moved me, and said with a laugh that I can never\r\nforget, \'well, _Grosse tante_, I know we have got you far enough this\r\ntime out of danger,\' away back in Dumont\'s field you recollect? I said\r\nthen, Marie Louise will move no more; she\'s too old. If the good God\r\ndoes not want to take care of me, then it\'s time for me to go."\r\n\r\n"Ah but, _Grosse tante_, remember--God does not want all the trouble\r\non his own shoulders," Th\xe9r\xe8se answered humoring the woman, in her\r\nconception of the Deity. "He wants us to do our share, too."\r\n\r\n"Well, I have done my share. Nothing is going to harm Marie Louise. I\r\nthought about all that, do not fret. So the last time P\xe8re Antoine\r\npassed in the road--going down to see that poor Pierre Pardou at the\r\nMouth--I called him in, and he blessed the whole house inside and out,\r\nwith holy water--notice how the roses have bloomed since then--and\r\ngave me medals of the holy Virgin to hang about. Look over the door,\r\n_Tite ma\xeetresse_, how it shines, like a silver star."\r\n\r\n"If you will not have your cabin removed, _Grosse tante_, then come\r\nlive with me. Old Hatton has wanted work at Place-du-Bois, the longest\r\ntime. We will have him build you a room wherever you choose, a pretty\r\nlittle house like those in the city."\r\n\r\n"_Non--non, Tite ma\xeetresse, Marie Louise \'pr\xe8 cr\xe9ver icite av\xe9 tous\r\nson butin, si faut_" (no, no, _Tite ma\xeetresse_, Marie Louise will die\r\nhere with all her belongings if it must be).\r\n\r\nThe servants were instructed that when their mistress was not at home\r\nat a given hour, her absence should cause no delay in the household\r\narrangements. She did not choose that her humor or her movements be\r\nhampered by a necessity of regularity which she owed to no one. When\r\nshe reached home supper had long been over.\r\n\r\nNearing the house she heard the scraping of Nathan\'s violin, the noise\r\nof shuffling feet and unconstrained laughter. These festive sounds\r\ncame from the back veranda. She entered the dining-room, and from its\r\nobscurity looked out on a curious scene. The veranda was lighted by a\r\nlamp suspended from one of its pillars. In a corner sat Nathan;\r\nserious, dignified, scraping out a monotonous but rhythmic minor\r\nstrain to which two young negroes from the lower quarters--famous\r\ndancers--were keeping time in marvelous shuffling and pigeon-wings;\r\ntwisting their supple joints into astonishing contortions and the\r\nsweat rolling from their black visages. A crowd of darkies stood at a\r\nrespectful distance an appreciative and encouraging audience. And\r\nseated on the broad rail of the veranda were Melicent and Gr\xe9goire,\r\npatting Juba and singing a loud accompaniment to the breakdown.\r\n\r\nWas this the Gr\xe9goire who had only yesterday wept such bitter tears on\r\nhis aunt\'s bosom?\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se turning away from the scene, the doubt assailed her whether it\r\nwere after all worth while to strive against the sorrows of life that\r\ncan be so readily put aside.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\nOne Afternoon.\r\n\r\n\r\nWhatever may have been Torpedo\'s characteristics in days gone by, at\r\nthis advanced period in his history he possessed none so striking as a\r\nstoical inaptitude for being moved. Another of his distinguishing\r\ntraits was a propensity for grazing which he was prone to indulge at\r\ninopportune moments. Such points taken in conjunction with a gait\r\nclosely resembling that of the camel in the desert, might give much\r\ncause to wonder at Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s motive in recommending him as a suitable\r\nmount for the unfortunate Fanny, were it not for his wide-spread\r\nreputation of angelic inoffensiveness.\r\n\r\nThe ride which Melicent had arranged and in which she held out such\r\npromises of a "lark" proved after all but a desultory affair. For with\r\nFanny making but a sorry equestrian debut and Hosmer creeping along at\r\nher side; Th\xe9r\xe8se unable to hold Beauregard within conventional\r\nlimits, and Melicent and Gr\xe9goire vanishing utterly from the scene,\r\nsociability was a feature entirely lacking to the excursion.\r\n\r\n"David, I can\'t go another step: I just can\'t, so that settles it."\r\n\r\nThe look of unhappiness in Fanny\'s face and attitude, would have moved\r\nthe proverbial stone.\r\n\r\n"I think if you change horses with me, Fanny, you\'ll find it more\r\ncomfortable, and we\'ll turn about and go home."\r\n\r\n"I wouldn\'t get on that horse\'s back, David Hosmer, if I had to die\r\nright here in the woods, I wouldn\'t."\r\n\r\n"Do you think you could manage to walk back that distance then? I can\r\nlead the horses," he suggested as a _pis aller_.\r\n\r\n"I guess I\'ll haf to; but goodness knows if I\'ll ever get there\r\nalive."\r\n\r\nThey were far up on the hill, which spot they had reached by painfully\r\nslow and labored stages, each refraining from mention of a discomfort\r\nthat might interfere with the supposed enjoyment of the other, till\r\nFanny\'s note of protest.\r\n\r\nHosmer cast about him for some expedient that might lighten the\r\nunpleasantness of the situation, when a happy thought occurred to him.\r\n\r\n"If you\'ll try to bear up, a few yards further, you can dismount at\r\nold Morico\'s cabin and I\'ll hurry back and get the buggy. It can be\r\ndriven this far anyway: and it\'s only a short walk from here through\r\nthe woods."\r\n\r\nSo Hosmer set her down before Morico\'s door: her long riding skirt,\r\nborrowed for the occasion, twisting awkwardly around her legs, and\r\nevery joint in her body aching.\r\n\r\nPartly by pantomimic signs interwoven with a few French words which he\r\nhad picked up within the last year, Hosmer succeeded in making himself\r\nunderstood to the old man, and rode away leaving Fanny in his care.\r\n\r\nMorico fussily preceded her into the house and placed a great clumsy\r\nhome-made rocker at her disposal, into which she cast herself with\r\nevery appearance of bodily distress. He then busied himself in tidying\r\nup the room out of deference to his guest; gathering up the scissors,\r\nwaxen thread and turkey feathers which had fallen from his lap in his\r\ndisturbance, and laying them on the table. He knocked the ashes from\r\nhis corn-cob pipe which he now rested on a projection of the brick\r\nchimney that extended into the room and that served as mantel-piece.\r\nAll the while he cast snatched glances at Fanny, who sat pale and\r\ntired. Her appearance seemed to move him to make an effort towards\r\nrelieving it. He took a key from his pocket and unlocking a side of\r\nthe _garde manger_, drew forth a small flask of whisky. Fanny had\r\nclosed her eyes and was not aware of his action, till she heard him at\r\nher elbow saying in his feeble quavering voice:--\r\n\r\n"_Tenez madame; goutez un peu: \xe7a va vous faire du bien,_" and opening\r\nher eyes she saw that he held a glass half filled with strong "toddy"\r\nfor her acceptance.\r\n\r\nShe thrust out her hand to ward it away as though it had been a\r\nreptile that menaced her with its sting.\r\n\r\nMorico looked nonplussed and a little abashed: but he had much faith\r\nin the healing qualities of his remedy and urged it on her anew. She\r\ntrembled a little, and looked away with rather excited eyes.\r\n\r\n"_Je vous assure madame, \xe7a ne peut pas vous faire du mal._"\r\n\r\nFanny took the glass from his hand, and rising went and placed it on\r\nthe table, then walked to the open door and looked eagerly out, as\r\nthough hoping for the impossibility of her husband\'s return.\r\n\r\nShe did not seat herself again, but walked restlessly about the room,\r\nintently examining its meager details. The circuit of inspection\r\nbringing her again to the table, she picked up Morico\'s turkey fan,\r\nlooking at it long and critically. When she laid it down, it was to\r\nseize the glass of "toddy" which she unhesitatingly put to her lips\r\nand drained at a draught. All uneasiness and fatigue seemed to leave\r\nher on the instant as though by magic. She went back to her chair and\r\nreseated herself composedly. Her eyes now rested on her old host with\r\na certain quizzical curiosity strange to them.\r\n\r\nHe was plainly demoralized by her presence, and still made pretense of\r\noccupying himself with the arrangement of the room.\r\n\r\nPresently she said to him: "Your remedy did me more good than I\'d\r\nexpected," but not understanding her, he only smiled and looked at her\r\nblankly.\r\n\r\nShe laughed good-humoredly back at him, then went to the table and\r\npoured from the flask which he had left standing there, liquor to the\r\ndepth of two fingers, this time drinking it more deliberately. After\r\nthat she tried to talk to Morico and thought it very amusing that he\r\ncould not understand her.\r\n\r\nPresently Jo\xe7int came home and accepted her presence there very\r\nindifferently. He went to the _garde manger_ to stay his hunger, much\r\nas he had done on the occasion of Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s visit; talked in grum\r\nabrupt utterances to his father, and disappeared into the adjoining\r\nroom where Fanny could hear him and occasionally see him polishing and\r\noiling his cherished rifle.\r\n\r\nMorico, more accustomed to foreign sounds in the woods than she, was\r\nthe first to detect the approach of Gr\xe9goire, whom he went out\r\nhurriedly to meet, glad of the relief from the supposed necessity of\r\nentertaining his puzzling visitor. When he was fairly out of the room,\r\nshe arose quickly, approached the table and reaching for the flask of\r\nliquor, thrust it hastily into her pocket, then went to join him. At\r\nthe moment that Gr\xe9goire came up, Jo\xe7int issued from a side door and\r\nstood looking at the group.\r\n\r\n"Well, Mrs. Hosma, yere I am. I reckon you was tired waitin\'. The\r\nbuggy\'s yonda in the road."\r\n\r\nHe shook hands cordially with Morico saying something to him in French\r\nwhich made the old man laugh heartily.\r\n\r\n"Why didn\'t David come? I thought he said he was coming; that\'s the\r\nway he does," said Fanny complainingly.\r\n\r\n"That\'s a po\' compliment to me, Mrs. Hosrma. Can\'t you stan\' my\r\ncompany for that li\'le distance?" returned Gr\xe9goire gallantly. "Mr.\r\nHosma had a good deal to do w\'en he got back, that\'s w\'y he sent me.\r\nAn\' we betta hurry up if we expec\' to git any suppa\' to-night. Like as\r\nnot you\'ll fine your kitchen cleaned out."\r\n\r\nFanny looked her inquiry for his meaning.\r\n\r\n"Why, don\'t you know this is \'Tous-saint\' eve--w\'en the dead git out\r\no\' their graves an\' walk about? You wouldn\'t ketch a nigga out o\' his\r\ncabin to-night afta dark to save his soul. They all gittin\' ready now\r\nto hustle back to the quartas."\r\n\r\n"That\'s nonsense," said Fanny, drawing on her gloves, "you ought to\r\nhave more sense than to repeat such things."\r\n\r\nGr\xe9goire laughed, looking surprised at her unusual energy of speech\r\nand manner. Then he turned to Jo\xe7int, whose presence he had thus far\r\nignored, and asked in a peremptory tone:\r\n\r\n"W\'at did Woodson say \'bout watchin\' at the mill to-night? Did you ask\r\nhim like I tole you?"\r\n\r\n"Yaas, me ax um: ee\' low ee an\' goin\'. Say how Sylveste d\'wan\' watch\r\nlak alluz. Say ee an\' goin\'. Me don\' blem \'im neida, don\' ketch me\r\nout de \'ouse night lak dat fu no man."\r\n\r\n"_Sacr\xe9 imb\xe9cile_," muttered Gr\xe9goire, between his teeth, and\r\nvouchsafed him no other answer, but nodded to Morico and turned away.\r\nFanny followed with a freedom of movement quite unlike that of her\r\ncoming.\r\n\r\nMorico went into the house and coming back hastily to the door called\r\nto Jo\xe7int:\r\n\r\n"Bring back that flask of whisky that you took off the table."\r\n\r\n"You\'re a liar: you know I have no use for whisky. That\'s one of your\r\ndamned tricks to make me buy you more." And he seated himself on an\r\nover-turned tub and with his small black eyes half closed, looked\r\nmoodily out into the solemn darkening woods. The old man showed no\r\nresentment at the harshness and disrespect of his son\'s speech, being\r\nevidently used to such. He passed his hand slowly over his white long\r\nhair and turned bewildered into the house.\r\n\r\n"Is it just this same old thing year in and year out, Gr\xe9goire? Don\'t\r\nany one ever get up a dance, or a card party or anything?"\r\n\r\n"Jus\' as you say; the same old thing f\'om one yea\'s en\' to the otha. I\r\nused to think it was putty lonesome myse\'f w\'en I firs\' come yere.\r\nThen you see they\'s no neighbo\'s right roun\' yere. In Natchitoches\r\nnow; that\'s the place to have a right down good time. But see yere; I\r\ndidn\' know you was fon\' o\' dancin\' an\' such things."\r\n\r\n"Why, of course, I just dearly love to dance. But it\'s as much as my\r\nlife\'s worth to say that before David; he\'s such a stick; but I guess\r\nyou know that by this time," with a laugh, as he had never heard from\r\nher before--so unconstrained; at the same time drawing nearer to him\r\nand looking merrily into his face.\r\n\r\n"The little lady\'s been having a \'toddy\' at Morico\'s, that makes her\r\nlively," thought Gr\xe9goire. But the knowledge did not abash him in the\r\nleast. He accommodated himself at once to the situation with that\r\nadaptability common to the American youth, whether of the South,\r\nNorth, East or West.\r\n\r\n"Where abouts did you leave David when you come away?" she asked with\r\na studied indifference.\r\n\r\n"Hol\' on there, Buckskin--w\'ere you takin\' us? W\'y, I lef\' him at the\r\nsto\' mailin\' lettas."\r\n\r\n"Had the others all got back? Mrs. Laferm? Melicent? did they all stop\r\nat the store, too?"\r\n\r\n"Who? Aunt Thr\xe9r\xe8se? no, she was up at the house w\'en I lef\'--I reckon\r\nMiss Melicent was there too. Talkin\' \'bout fun,--it\'s to git into one\r\no\' them big spring wagons on a moonlight night, like they do in\r\nCentaville sometimes; jus\' packed down with young folks--and start out\r\nfur a dance up the coast. They ain\'t nothin\' to beat it as fah as fun\r\ngoes."\r\n\r\n"It must be just jolly. I guess you\'re a pretty good dancer,\r\nGr\xe9goire?"\r\n\r\n"Well--\'taint fur me to say. But they ain\'t many can out dance me: not\r\nin Natchitoches pa\'ish, anyway. I can say that much."\r\n\r\nIf such a thing could have been, Fanny would have startled Gr\xe9goire\r\nmore than once during the drive home. Before its close she had\r\nobtained a promise from him to take her up to Natchitoches for the\r\nvery next entertainment,--averring that she didn\'t care what David\r\nsaid. If he wanted to bury himself that was his own look out. And if\r\nMrs. Laferm took people to be angels that they could live in a place\r\nlike that, and give up everything and not have any kind of enjoyment\r\nout of life, why, she was mistaken and that\'s all there was to it. To\r\nall of which freely expressed views Gr\xe9goire emphatically assented.\r\n\r\nHosmer had very soon disembarrassed himself of Torpedo, knowing that\r\nthe animal would unerringly find his way to the corn crib by supper\r\ntime. He continued his own way now untrammelled, and at an agreeable\r\nspeed which soon brought him to the spring at the road side. Here he\r\nfound Th\xe9r\xe8se, half seated against a projection of rock, in her hand a\r\nbunch of ferns which she had evidently dismounted to gather, and\r\nholding Beauregard\'s bridle while he munched at the cool wet tufts of\r\ngrass that grew everywhere.\r\n\r\nAs Hosmer rode up at a rapid pace, he swung himself from his horse\r\nalmost before the animal came to a full stop. He removed his hat,\r\nmopped his forehead, stamped about a little to relax his limbs and\r\nturned to answer the enquiry with which Th\xe9r\xe8se met him.\r\n\r\n"Left her at Morico\'s. I\'ll have to send the buggy back for her."\r\n\r\n"I can\'t forgive myself for such a blunder," said Th\xe9r\xe8se regretfully,\r\n"indeed I had no idea of that miserable beast\'s character. I never was\r\non him you know--only the little darkies, and they never complained:\r\nthey\'d as well ride cows as not."\r\n\r\n"Oh, it\'s mainly from her being unaccustomed to riding, I believe."\r\n\r\nThis was the first time that Hosmer and Th\xe9r\xe8se had met alone since\r\nhis return from St. Louis. They looked at each other with full\r\nconsciousness of what lay in the other\'s mind. Th\xe9r\xe8se felt that\r\nhowever adroitly another woman might have managed the situation, for\r\nherself, it would have been a piece of affectation to completely\r\nignore it at this moment.\r\n\r\n"Mr. Hosmer, perhaps I ought to have said something before this, to\r\nyou--about what you\'ve done."\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes, congratulated me--complimented me," he replied with a\r\npretense at a laugh.\r\n\r\n"Well, the latter, perhaps. I think we all like to have our good and\r\nright actions recognized for their worth."\r\n\r\nHe flushed, looked at her with a smile, then laughed out-right--this\r\ntime it was no pretense.\r\n\r\n"So I\'ve been a good boy; have done as my mistress bade me and now I\'m\r\nto receive a condescending little pat on the head--and of course must\r\nsay thank you. Do you know, Mrs. Lafirme--and I don\'t see why a woman\r\nlike you oughtn\'t to know it--it\'s one of those things to drive a man\r\nmad, the sweet complaisance with which women accept situations, or\r\ninflict situations that it takes the utmost of a man\'s strength to\r\nendure."\r\n\r\n"Well, Mr. Hosmer," said Th\xe9r\xe8se plainly discomposed, "you must\r\nconcede you decided it was the right thing to do."\r\n\r\n"I didn\'t do it because I thought it was right, but because you\r\nthought it was right. But that makes no difference."\r\n\r\n"Then remember your wife is going to do the right thing herself--she\r\nadmitted as much to me."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t you fool yourself, as Melicent says, about what Mrs. Hosmer\r\nmeans to do. I take no account of it. But you take it so easily; so as\r\na matter of course. That\'s what exasperates me. That you, you, you,\r\nshouldn\'t have a suspicion of the torture of it; the loathsomeness of\r\nit. But how could you--how could any woman understand it? Oh forgive\r\nme, Th\xe9r\xe8se--I wouldn\'t want you to. There\'s no brute so brutal as a\r\nman," he cried, seeing the pain in her face and knowing he had caused\r\nit. "But you know you promised to help me--oh I\'m talking like an\r\nidiot."\r\n\r\n"And I do," returned Th\xe9r\xe8se, "that is, I want to, I mean to."\r\n\r\n"Then don\'t tell me again that I have done right. Only look at me\r\nsometimes a little differently than you do at Hiram or the gate post.\r\nLet me once in a while see a look in your face that tells me that you\r\nunderstand--if it\'s only a little bit."\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se thought it best to interrupt the situation; so, pale and\r\nsilently she prepared to mount her horse. He came to her assistance of\r\ncourse, and when she was seated she drew off her loose riding glove\r\nand held out her hand to him. He pressed it gratefully, then touched\r\nit with his lips; then turned it and kissed the half open palm.\r\n\r\nShe did not leave him this time, but rode at his side in silence with\r\na frown and little line of thought between her blue eyes.\r\n\r\nAs they were nearing the store she said diffidently: "Mr. Hosmer, I\r\nwonder if it wouldn\'t be best for you to put the mill in some one\r\nelse\'s charge--and go away from Place-du-Bois."\r\n\r\n"I believe you always speak with a purpose, Mrs. Lafirme: you have\r\nsomebody\'s ultimate good in view, when you say that. Is it your own,\r\nor mine or whose is it?"\r\n\r\n"Oh! not mine."\r\n\r\n"I will leave Place-du-Bois, certainly, if you wish it."\r\n\r\nAs she looked at him she was forced to admit that she had never seen\r\nhim look as he did now. His face, usually serious, had a whole\r\nunwritten tragedy in it. And she felt altogether sore and puzzled and\r\nexasperated over man\'s problematic nature.\r\n\r\n"I don\'t think it should be left entirely to me to say. Doesn\'t your\r\nown reason suggest a proper course in the matter?"\r\n\r\n"My reason is utterly unable to determine anything in which you are\r\nconcerned. Mrs. Lafirme," he said checking his horse and laying a\r\nrestraining hand on her bridle, "let me speak to you one moment. I\r\nknow you are a woman to whom one may speak the truth. Of course, you\r\nremember that you prevailed upon me to go back to my wife. To you it\r\nseemed the right thing--to me it seemed certainly hard--but no more\r\nnor less than taking up the old unhappy routine of life, where I had\r\nleft it when I quitted her. I reasoned much like a stupid child who\r\nthinks the colors in his kaleidoscope may fall twice into the same\r\ndesign. In place of the old, I found an entirely new situation--horrid,\r\nsickening, requiring such a strain upon my energies to live through\r\nit, that I believe it\'s an absurdity to waste so much moral force for\r\nso poor an aim--there would be more dignity in putting an end to my\r\nlife. It doesn\'t make it any the more bearable to feel that the cause\r\nof this unlooked for change lies within myself--my altered feelings.\r\nBut it seems to me that I have the right to ask you not to take\r\nyourself out of my life; your moral support; your bodily atmosphere. I\r\nhope not to give way to the weakness of speaking of these things\r\nagain: but before you leave me, tell me, do you understand a little\r\nbetter why I need you?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, I understand now; and I thank you for talking so openly to me.\r\nDon\'t go away from Place-du-Bois: it would make me very wretched."\r\n\r\nShe said no more and he was glad of it, for her last words held almost\r\nthe force of action for him; as though she had let him feel for an\r\ninstant her heart beat against his own with an echoing pain.\r\n\r\nTheir ways now diverged. She went in the direction of the house and he\r\nto the store where he found Gr\xe9goire, whom he sent for his wife.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\nOne Night.\r\n\r\n\r\n"Gr\xe9goire was right: do you know those nasty creatures have gone and\r\nleft every speck of the supper dishes unwashed? I\'ve got half a mind\r\nto give them both warning to-morrow morning."\r\n\r\nFanny had come in from the kitchen to the sitting-room, and the above\r\nhomily was addressed to her husband who stood lighting his cigar. He\r\nhad lately taken to smoking.\r\n\r\n"You\'d better do nothing of the kind; you wouldn\'t find it easy to\r\nreplace them. Put up a little with their vagaries: this sort of thing\r\nonly happens once a year."\r\n\r\n"How do you know it won\'t be something else just as ridiculous\r\nto-morrow? And that idiot of a Minervy; what do you suppose she told\r\nme when I insisted on her staying to wash up things? She says, last\r\nwhatever you call it, her husband wanted to act hard-headed and staid\r\nout after dark, and when he was crossing the bayou, the spirits jerked\r\nhim off his horse and dragged him up and down in the water, till he\r\nwas nearly drowned. I don\'t see what you\'re laughing at; I guess you\'d\r\nlike to make out that they\'re in the right."\r\n\r\nHosmer was perfectly aware that Fanny had had a drink, and he rightly\r\nguessed that Morico had given it to her. But he was at a loss to\r\naccount for the increasing symptoms of intoxication that she showed.\r\nHe tried to persuade her to go to bed; but his efforts to that end\r\nremained unheeded, till she had eased her mind of an accumulation of\r\ngrievances, mostly fancied. He had much difficulty in preventing her\r\nfrom going over to give Melicent a piece of her mind about her lofty\r\nairs and arrogance in thinking herself better than other people. And\r\nshe was very eager to tell Th\xe9r\xe8se that she meant to do as she liked,\r\nand would stand no poking of noses in her business. It was a good\r\nwhile before she fell into a heavy sleep, after shedding a few maudlin\r\ntears over the conviction that he intended to leave her again, and\r\nclinging to his neck with beseeching enquiry whether he loved her.\r\n\r\nHe went out on the veranda feeling much as if he had been wrestling\r\nwith a strong adversary who had mastered him, and whom he was glad to\r\nbe freed of, even at the cost of coming inglorious from the conflict.\r\nThe night was so dark, so hushed, that if ever the dead had wished to\r\nstep from their graves and take a stroll above ground, they could not\r\nhave found a more fitting hour. Hosmer walked very long in the\r\nsoothing quiet. He would have liked to walk the night through. The\r\nlast three hours had been like an acute physical pain, that was over\r\nfor the moment, and that being over, left his mind free to return to\r\nthe delicious consciousness, that he had needed to be reminded of,\r\nthat Th\xe9r\xe8se loved him after all. When his measured tread upon the\r\nveranda finally ceased to mark the passing hours, a quiet that was\r\nalmost pulseless fell upon the plantation. Place-du-Bois slept.\r\nPerhaps the only night in the year that some or other of the negroes\r\ndid not lurk in fence corners, or make exchange of nocturnal visits.\r\n\r\nBut out in the hills there was no such unearthly stillness reigning.\r\nThose restless wood-dwellers, that never sleep, were sending startling\r\ngruesome calls to each other. Bats were flapping and whirling and\r\ndarting hither and thither; the gliding serpent making quick rustle\r\namid the dry, crisp leaves, and over all sounded the murmur of the\r\ngreat pine trees, telling their mystic secrets to the night.\r\n\r\nA human creature was there too, feeling a close fellowship with these\r\nspirits of night and darkness; with no more fear in his heart than the\r\nunheeded serpent crossing his path. Every inch of the ground he knew.\r\nHe wanted no daylight to guide him. Had his eyes been blinded he would\r\nno doubt have bent his body close to earth and scented his way along\r\nlike the human hound that he was. Over his shoulder hung the polished\r\nrifle that sent dull and sudden gleamings into the dark. A large tin\r\npail swung from his hand. He was very careful of this pail--or its\r\ncontents, for he feared to lose a drop. And when he accidentally\r\nstruck an intervening tree and spilled some upon the ground, he\r\nmuttered a curse against his own awkwardness.\r\n\r\nTwice since leaving his cabin up in the clearing, he had turned to\r\ndrive back his yellow skulking dog that followed him. Each time the\r\nbrute had fled in abject terror, only to come creeping again into his\r\nmaster\'s footsteps, when he thought himself forgotten. Here was a\r\ncompanion whom neither Jo\xe7int nor his mission required. Exasperated,\r\nhe seated himself on a fallen tree and whistled softly. The dog, who\r\nhad been holding back, dashed to his side, trembling with eagerness,\r\nand striving to twist his head around to lick the hand that patted\r\nhim. Jo\xe7int\'s other hand glided quickly into his pocket, from which he\r\ndrew forth a coil of thin rope that he flung deftly over the animal\'s\r\nhead, drawing it close and tight about the homely, shaggy throat. So\r\nquickly was the action done, that no sound was uttered, and Jo\xe7int\r\ncontinued his way untroubled by his old and faithful friend, whom he\r\nleft hanging to the limb of a tree.\r\n\r\nHe was following the same path that he traversed daily to and from the\r\nmill, and which soon brought him out into the level with its soft\r\ntufted grass and clumps of squat thorn trees. There was no longer the\r\nprotecting wood to screen him; but of such there was no need, for the\r\ndarkness hung about him like the magic mantle of story. Nearing the\r\nmill he grew cautious, creeping along with the tread of a stealthy\r\nbeast, and halting at intervals to listen for sounds that he wished\r\nnot to hear. He knew there was no one on guard tonight. A movement in\r\nthe bushes near by, made him fall quick and sprawling to earth. It was\r\nonly Gr\xe9goire\'s horse munching the soft grass. Jo\xe7int drew near and\r\nlaid his hand on the horse\'s back. It was hot and reeking with sweat.\r\nHere was a fact to make him more wary. Horses were not found in such\r\ncondition from quietly grazing of a cool autumn night. He seated\r\nhimself upon the ground, with his hands clasped about his knees, all\r\ndoubled up in a little heap, and waited there with the patience of the\r\nsavage, letting an hour go by, whilst he made no movement.\r\n\r\nThe hour past, he stole towards the mill, and began his work of\r\nsprinkling the contents of his pail here and there along the dry\r\ntimbers at well calculated distances, with care that no drop should be\r\nlost. Then, he drew together a great heap of crisp shavings and\r\nslathers, plentifully besprinkling it with what remained in the can.\r\nWhen he had struck a match against his rough trousers and placed it\r\ncarefully in the midst of this small pyramid, he found that he had\r\ndone his work but too surely. The quick flame sprang into life,\r\nseizing at once all it could reach. Leaping over intervals; effacing\r\nthe darkness that had shrouded him; seeming to mock him as a fool and\r\npoint him out as a target for heaven and earth to hurl destruction at\r\nif they would. Where should he hide himself? He only thought now of\r\nhow he might have done the deed differently, and with safety to\r\nhimself. He stood with great beams and loose planks surrounding him;\r\nquaking with a premonition of evil. He wanted to fly in one direction;\r\nthen thought it best to follow the opposite; but a force outside of\r\nhimself seemed to hold him fast to one spot. When turning suddenly\r\nabout, he knew it was too late, he felt that all was lost, for there\r\nwas Gr\xe9goire, not twenty paces away--covering him with the muzzle of a\r\npistol and--cursed luck--his own rifle along with the empty pail in\r\nthe raging fire.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se was passing a restless night. She had lain long awake,\r\ndwelling on the insistent thoughts that the day\'s happenings had given\r\nrise to. The sleep which finally came to her was troubled by\r\ndreams--demoniac--grotesque. Hosmer was in a danger from which she was\r\nstriving with physical effort to rescue him, and when she dragged him\r\npainfully from the peril that menaced him, she turned to see that it\r\nwas Fanny whom she had saved--laughing at her derisively, and Hosmer\r\nhad been left to perish. The dream was agonizing; like an appalling\r\nnightmare. She awoke in a fever of distress, and raised herself in bed\r\nto shake off the unnatural impression which such a dream can leave.\r\nThe curtains were drawn aside from the window that faced her bed, and\r\nlooking out she saw a long tongue of flame, reaching far up into the\r\nsky--away over the tree tops and the whole Southern horizon a glow.\r\nShe knew at once that the mill was burning, and it was the affair of a\r\nmoment with her to spring from her bed and don slippers and wrapper.\r\nShe knocked on Melicent\'s door to acquaint her with the startling\r\nnews; then hurried out into the back yard and rang the plantation\r\nbell.\r\n\r\nNext she was at the cottage rousing Hosmer. But the alarm of the bell\r\nhad already awakened him, and he was dressed and out on the porch\r\nalmost as soon as Th\xe9r\xe8se had called. Melicent joined them, highly\r\nagitated, and prepared to contribute her share towards any scene that\r\nmight be going forward. But she found little encouragement for heroics\r\nwith Hosmer. In saddling his horse rather hastily he was as unmoved as\r\nthough preparing for an uneventful morning canter. He stood at the\r\nfoot of the stairs preparing to mount when Gr\xe9goire rode up as if\r\npursued by furies; checking his horse with a quick, violent wrench\r\nthat set it quivering in its taut limbs.\r\n\r\n"Well," said Hosmer, "I guess it\'s done for. How did it happen? who\r\ndid it?"\r\n\r\n"Jo\xe7int\'s work," answered Gr\xe9goire bitingly.\r\n\r\n"The damned scoundrel," muttered Hosmer, "where is he?"\r\n\r\n"Don\' botha \'bout Jo\xe7int; he ain\'t goin\' to set no mo\' mill afire,"\r\nsaying which, he turned his horse and the two rode furiously away.\r\n\r\nMelicent grasped Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s arm convulsively.\r\n\r\n"What does he mean?" she asked in a frightened whisper.\r\n\r\n"I--I don\'t know," Th\xe9r\xe8se faltered. She had clasped her hands\r\nspasmodically together, at Gr\xe9goire\'s words, trembling with horror of\r\nwhat must be their meaning.\r\n\r\n"May be he arrested him," suggested the girl.\r\n\r\n"I hope so. Come; let\'s go to bed: there\'s no use staying out here in\r\nthe cold and dark."\r\n\r\nHosmer had left the sitting-room door open, and Th\xe9r\xe8se entered. She\r\napproached Fanny\'s door and knocked twice: not brusquely, but\r\nsufficiently loud to be heard from within, by any one who was awake.\r\nNo answer came, and she went away, knowing that Fanny slept.\r\n\r\nThe unusual sound of the bell, ringing two hours past midnight--that\r\nvery deadest hour of the night--had roused the whole plantation. On\r\nall sides squads of men and a few venturesome women were hurrying\r\ntowards the fire; the dread of supernatural encounters overcome for\r\nthe moment by such strong reality and by the confidence lent them in\r\neach other\'s company.\r\n\r\nThere were many already gathered around the mill, when Gr\xe9goire and\r\nHosmer reached it. All effort to save anything had been abandoned as\r\nuseless. The books and valuables had been removed from the office. The\r\nfew householders--mill-hands--whose homes were close by, had carried\r\ntheir scant belongings to places of safety, but everything else was\r\ngiven over to the devouring flames.\r\n\r\nThe heat from this big raging fire was intense, and had driven most of\r\nthe gaping spectators gradually back--almost into the woods. But\r\nthere, to one side, where the fire was rapidly gaining, and making\r\nitself already uncomfortably felt, stood a small awe-stricken group\r\ntalking in whispers; their ignorance and superstition making them\r\nirresolute to lay a hand upon the dead Jo\xe7int. His body lay amongst\r\nthe heavy timbers, across a huge beam, with arms outstretched and head\r\nhanging down upon the ground. The glazed eyes were staring up into the\r\nred sky, and on his swarthy visage was yet the horror which had come\r\nthere, when he looked in the face of death.\r\n\r\n"In God\'s name, what are you doing?" cried Hosmer. "Can\'t some of you\r\ncarry that boy\'s body to a place of safety?"\r\n\r\nGr\xe9goire had followed, and was looking down indifferently at the dead.\r\n"Come, len\' a han\' there; this is gittin\' too durn hot," he said,\r\nstooping to raise the lifeless form. Hosmer was preparing to help him.\r\nBut there was some one staggering through the crowd; pushing men to\r\nright and left. With now a hand upon the breast of both Hosmer and\r\nGr\xe9goire, and thrusting them with such force and violence, as to lay\r\nthem prone amongst the timbers. It was the father. It was old Morico.\r\nHe had awakened in the night and missed his boy. He had seen the fire;\r\nindeed close enough that he could hear its roaring; and he knew\r\neverything. The whole story was plain to him as if it had been told by\r\na revealing angel. The strength of his youth had come back to speed\r\nhim over the ground.\r\n\r\n"Murderers!" he cried looking about him with hate in his face. He did\r\nnot know who had done it; no one knew yet, and he saw in every man he\r\nlooked upon the possible slayer of his child.\r\n\r\nSo here he stood over the prostrate figure; his old gray jeans hanging\r\nloosely about him; wild eyed--with bare head clasped between his\r\nclaw-like hands, which the white disheveled hair swept over. Hosmer\r\napproached again, offering gently to help him carry his son away.\r\n\r\n"Stand back," he hurled at him. But he had understood the offer. His\r\nboy must not be left to burn like a log of wood. He bent down and\r\nstrove to lift the heavy body, but the effort was beyond his strength.\r\nSeeing this he stooped again and this time grasped it beneath the\r\narms; then slowly, draggingly, with halting step, began to move\r\nbackward.\r\n\r\nThe fire claimed no more attention. All eyes were fastened upon this\r\nweird picture; a sight which moved the most callous to offer again and\r\nagain assistance, that was each time spurned with an added defiance.\r\n\r\nHosmer stood looking on, with folded arms; moved by the grandeur and\r\nmajesty of the scene. The devouring element, loosed in its awful\r\nrecklessness there in the heart of this lonely forest. The motley\r\ngroup of black and white standing out in the great red light,\r\npowerless to do more than wait and watch. But more was he stirred to\r\nthe depths of his being, by the sight of this human tragedy enacted\r\nbefore his eyes.\r\n\r\nOnce, the old man stops in his backward journey. Will he give over?\r\nhas his strength deserted him? is the thought that seizes every\r\non-looker. But no--with renewed effort he begins again his slow\r\nretreat, till at last a sigh of relief comes from the whole watching\r\nmultitude. Morico with his burden has reached a spot of safety. What\r\nwill he do next? They watch in breathless suspense. But Morico does\r\nnothing. He only stands immovable as a carved image. Suddenly there is\r\na cry that reaches far above the roar of fire and crash of falling\r\ntimbers: "_Mon fils! mon gar\xe7on!_" and the old man totters and falls\r\nbackward to earth, still clinging to the lifeless body of his son. All\r\nhasten towards him. Hosmer reaches him first. And when he gently lifts\r\nthe dead Jo\xe7int, the father this time makes no hinderance, for he too\r\nhas gone beyond the knowledge of all earthly happenings.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII\r\n\r\nMelicent Leaves Place-du-Bois.\r\n\r\n\r\nThere had been no witness to the killing of Jo\xe7int; but there were few\r\nwho did not recognize Gr\xe9goire\'s hand in the affair. When met with the\r\naccusation, he denied it, or acknowledged it, or evaded the charge\r\nwith a jest, as he felt for the moment inclined. It was a deed\r\ncharacteristic of any one of the Santien boys, and if not altogether\r\nlaudable--Jo\xe7int having been at the time of the shooting unarmed--yet\r\nwas it thought in a measure justified by the heinousness of his\r\noffense, and beyond dispute, a benefit to the community.\r\n\r\nHosmer reserved the expression of his opinion. The occurrence once\r\nover, with the emotions which it had awakened, he was inclined to look\r\nat it from one of those philosophic stand-points of his friend\r\nHomeyer. Heredity and pathology had to be considered in relation with\r\nthe slayer\'s character. He saw in it one of those interesting problems\r\nof human existence that are ever turning up for man\'s contemplation,\r\nbut hardly for the exercise of man\'s individual judgment. He was\r\nconscious of an inward repulsion which this action of Gr\xe9goire\'s\r\nawakened in him,--much the same as a feeling of disgust for an animal\r\nwhose instinct drives it to the doing of violent deeds,--yet he made\r\nno difference in his manner towards him.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se was deeply distressed over this double tragedy: feeling keenly\r\nthe unhappy ending of old Morico. But her chief sorrow came from the\r\ncallousness of Gr\xe9goire, whom she could not move even to an avowal of\r\nregret. He could not understand that he should receive any thing but\r\npraise for having rid the community of so offensive and dangerous a\r\npersonage as Jo\xe7int; and seemed utterly blind to the moral aspect of\r\nhis deed.\r\n\r\nAn event at once so exciting and dramatic as this conflagration, with\r\nthe attendant deaths of Morico and his son, was much discussed amongst\r\nthe negroes. They were a good deal of one opinion in regard to Jo\xe7int\r\nhaving been only properly served in getting "w\'at he done ben lookin\'\r\nfu\' dis long time." Gr\xe9goire was rather looked upon as a clever\r\ninstrument in the Lord\'s service; and the occurrence pointed a moral\r\nwhich they were not likely to forget.\r\n\r\nThe burning of the mill entailed much work upon Hosmer, to which he\r\nturned with a zest--an absorption that for the time excluded\r\neverything else.\r\n\r\nMelicent had shunned Gr\xe9goire since the shooting. She had avoided\r\nspeaking with him--even looking at him. During the turmoil which\r\nclosely followed upon the tragic event, this change in the girl had\r\nescaped his notice. On the next day he suspected it only. But the\r\nthird day brought him the terrible conviction. He did not know that\r\nshe was making preparations to leave for St. Louis, and quite\r\naccidentally overheard Hosmer giving an order to one of the unemployed\r\nmill hands to call for her baggage on the following morning before\r\ntrain time.\r\n\r\nAs much as he had expected her departure, and looked painfully forward\r\nto it, this certainty--that she was leaving on the morrow and without\r\na word to him--bewildered him. He abandoned at once the work that was\r\noccupying him.\r\n\r\n"I didn\' know Miss Melicent was goin\' away to-morrow," he said in a\r\nstrange pleading voice to Hosmer.\r\n\r\n"Why, yes," Hosmer answered, "I thought you knew. She\'s been talking\r\nabout it for a couple of days."\r\n\r\n"No, I didn\' know nothin\' \'tall \'bout it," he said, turning away and\r\nreaching for his hat, but with such nerveless hand that he almost\r\ndropped it before placing it on his head.\r\n\r\n"If you\'re going to the house," Hosmer called after him, "tell\r\nMelicent that Woodson won\'t go for her trunks before morning. She\r\nthought she\'d need to have them ready to-night."\r\n\r\n"Yes, if I go to the house. I don\' know if I\'m goin\' to the house or\r\nnot," he replied, walking listlessly away.\r\n\r\nHosmer looked after the young man, and thought of him for a moment: of\r\nhis soft voice and gentle manner--perplexed that he should be the same\r\nwho had expressed in confidence the single regret that he had not been\r\nable to kill Jo\xe7int more than once.\r\n\r\nGr\xe9goire went directly to the house, and approached that end of the\r\nveranda on which Melicent\'s room opened. A trunk had already been\r\npacked and fastened and stood outside, just beneath the low-silled\r\nwindow that was open. Within the room, and also beneath the window,\r\nwas another trunk, before which Melicent kneeled, filling it more or\r\nless systematically from an abundance of woman\'s toggery that lay in a\r\ncumbrous heap on the floor beside her. Gr\xe9goire stopped at the window\r\nto tell her, with a sad attempt at indifference:\r\n\r\n"Yo\' brotha says don\'t hurry packin\'; Woodson ain\'t goin\' to come fur\r\nyour trunks tell mornin\'."\r\n\r\n"All right, thank you," glancing towards him for an instant carelessly\r\nand going on with her work.\r\n\r\n"I didn\' know you was goin\' away."\r\n\r\n"That\'s absurd: you knew all along I was going away," she returned,\r\nwith countenance as expressionless as feminine subtlety could make it.\r\n\r\n"W\'y don\'t you let somebody else do that? Can\'t you come out yere a\r\nw\'ile?"\r\n\r\n"No, I prefer doing it myself; and I don\'t care to go out."\r\n\r\nWhat could he do? what could he say? There were no convenient depths\r\nin his mind from which he might draw at will, apt and telling speeches\r\nto taunt her with. His heart was swelling and choking him, at sight of\r\nthe eyes that looked anywhere, but in his own; at sight of the lips\r\nthat he had one time kissed, pressed into an icy silence. She went on\r\nwith her task of packing, unmoved. He stood a while longer, silently\r\nwatching her, his hat in his hands that were clasped behind him, and a\r\nstupor of grief holding him vise-like. Then he walked away. He felt\r\nsomewhat as he remembered to have felt oftentimes as a boy, when ill\r\nand suffering, his mother would put him to bed and send him a cup of\r\nbouillon perhaps, and a little negro to sit beside him. It seemed very\r\ncruel to him now that some one should not do something for him--that\r\nhe should be left to suffer this way. He walked across the lawn over\r\nto the cottage, where he saw Fanny pacing slowly up and down the\r\nporch.\r\n\r\nShe saw him approach and stood in a patch of sunlight to wait for him.\r\nHe really had nothing to say to her as he stood grasping two of the\r\nbalustrades and looking up at her. He wanted somebody to talk to him\r\nabout Melicent.\r\n\r\n"Did you know Miss Melicent was goin\' away?"\r\n\r\nHad it been Hosmer or Th\xe9r\xe8se asking her the question she would have\r\nreplied simply "yes," but to Gr\xe9goire she said "yes; thank Goodness,"\r\nas frankly as though she had been speaking to Belle Worthington. "I\r\ndon\'t see what\'s kept her down here all this time, anyway."\r\n\r\n"You don\'t like her?" he asked, stupefied at the strange possibility\r\nof any one not loving Melicent to distraction.\r\n\r\n"No. You wouldn\'t either, if you knew her as well as I do. If she\r\nlikes a person she goes on like a lunatic over them as long as it\r\nlasts; then good-bye John! she\'ll throw them aside as she would an old\r\ndress."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I believe she thinks a heap of Aunt Th\xe9r\xe8se."\r\n\r\n"All right; you\'ll see how much she thinks of Aunt Th\xe9r\xe8se. And the\r\npeople she\'s been engaged to! There ain\'t a worse flirt in the city of\r\nSt. Louis; and always some excuse or other to break it off at the last\r\nminute. I haven\'t got any use for her, Lord knows. There ain\'t much\r\nlove lost between us."\r\n\r\n"Well, I reckon she knows they ain\'t anybody born, good enough fur\r\nher?" he said, thinking of those engagements that she had shattered.\r\n\r\n"What was David doing?" Fanny asked abruptly.\r\n\r\n"Writin\' lettas at the sto\'."\r\n\r\n"Did he say when he was coming?"\r\n\r\n"No."\r\n\r\n"Do you guess he\'ll come pretty soon?"\r\n\r\n"No, I reckon not fur a good w\'ile."\r\n\r\n"Is Melicent with Mrs. Laferm?"\r\n\r\n"No; she\'s packin\' her things."\r\n\r\n"I guess I\'ll go sit with Mrs. Laferm, d\'you think she\'ll mind?"\r\n\r\n"No, she\'ll be glad to have you."\r\n\r\nFanny crossed over to go join Th\xe9r\xe8se. She liked to be with her when\r\nthere was no danger of interruption from Melicent, and Gr\xe9goire went\r\nwandering aimlessly about the plantation.\r\n\r\nHe staked great hopes on what the night might bring for him. She would\r\nmelt, perhaps, to the extent of a smile or one of her old glances. He\r\nwas almost cheerful when he seated himself at table; only he and his\r\naunt and Melicent. He had never seen her look so handsome as now, in a\r\nwoolen gown that she had not worn before, of warm rich tint, that\r\nbrought out a certain regal splendor that he had not suspected in her.\r\nA something that she seemed to have held in reserve till this final\r\nmoment. But she had nothing for him--nothing. All her conversation was\r\naddressed to Th\xe9r\xe8se; and she hurried away from table at the close of\r\nthe meal, under pretext of completing her arrangements for departure.\r\n\r\n"Doesn\'t she mean to speak to me?" he asked fiercely of Th\xe9r\xe8se.\r\n\r\n"Oh, Gr\xe9goire, I see so much trouble around me; so many sad mistakes,\r\nand I feel so powerless to right them; as if my hands were tied. I\r\ncan\'t help you in this; not now. But let me help you in other ways.\r\nWill you listen to me?"\r\n\r\n"If you want to help me, Aunt," he said stabbing his fork into a piece\r\nof bread before him, "go and ask her if she doesn\'t mean to talk to\r\nme: if she won\'t come out on the gallery a minute."\r\n\r\n"Gr\xe9goire wants to know if you won\'t go out and speak to him a moment,\r\nMelicent," said Th\xe9r\xe8se entering the girl\'s room. "Do as you wish, of\r\ncourse. But remember you are going away to-morrow; you\'ll likely never\r\nsee him again. A friendly word from you now, may do more good than you\r\nimagine. I believe he\'s as unhappy at this moment as a creature can\r\nbe!"\r\n\r\nMelicent looked at her horrified. "I don\'t understand you at all, Mrs.\r\nLafirme. Think what he\'s done; murdered a defenseless man! How can you\r\nhave him near you--seated at your table? I don\'t know what nerves you\r\nhave in your bodies, you and David. There\'s David, hobnobbing with\r\nhim. Even that Fanny talking to him as if he were blameless. Never! If\r\nhe were dying I wouldn\'t go near him."\r\n\r\n"Haven\'t you a spark of humanity in you?" asked Th\xe9r\xe8se, flushing\r\nviolently.\r\n\r\n"Oh, this is something physical," she replied, shivering, "let me\r\nalone."\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se went out to Gr\xe9goire, who stood waiting on the veranda. She\r\nonly took his hand and pressed it telling him good-night, and he knew\r\nthat it was a dismissal.\r\n\r\nThere may be lovers, who, under the circumstances, would have felt\r\nsufficient pride to refrain from going to the dep\xf4t on the following\r\nmorning, but Gr\xe9goire was not one of them. He was there. He who only a\r\nweek before had thought that nothing but her constant presence could\r\nreconcile him with life, had narrowed down the conditions for his\r\nlife\'s happiness now to a glance or a kind word. He stood close to the\r\nsteps of the Pullman car that she was about to enter, and as she\r\npassed him he held out his hand, saying "Good-bye." But he held his\r\nhand to no purpose. She was much occupied in taking her valise from\r\nthe conductor who had hoisted her up, and who was now shouting in\r\nstentorian tones "All aboard," though there was not a soul with the\r\nslightest intention of boarding the train but herself.\r\n\r\nShe leaned forward to wave good-bye to Hosmer, and Fanny, and Th\xe9r\xe8se,\r\nwho were on the platform; then she was gone.\r\n\r\nGr\xe9goire stood looking stupidly at the vanishing train.\r\n\r\n"Are you going back with us?" Hosmer asked him. Fanny and Th\xe9r\xe8se had\r\nwalked ahead.\r\n\r\n"No," he replied, looking at Hosmer with ashen face, "I got to go fine\r\nmy hoss."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII\r\n\r\nWith Loose Rein.\r\n\r\n\r\n"De Lord be praised fu\' de blessin\'s dat he showers down \'pon us," was\r\nUncle Hiram\'s graceful conclusion of his supper, after which he pushed\r\nhis empty plate aside regretfully, and addressed Aunt Belindy.\r\n"\xa0\'Pears to me, Belindy, as you reached a pint wid dem bacon an\' greens\r\nto-night, dat you never tetched befo\'. De pint o\' de flavorin\' is w\'at\r\nI alludes to."\r\n\r\n"All de same, dat ain\'t gwine to fetch no mo\'," was the rather uncivil\r\nreply to this neat compliment to her culinary powers.\r\n\r\n"Dah!" cried the youthful Betsy, who formed one of the trio gathered\r\ntogether in the kitchen at Place-du-Bois. "Jis listen (to) Unc\' Hiurm!\r\nAunt B\'lindy neva tetched a han\' to dem bacon an\' greens. She tole me\r\nout o\' her own mouf to put\'em on de fiar; she warn\'t gwine pesta wid\r\n\'em."\r\n\r\n"Warn\'t gwine pesta wid \'em?" administering a cuff on the ear of the\r\ntoo communicative Betsy, that sent her sprawling across the table.\r\n"T\'inks I\'se gwine pesta wid you--does you? Messin\' roun\' heah in de\r\nkitchin\' an\' ain\'t tu\'ned down a bed or drawed a bah, or done a lick\r\no\' yo\' night wurk yit."\r\n\r\n"I is done my night wurk, too," returned Betsy whimpering but\r\ndefiantly, as she retreated beyond reach of further blows from Aunt\r\nBelindy\'s powerful right hand.\r\n\r\n"Dat harshness o\' yourn, Belindy, is wat\'s a sourin\' yo\' tempa, an\' a\r\nturnin\' of it intur gall an\' wormwood. Does you know wat de Scripture\r\ntells us of de wrathful woman?"\r\n\r\n"Whar I got time to go a foolin\' wid Scripture? W\'at I wants to know;\r\nwhar dat Pierson boy, he don\'t come. He ben gone time \'nough to walk\r\nto Natch\'toches an\' back."\r\n\r\n"Ain\'t dat him I years yonda tu de crib?" suggestod Betsy, coming to\r\njoin Aunt Belindy in the open doorway.\r\n\r\n"You heahs mos\' too much fu\' yo\' own good, you does, gal."\r\n\r\nBut Betsy was right. For soon a tall, slim negro, young and coal\r\nblack, mounted the stairs and came into the kitchen, where he\r\ndeposited a meal bag filled with various necessities that he had\r\nbrought from Centerville. He was one of the dancers who had displayed\r\ntheir skill before Melicent and Gr\xe9goire. Uncle Hiram at once accosted\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n"Well, Pierson, we jest a ben a wonderin\' consarnin\' you. W\'at was de\r\n\'casion o\' dat long delay?"\r\n\r\n"De \'casion? W\'y man alive, I couldn\'t git a dog gone soul in de town\r\nto wait on me."\r\n\r\n"Dat boy kin lie, yas," said Aunt Belindy, "God A\'mighty knows ever\r\ntime I ben to Centaville dem sto\' keepas ain\'t done a blessed t\'ing\r\nbut settin\' down."\r\n\r\n"Settin\' down--Lord! dey warn\'t settin\' down to-day; you heah me."\r\n\r\n"W\'at dey doin\' ef dey ain\'t settin\' down, Unc\' Pierson?" asked Betsy\r\nwith amiable curiosity.\r\n\r\n"You jis drap dat \'uncle,\' you," turning wrathfully upon the girl,\r\n"sence w\'en you start dat new trick?"\r\n\r\n"Lef de chile \'lone, Pierson, lef \'er alone. Come heah, Betsy, an\' set\r\nby yo\' Uncle Hiurm."\r\n\r\nFrom the encouraging nearness of Uncle Hiram, she ventured to ask\r\n"w\'at you \'low dey doin\' ef dey ain\'t settin\' down?" this time without\r\nadding the offensive title.\r\n\r\n"Dey flyin\' \'roun\', Lord! dey hidin\' dey sef! dey gittin\' out o\' de\r\nway, I tell you. Gr\xe9gor jis ben a raisin\' ole Cain in Centaville."\r\n\r\n"I know\'d it; could a\' tole you dat mese\'f. My Lan\'! but dats a piece,\r\ndat Gr\xe9gor," Aunt Belindy enunciated between paroxysms of laughter,\r\nseating herself with her fat arms resting on her knees, and her whole\r\nbearing announcing pleased anticipation.\r\n\r\n"Dat boy neva did have no car\' fur de salvation o\' his soul," groaned\r\nUncle Hiram.\r\n\r\n"W\'at he ben a doin\' yonda?" demanded Aunt Belindy impatiently.\r\n\r\n"Well," said Pierson, assuming a declamatory air and position in the\r\nmiddle of the large kitchen, "he lef\' heah--w\'at time he lef heah,\r\nAunt B\'lindy?"\r\n\r\n"He done lef\' fo\' dinna, \'caze I seed \'im a lopin\' to\'ads de riva,\r\ntime I flung dat Sampson boy out o\' de doo\', bringin\' dem greens in\r\nheah \'dout washin\' of \'em."\r\n\r\n"Dat\'s so; it war good dinna time w\'en he come a lopin\' in town. Dat\r\nhoss look like he ben swimmin\' in Cane Riva, he done ride him so hard.\r\nHe fling he se\'f down front o\' Grammont\'s sto\' an\' he come a stompin\'\r\nin, look like gwine hu\'t somebody. Ole Grammont tell him, \'How you\r\ncome on, Gr\xe9gor? Come ova tu de house an\' eat dinna wid us: de ladies\r\nbe pleas tu see you.\'\xa0"\r\n\r\n"Humph," muttered Aunt Belindy, "dem Grammont gals be glad to see any\r\nt\'ing dat got breeches on; lef \'lone good lookin\' piece like dat\r\nGr\xe9gor."\r\n\r\n"Gr\xe9gor, he neva sey, \'Tank you dog,\' jis\' fling he big dolla down on\r\nde counta an\' \'low \'don\'t want no dinna: gimme some w\'iskey.\'\xa0"\r\n\r\n"Yas, yas, Lord," from Aunt Belindy.\r\n\r\n"Ole Grammont, he push de bottle to\'ads \'im, an\' I \'clar to Goodness\r\nef he didn\' mos fill dat tumbla to de brim, an\' drink it down, neva\r\nblink a eye. Den he tu\'n an treat ev\'y las\' w\'ite man stan\'in\' roun\';\r\ndat ole kiarpenta man; de blacksmif; Marse Verdon. He keep on a\r\ntreatin\'; Grammont, he keep a handin\' out de w\'iskey; Gr\xe9gor he keep\r\non a drinkin\' an a treatin\'--Grammont, he keep a handin\' out; don\'t\r\nmake no odds tu him s\'long uz dat bring de money in de draw. I ben a\r\nstan\'in\' out on de gallery, me, a peekin\' in. An\' Gr\xe9gor, he cuss and\r\nswar an\' he kiarry on, an \'low he want play game poka. Den dey all\r\ngoes a trompin\' in de back room an\' sets down roun\' de table, an\' I\r\ncomes a creepin\' in, me, whar I kin look frough de doo\', an dar dey\r\nsets an\' plays an Gr\xe9gor, he drinks w\'iskey an\' he wins de money. An\'\r\narta w\'ile Marse Verdon, he little eyes blinkin\', he \'low\', \'y\' all\r\nhad a shootin\' down tu Place-du-Bois, _hein_ Gr\xe9gor?\' Gr\xe9gor, he neva\r\nsay nuttin\': he jis\' draw he pistol slow out o\' he pocket an\' lay it\r\ndown on de table; an\' he look squar in Marse Verdon eyes. Man! ef you\r\neva seed some pussun tu\'n\' w\'ite!"\r\n\r\n"Reckon dat heifa \'Milky\' look black side li\'le Verdon dat time,"\r\nchuckled Aunt Belindy.\r\n\r\n"Jis\' uz w\'ite uz Unc\' Hiurm\'s shurt an\' a trimblin\', an\' neva say no\r\nmo\' \'bout shootin\'. Den ole Grammont, he kine o\' hang back an\' say,\r\n\'You git de jestice de peace, \'hine you, kiarrin\' conceal\' weepons dat\r\na-way, Gr\xe9gor.\'\xa0"\r\n\r\n"Dat ole Grammont, he got to git he gab in ef he gwine die fu\' it,"\r\ninterrupted Aunt Belindy.\r\n\r\n"Gr\xe9gor say--\'I don\'t \'lows to kiarr no conceal\' weepons,\' an he draw\r\nnudda pistol slow out o\' he udda pocket an\' lay et on de table. By dat\r\ntime he gittin\' all de money, he crammin\' de money in he pocket; an\'\r\ndem fellas dey gits up one arta d\'udda kine o\' shy-like, an\' sneaks\r\nout. Den Gr\xe9gor, he git up an come out o\' de room, he coat \'crost he\r\narm, an\' de pistols a stickin\' out an him lookin\' sassy tell ev\'y body\r\nmake way, same ef he ben Jay Goul\'. Ef he look one o\' \'em in de eye\r\ndey outs wid, \'Howdy, Gr\xe9gor--how you come on, Gr\xe9gor?\' jis\' uz pelite\r\nuz a peacock, an\' him neva take no trouble to yansa \'em. He jis\' holla\r\nout fu\' somebody bring dat hoss tu de steps, an\' him stan\'in\' \'s big\r\nuz life, waitin\'. I gits tu de hoss fus\', me, an\' leads \'im up, an\' he\r\ngits top dat hoss stidy like he ain\'t tetch a drap, an\' he fling me\r\nbig dolla."\r\n\r\n"Whar de dolla, Mista Pierson?" enquired Betsy.\r\n\r\n"De dolla in my pocket, an\' et gwine stay dah. Didn\' ax you fu\' no\r\n\'Mista Pierson.\' Whar yu\' all tink he went on dat hoss?"\r\n\r\n"How you reckon we knows whar he wint; we wasn\'t dah," replied Aunt\r\nBelindy.\r\n\r\n"He jis\' went a lopin\' twenty yards down to Chartrand\'s sto\'. I goes\r\non \'hine \'im see w\'at he gwine do. Dah he git down f\'um de hoss an\' go\r\na stompin\' in de sto\'--eve\'ybody stan\'in\' back jis\' same like fu\' Jay\r\nGoul\', an\' he fling bill down on de counta an\' \'low, \'Fill me up a\r\nbottle, Chartrand, I\'se gwine travelin\'.\' Den he \'lows, \'You treats\r\neve\'y las\' man roun\' heah at my \'spence, black an\' w\'ite--nuttin\' fu\'\r\nme,\' an\' he fole he arms an\' lean back on de counta, jis\' so.\r\nChartrand, he look skeerd, he say \'Fran\xe7ois gwine wait on you.\' But\r\nGr\xe9gor, he \'low he don\'t wants no rusty skileton a waitin\' on him w\'en\r\nhe treat, \'Wait on de gemmen yo\'se\'f--step up gemmen.\' Chartrand \'low,\r\n\'Damn ef nigga gwine drink wid w\'ite man in dat sto\',\' all same he\r\nkine git \'hine box tu say dat."\r\n\r\n"Lord, Lord, de ways o\' de transgressor!" groaned Uncle Hiram.\r\n\r\n"You want to see dem niggas sneaking \'way," resumed Pierson, "dey\r\nknows Gr\xe9gor gwine fo\'ce \'em drink; dey knows Chartrand gwine make it\r\nhot fu\' \'em art\'ards ef dey does. Gr\xe9gor he spie me jis\' I\'se tryin\'\r\nglide frough de doo\' an he call out, \'Yonda a gemmen f\'um\r\nPlace-du-Bois; Pierson, come heah; you\'se good \'nough tu drink wid any\r\nw\'ite man, \'cept me; you come heah, take drink wid Mr. Louis\r\nChartrand.\'\r\n\r\n"I \'lows don\'t wants no drink, much \'bleege, Marse Gr\xe9gor\'. \'Yis, you\r\nwants drink,\' an\' \'id dat he draws he pistol. \'Mista Chartrand want\r\ndrink, too. I done owe Mista Chartrand somethin\' dis long time; I\'se\r\ngwine pay \'im wid a treat,\' he say. Chartrand look like he on fiar, he\r\nso red, he so mad, he swell up same like ole bull frog."\r\n\r\n"Dat make no odd," chuckled Aunt Belindy, "he gwine drink wid nigga ef\r\nGr\xe9gor say so."\r\n\r\n"Yes, he drink, Lord, only he cuss me slow, an\' \'low he gwine break my\r\nskull."\r\n\r\n"Lordy! I knows you was jis\' a trimblin\', Mista Pierson."\r\n\r\n"Warn\'t trimblin\' no mo\' \'en I\'se trimblin\' dis minute, an\' you drap\r\ndat \'Mista.\' Den w\'at you reckon? Yonda come P\xe8re Antoine; he come an\'\r\nstan\' in de doo\' an\' he hole up he han\'; look like he ain\'t \'feard no\r\nbody an\' he \'low: \'Gr\xe9gor Sanchun, how is you dar\' come in dis heah\r\npeaceful town frowin\' of it into disorda an\' confusion? Ef you isn\'t\r\n\'feard o\' man; hasn\'t you got no fear o\' God A\'mighty wat punishes?\'\xa0"\r\n\r\n"Gr\xe9gor, he look at \'im an\' he say cool like, \'Howdy, P\xe8re Antoine;\r\nhow you come on?\' He got he pistol w\'at he draw fu\' make Chartrand\r\ndrink wid dis heah nigga,--he foolin\' wid it an\' a rubbin\' it up and\r\ndown he pants, an\' he \'low \'Dis a gemmen w\'at fit to drink wid a\r\nSanchun--w\'at\'ll you have?\' But P\xe8re Antoine, he go on makin\' a su\'mon\r\nsame like he make in chu\'ch, an\' Gr\xe9gor, he lean he two arm back on de\r\ncounta--kine o\' smilin\' like, an\' he say, \'Chartrand, whar dat bottle\r\nI orda you put up?\' Chartrand bring de bottle; Gr\xe9gor, he put de\r\nbottle in he coat pocket wat hang on he arm--car\'ful.\r\n\r\n"P\xe8re Antoine, he go on preachin\', he say, \'I tell you dis young man,\r\nyou \'se on de big road w\'at leads tu hell.\'\r\n\r\n"Den Gr\xe9gor straight he se\'f up an\' walk close to P\xe8re Antoine an\' he\r\nsay, \'Hell an\' damnation dar ain\'t no sich a place. I reckon she know;\r\nw\'at you know side o\' her. She say dar ain\'t no hell, an\' ef you an\'\r\nde Archbishop an\' de Angel Gabriel come along an\' \'low dey a hell, you\r\nall liars,\' an\' he say, \'Make way dah, I\'se a gittin\' out o\' heah; dis\r\nain\'t no town fittin\' to hol\' a Sanchun. Make way ef you don\' wants to\r\ngo to Kingdom come fo\' yo\' time.\'\r\n\r\n"Well, I \'lows dey did make way. Only P\xe8re Antoine, he look mighty\r\nsorry an\' down cas\'.\r\n\r\n"Gr\xe9gor go out dat sto\' taking plenty room, an\' walkin\' car\'ful like,\r\nan\' he swing he se\'f on de hoss; den he lean down mos\' flat an\' stick\r\nhe spurs in dat hoss an\' he go tar\'in\' like de win\' down street, out\r\no\' de town, a firin\' he pistol up in de a\'r."\r\n\r\nUncle Hiram had listened to the foregoing recital with troubled\r\ncountenance, and with many a protesting groan. He now shook his old\r\nwhite head, and heaved a deep sigh. "All dat gwine come hard an\' heavy\r\non de madam. She don\'t desarve it--God knows, she don\'t desarve it."\r\n\r\n"How you, ole like you is, kin look fu\' somethin\' diffunt, Unc\'\r\nHiurm?" observed Aunt Belindy philosophically. "Don\'t you know Gr\xe9gor\r\ngwine be Gr\xe9gor tell he die? Dat\'s all dar is \'bout it."\r\n\r\nBetsy arose with the sudden recollection that she had let the time\r\npass for bringing in Miss Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s hot water, and Pierson went to the\r\nstove to see what Aunt Belindy had reserved for him in the shape of\r\nsupper.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX\r\n\r\nThe Reason Why.\r\n\r\n\r\nSampson, the young colored boy who had lighted Fanny\'s fire on the\r\nfirst day of her arrival at Place-du-Bois, and who had made such\r\ninsinuating advances of friendliness towards her, had continued to\r\nattract her notice and good will. He it was who lighted her fires on\r\nsuch mornings as they were needed. For there had been no winter.
