In\r\nmid-January, the grass was fresh and green; trees and plants were\r\nputting forth tender shoots, as if in welcome to spring; roses were\r\nblossoming, and it was a veritable atmosphere of Havana rather than of\r\ncentral Louisiana that the dwellers at Place-du-Bois were enjoying.\r\nBut finally winter made tardy assertion of its rights. One morning\r\nbroke raw and black with an icy rain falling, and young Sampson\r\narriving in the early bleakness to attend to his duties at the\r\ncottage, presented a picture of human distress to move the most\r\nhardened to pity. Though dressed comfortably in the clothing with\r\nwhich Fanny had apparelled him--he was ashen. Save for the chattering\r\nof his teeth, his body seemed possessed of a paralytic inability to\r\nmove. He knelt before the empty fire-place as he had done on that\r\nfirst day, and with deep sighs and groans went about his work. Then he\r\nremained long before the warmth that he had kindled; even lying full\r\nlength upon the soft rug, to bask in the generous heat that permeated\r\nand seemed to thaw his stiffened limbs.\r\n\r\nNext, he went quietly into the bedroom to attend to the fire there.\r\nHosmer and Fanny were still sleeping. He approached a decorated basket\r\nthat hung against the wall; a receptacle for old newspapers and odds\r\nand ends. He drew something from his rather capacious coat pocket,\r\nand, satisfying himself that Hosmer slept, thrust it in the bottom of\r\nthe basket, well covered by the nondescript accumulation that was\r\nthere.\r\n\r\nThe house was very warm and cheerful when they arose, and after\r\nbreakfasting Hosmer felt unusually reluctant to quit his fire-side and\r\nface the inclement day; for an unaccustomed fatigue hung upon his\r\nlimbs and his body was sore, as from the effect of bruises. But he\r\nwent, nevertheless, well encased in protective rubber; and as he\r\nturned away from the house, Fanny hastened to the hanging basket, and\r\nfumbling nervously in its depths, found what the complaisant Sampson\r\nhad left for her.\r\n\r\nThe cold rain had gradually changed into a fine mist, that in\r\ndescending, spread an icy coat upon every object that it touched. When\r\nHosmer returned at noon, he did not leave the house again.\r\n\r\nDuring the afternoon Th\xe9r\xe8se knocked at Fanny\'s door. She was\r\nenveloped in a long hooded cloak, her face glowing from contact with\r\nthe sharp moist air, and myriad crystal drops clinging to her fluffy\r\nblonde hair that looked very golden under the dark hood that covered\r\nit. She wanted to learn how Fanny accepted this unpleasant change of\r\natmospheric conditions, intending to bear her company for the\r\nremainder of the day if she found her depressed, as was often the\r\ncase.\r\n\r\n"Why, I didn\'t know you were home," she said, a little startled, to\r\nHosmer who opened the door to her. "I came over to show Mrs. Hosmer\r\nsomething pretty that I don\'t suppose she ever saw before." It was a\r\nbranch from a rose-tree, bearing two open blossoms and a multitude of\r\nbuds, creamy pink, all encased in an icy transparency that gleamed\r\nlike diamonds. "Isn\'t it exquisite?" she said, holding the spray up\r\nfor Fanny\'s admiration. But she saw at a glance that the spirit of\r\nDisorder had descended and settled upon the Hosmer household.\r\n\r\nThe usually neat room was in a sad state of confusion. Some of the\r\npictures had been taken from the walls, and were leaning here and\r\nthere against chairs and tables. The mantel ornaments had been removed\r\nand deposited at random and in groups about the room. On the hearth\r\nwas a pail of water in which swam a huge sponge; and Fanny sat beside\r\nthe center-table that was piled with her husband\'s wearing apparel,\r\nholding in her lap a coat which she had evidently been passing under\r\ninspection. Her hair had escaped from its fastenings; her collar was\r\nhooked awry; her face was flushed and her whole bearing indicated her\r\ncondition.\r\n\r\nHosmer took the frozen spray from Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s hand, and spoke a little\r\nabout the beauty of the trees, especially the young cedars that he had\r\npassed out in the hills on his way home.\r\n\r\n"It\'s all well and good to talk about flowers and things, Mrs.\r\nLaferm--sit down please--but when a person\'s got the job that I\'ve got\r\non my hands, she\'s something else to think about. And David here\r\nsmoking one cigar after another. He knows all I\'ve got to do, and goes\r\nand sends those darkies home right after dinner."\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se was so shocked that for a while she could say nothing; till\r\nfor Hosmer\'s sake she made a quick effort to appear at ease.\r\n\r\n"What have you to do, Mrs. Hosmer? Let me help you, I can give you the\r\nwhole afternoon," she said with an appearance of being ready for any\r\nthing that was at hand to be done.\r\n\r\nFanny turned the coat over in her lap, and looked down helplessly at a\r\nstain on the collar, that she had been endeavoring to remove; at the\r\nsame time pushing aside with patient repetition the wisp of hair that\r\nkept falling over her cheek.\r\n\r\n"Belle Worthington\'ll be here before we know it; her and her husband\r\nand that Lucilla of hers. David knows how Belle Worthington is, just\r\nas well as I do; there\'s no use saying he don\'t. If she was to see a\r\nspeck of dirt in this house or on David\'s clothes, or anything, why\r\nwe\'d never hear the last of it. I got a letter from her," she\r\ncontinued, letting the coat fall to the floor, whilst she endeavored\r\nto find her pocket.\r\n\r\n"Is she coming to visit you?" asked Th\xe9r\xe8se who had taken up a feather\r\nbrush, and was dusting and replacing the various ornaments that were\r\nscattered through the room.\r\n\r\n"She\'s going down to Muddy Graw (Mardi-Gras) her and her husband and\r\nLucilla and she\'s going to stop here a while. I had that letter--I\r\nguess I must of left it in the other room."\r\n\r\n"Never mind," Th\xe9r\xe8se hastened to say, seeing that her whole energies\r\nwere centered on finding the letter.\r\n\r\n"Let me look," said Hosmer, making a movement towards the bedroom\r\ndoor, but Fanny had arisen and holding out a hand to detain him she\r\nwent into the room herself, saying she knew where she\'d left it.\r\n\r\n"Is this the reason you\'ve kept yourself shut up here in the house so\r\noften?" Th\xe9r\xe8se asked of Hosmer, drawing near him. "Never telling me a\r\nword of it," she went on, "it wasn\'t right; it wasn\'t kind."\r\n\r\n"Why should I have put any extra burden on you?" he answered, looking\r\ndown at her, and feeling a joy in her presence there, that seemed like\r\na guilty indulgence in face of his domestic shame.\r\n\r\n"Don\'t stay," Th\xe9r\xe8se said. "Leave me here. Go to your office or over\r\nto the house--leave me alone with her."\r\n\r\nFanny returned, having found the letter, and spoke with increased\r\nvehemence of the necessity of having the house in perfect trim against\r\nthe arrival of Belle Worthington, from whom they would never hear the\r\nlast, and so forth.\r\n\r\n"Well, your husband is going out, and that will give us a chance to\r\nget things righted," said Th\xe9r\xe8se encouragingly. "You know men are\r\nalways in the way at such times."\r\n\r\n"It\'s what he ought to done before; and left Suze and Minervy here,"\r\nshe replied with grudging acquiescence.\r\n\r\nAfter repeated visits to the bedroom, under various pretexts, Fanny\r\ngrew utterly incapable to do more than sit and gaze stupidly at\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se, who busied herself in bringing the confusion of the\r\nsitting-room into some order.\r\n\r\nShe continued to talk disjointedly of Belle Worthington and her well\r\nknown tyrannical characteristics in regard to cleanliness; finishing\r\nby weeping mildly at the prospect of her own inability to ever reach\r\nthe high standard required by her exacting friend.\r\n\r\nIt was far in the afternoon--verging upon night, when Th\xe9r\xe8se\r\nsucceeded in persuading her that she was ill and should go to bed. She\r\ngladly seized upon the suggestion of illness; assuring Th\xe9r\xe8se that\r\nshe alone had guessed her affliction: that whatever was thought\r\nsingular in her behavior must be explained by that sickness which was\r\npast being guessed at--then she went to bed.\r\n\r\nIt was late when Hosmer left his office; a rough temporary shanty, put\r\ntogether near the ruined mill.\r\n\r\nHe started out slowly on his long cold ride. His physical malaise of\r\nthe morning had augmented as the day went on, and he was beginning to\r\nadmit to himself that he was "in for it."\r\n\r\nBut the cheerless ride was lightened by a picture that had been with\r\nhim through the afternoon, and that moved him in his whole being, as\r\nthe moment approached when it might be changed to reality. He knew\r\nFanny\'s habits; knew that she would be sleeping now. Th\xe9r\xe8se would not\r\nleave her there alone in the house--of that he was sure. And he\r\npictured Th\xe9r\xe8se at this moment seated at his fire-side. He would find\r\nher there when he entered. His heart beat tumultuously at the thought.\r\nIt was a very weak moment with him, possibly, one in which his\r\nunnerved condition stood for some account. But he felt that when he\r\nsaw her there, waiting for him, he would cast himself at her feet and\r\nkiss them. He would crush her white hands against his bosom. He would\r\nbury his face in her silken hair. She should know how strong his love\r\nwas, and he would hold her in his arms till she yield back tenderness\r\nto his own. But--Th\xe9r\xe8se met him on the steps. As he was mounting\r\nthem, she was descending; wrapped in her long cloak, her pretty head\r\ncovered by the dark hood.\r\n\r\n"Oh, are you going?" he asked.\r\n\r\nShe heard the note of entreaty in his voice.\r\n\r\n"Yes," she answered, "I shouldn\'t have left her before you came; but I\r\nknew you were here; I heard your horse\'s tread a moment ago. She\'s\r\nasleep. Good night. Take courage and have a brave heart," she said,\r\npressing his hand a moment in both hers, and was gone.\r\n\r\nThe room was as he had pictured it; order restored and the fire\r\nblazing brightly. On the table was a pot of hot tea and a tempting\r\nlittle supper laid. But he pushed it all aside and buried his face\r\ndown upon the table into his folded arms, groaning aloud. Physical\r\nsuffering; thwarted love, and at the same time a feeling of\r\nself-condemnation, made him wish that life were ended for him.\r\n\r\nFanny awoke close upon morning, not knowing what had aroused her. She\r\nwas for a little while all bewildered and unable to collect herself.\r\nShe soon learned the cause of her disturbance. Hosmer was tossing\r\nabout and his outstretched arm lay across her face, where it had\r\nevidently been flung with some violence. She took his hand to move it\r\naway, and it burned her like a coal of fire. As she touched him he\r\nstarted and began to talk incoherently. He evidently fancied himself\r\ndictating a letter to some insurance company, in no pleased terms--of\r\nwhich Fanny caught but snatches. Then:\r\n\r\n"That\'s too much, Mrs. Lafirme; too much--too much--Don\'t let Gr\xe9goire\r\nburn--take him from the fire, some one. Thirty day\'s credit--shipment\r\nmade on tenth," he rambled on at intervals in his troubled sleep.\r\n\r\nFanny trembled with apprehension as she heard him. Surely he has brain\r\nfever she thought, and she laid her hand gently on his burning\r\nforehead. He covered it with his own, muttering "Th\xe9r\xe8se, Th\xe9r\xe8se--so\r\ngood--let me love you."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX\r\n\r\nPerplexing Things.\r\n\r\n\r\n"Lucilla!"\r\n\r\nThe pale, drooping girl started guiltily at her mother\'s sharp\r\nexclamation, and made an effort to throw back her shoulders. Then she\r\nbit her nails nervously, but soon desisted, remembering that that\r\nalso, as well as yielding to a relaxed tendency of the spinal column,\r\nwas a forbidden indulgence.\r\n\r\n"Put on your hat and go on out and get a breath of fresh air; you\'re\r\nas white as milk-man\'s cream."\r\n\r\nLucilla rose and obeyed her mother\'s order with the precision of a\r\nsoldier, following the directions of his commander.\r\n\r\n"How submissive and gentle your daughter is," remarked Th\xe9r\xe8se.\r\n\r\n"Well, she\'s got to be, and she knows it. Why, I haven\'t got to do\r\nmore than look at that girl most times for her to understand what I\r\nwant. You didn\'t notice, did you, how she straightened up when I\r\ncalled \'Lucilla\' to her? She knows by the tone of my voice what she\'s\r\ngot to do."\r\n\r\n"Most mothers can\'t boast of having such power over their daughters."\r\n\r\n"Well, I\'m not the woman to stand any shenanigans from a child of\r\nmine. I could name you dead loads of women that are just completely\r\nwalked over by their children. It\'s a blessing that boy of Fanny\'s\r\ndied, between you and I; its what I\'ve always said. Why, Mrs. Laferm,\r\nshe couldn\'t any more look after a youngster than she could after a\r\nbaby elephant. By the by, what do you guess is the matter with her,\r\nany way?"\r\n\r\n"How, the matter?" Th\xe9r\xe8se asked; the too ready blood flushing her\r\nface and neck as she laid down her work and looked up at Mrs.\r\nWorthington.\r\n\r\n"Why, she\'s acting mighty queer, that\'s all I can say for her."\r\n\r\n"I haven\'t been able to see her for some time," Th\xe9r\xe8se returned,\r\ngoing back to her sewing, "but I suppose she got a little upset and\r\nnervous over her husband; he had a few days of very serious illness\r\nbefore you came."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I\'ve seen her in all sorts of states and conditions, and I\'ve\r\nnever seen her like that before. Why, she does nothing in the God\'s\r\nworld but whine and sniffle, and wish she was dead; it\'s enough to\r\ngive a person the horrors. She can\'t make out she\'s sick; I never saw\r\nher look better in my life. She must of gained ten pounds since she\r\ncome down here."\r\n\r\n"Yes," said Th\xe9r\xe8se, "she was looking so well, and--and I thought\r\neverything was going well with her too, but--" and she hesitated to go\r\non.\r\n\r\n"Oh, I know what you want to say. You can\'t help that. No use\r\nbothering your brains about that--now you just take my advice,"\r\nexclaimed Mrs. Worthington brusquely.\r\n\r\nThen she laughed so loud and suddenly that Th\xe9r\xe8se, being already\r\nnervous, pricked her finger with her needle till the blood came; a\r\nmishap which decided her to lay aside her work.\r\n\r\n"If you never saw a fish out of water, Mrs. Laferm, do take a peep at\r\nMr. Worthington astride that horse; it\'s enough to make a cat expire!"\r\n\r\nMrs. Worthington was on the whole rather inclined to take her husband\r\nseriously. As often as he might excite her disapproval, it was seldom\r\nthat he aroused her mirth. So it may be gathered that his appearance\r\nin this unfamiliar r\xf4le of horseman was of the most mirth-provoking.\r\n\r\nHe and Hosmer were dismounting at the cottage, which decided Mrs.\r\nWorthington to go and look after them; Fanny for the time being--in\r\nher opinion--not having "the gumption to look after a sick kitten."\r\n\r\n"This is what I call solid comfort," she said looking around the well\r\nappointed sitting-room, before quitting it.\r\n\r\n"You ought to be a mighty happy woman, Mrs. Laferm; only I\'d think\r\nyou\'d die of lonesomeness, sometimes."\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se laughed, and told her not to forget that she expected them all\r\nover in the evening.\r\n\r\n"You can depend on me; and I\'ll do my best to drag Fanny over;\r\nso-long."\r\n\r\nWhen left alone, Th\xe9r\xe8se at once relapsed into the gloomy train of\r\nreflections that had occupied her since the day she had seen with her\r\nbodily eyes something of the wretched life that she had brought upon\r\nthe man she loved. And yet that wretchedness in its refinement of\r\ncruelty and immorality she could not guess and was never to know.\r\nStill, she had seen enough to cause her to ask herself with a shudder\r\n"was I right--was I right?"\r\n\r\nShe had always thought this lesson of right and wrong a very plain\r\none. So easy of interpretation that the simplest minded might solve it\r\nif they would. And here had come for the first time in her life a\r\nstaggering doubt as to its nature. She did not suspect that she was\r\nsubmitting one of those knotty problems to her unpracticed judgment\r\nthat philosophers and theologians delight in disagreeing upon, and her\r\ninability to unravel it staggered her. She tried to convince herself\r\nthat a very insistent sting of remorse which she felt, came from\r\nselfishness--from the pain that her own heart suffered in the\r\nknowledge of Hosmer\'s unhappiness. She was not callous enough to quiet\r\nher soul with the balm of having intended the best. She continued to\r\nask herself only "was I right?" and it was by the answer to that\r\nquestion that she would abide, whether in the stony content of\r\naccomplished righteousness, or in an enduring remorse that pointed to\r\na goal in whose labyrinthine possibilities her soul lost itself and\r\nfainted away.\r\n\r\nLucilla went out to get a breath of fresh air as her mother had\r\ncommanded, but she did not go far to seek it. Not further than the end\r\nof the back veranda, where she stood for some time motionless, before\r\nbeginning to occupy herself in a way which Aunt Belindy, who was\r\nwatching her from the kitchen window, considered highly problematical.\r\nThe negress was wiping a dish and giving it a fine polish in her\r\nabsence of mind. When her curiosity could no longer contain itself she\r\ncalled out:\r\n\r\n"W\'ats dat you\'se doin\' dah, you li\'le gal? Come heah an\' le\' me see."\r\nLucilla turned with the startled look which seemed to be usual with\r\nher when addressed.\r\n\r\n"Le\' me see," repeated Aunt Belindy pleasantly.\r\n\r\nLucilla approached the window and handed the woman a small square of\r\nstiff writing paper which was stuck with myriad tiny pin-holes; some\r\nof which she had been making when interrupted by Aunt Belindy.\r\n\r\n"W\'at in God A\'Mighty\'s name you call dat \'ar?" the darkey asked\r\nexamining the paper critically, as though expecting the riddle would\r\nsolve itself before her eyes.\r\n\r\n"Those are my acts I\'ve been counting," the girl replied a little\r\ngingerly.\r\n\r\n"Yo\' ax? I don\' see nuttin\' \'cep\' a piece o\' papah plum fill up wid\r\nholes. W\'at you call ax?"\r\n\r\n"Acts--acts. Don\'t you know what acts are?"\r\n\r\n"How you want me know? I neva ben to no school whar you larn all dat."\r\n\r\n"Why, an act is something you do that you don\'t want to do--or\r\nsomething you don\'t want to do, that you do--I mean that you don\'t do.\r\nOr if you want to eat something and don\'t. Or an aspiration; that\'s an\r\nact, too."\r\n\r\n"Go long! W\'ats dat--aspiration?"\r\n\r\n"Why, to say any kind of little prayer; or if you invoke our Lord, or\r\nour Blessed Lady, or one of the saints, that\'s an aspiration. You can\r\nmake them just as quick as you can think--you can make hundreds and\r\nhundreds in a day."\r\n\r\n"My Lan\'! Dat\'s w\'at you\'se studyin\' \'bout w\'en you\'se steppin\' \'roun\'\r\nheah like a droopy pullet? An\' I t\'ought you was studyin\' \'bout dat\r\nbeau you lef\' yonda to Sent Lous."\r\n\r\n"You mustn\'t say such things to me; I\'m going to be a religious."\r\n\r\n"How dat gwine henda you have a beau ef you\'se religious?"\r\n\r\n"The religious never get married," turning very red, "and don\'t live\r\nin the world like others."\r\n\r\n"Look heah, chile, you t\'inks I\'se fool? Religion--no religion, whar\r\nyou gwine live ef you don\' live in de word? Gwine live up in de moon?"\r\n\r\n"You\'re a very ignorant person," replied Lucilla, highly offended. "A\r\nreligious devotes her life to God, and lives in the convent."\r\n\r\n"Den w\'y you neva said \'convent\'? I knows all \'bout convent. W\'at you\r\ngwine do wid dem ax w\'en de papah done all fill up?" handing the\r\nsingular tablet back to her.\r\n\r\n"Oh," replied Lucilla, "when I have thousands and thousands I gain\r\ntwenty-five years\' indulgence."\r\n\r\n"Is dat so?"\r\n\r\n"Yes," said the girl; and divining that Aunt Belindy had not\r\nunderstood, "twenty-five years that I don\'t have to go to purgatory.\r\nYou see most people have to spend years and years in purgatory, before\r\nthey can get to Heaven."\r\n\r\n"How you know dat?"\r\n\r\nIf Aunt Belindy had asked Lucilla how she knew that the sun shone, she\r\ncould not have answered with more assurance "because I know" as she\r\nturned and walked rather scornfully away.\r\n\r\n"W\'at dat kine o\' fool talk dey larns gals up yonda tu Sent Lous? An\'\r\nhuh ma a putty woman; yas, bless me; all dress up fittin\' to kill.\r\nDon\' \'pear like she studyin\' \'bout ax."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXI\r\n\r\nA Social Evening.\r\n\r\n\r\nMr. and Mrs. Joseph Duplan with their little daughter Ninette, who had\r\nbeen invited to Place-du-Bois for supper, as well as for the evening,\r\nwere seated with Th\xe9r\xe8se in the parlor, awaiting the arrival of the\r\ncottage guests. They had left their rather distant plantation, Les\r\nCh\xeani\xe8res, early in the afternoon, wishing as usual to make the most\r\nof these visits, which, though infrequent, were always so much\r\nenjoyed.\r\n\r\nThe room was somewhat altered since that summer day when Th\xe9r\xe8se had\r\nsat in its cool shadows, hearing the story of David Hosmer\'s life.\r\nOnly with such difference, however, as the change of season called\r\nfor; imparting to it a rich warmth that invited to sociability and\r\nfriendly confidences. In the depths of the great chimney glowed with a\r\nsteady and dignified heat, the huge back-log, whose disposal Uncle\r\nHiram had superintended in person; and the leaping flames from the dry\r\nhickories that surrounded it, lent a very genial light to the\r\ngrim-visaged Lafirmes who looked down from their elevation on the\r\ninteresting group gathered about the hearth.\r\n\r\nConversation had never once flagged with these good friends; for,\r\naside from much neighborhood gossip to be told and listened to, there\r\nwas the always fertile topic of "crops" to be discussed in all its\r\nbearings, that touched, in its local and restricted sense, the labor\r\nquestion, cultivation, freight rates, and the city merchant.\r\n\r\nWith Mrs. Duplan there was a good deal to be said about the unusual\r\nmortality among "Plymouth-Rocks" owing to an alarming prevalence of\r\n"pip," which malady, however, that lady found to be gradually yielding\r\nto a heroic treatment introduced into her _basse-cour_ by one Coulon,\r\na piney wood sage of some repute as a mystic healer.\r\n\r\nThis was a delicate refined little woman, somewhat old-fashioned and\r\nstranded in her incapability to keep pace with the modern conduct of\r\nlife; but giving her views with a pretty self-confidence, that showed\r\nher a ruler in her peculiar realm.\r\n\r\nThe young Ninette had extended herself in an easy chair, in an\r\nattitude of graceful abandonment, the earnest brown eyes looking\r\neagerly out from under a tangle of auburn hair, and resting with\r\nabsorbed admiration upon her father, whose words and movements she\r\nfollowed with unflagging attentiveness. The fastidious little miss was\r\nclad in a dainty gown that reached scarcely below the knees; revealing\r\nthe shapely limbs that were crossed and extended to let the well shod\r\nfeet rest upon the polished brass fender.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se had given what information lay within her range, concerning\r\nthe company which was expected. But her confidences had plainly been\r\ninsufficient to prepare Mrs. Duplan for the startling effect produced\r\nby Mrs. Worthington on that little woman in her black silk of a\r\nby-gone fashion; so splendid was Mrs. Worthington\'s erect and imposing\r\nfigure, so blonde her blonde hair, so bright her striking color and so\r\ncomprehensive the sweep of her blue and scintillating gown. Yet was\r\nMrs. Worthington not at ease, as might be noticed in the unnatural\r\nquaver of her high-pitched voice and the restless motion of her hands,\r\nas she seated herself with an arm studiedly resting upon the table\r\nnear by.\r\n\r\nHosmer had met the Duplans before; on the occasion of a former visit\r\nto Place-du-Bois and again at Les Ch\xeani\xe8res when he had gone to see\r\nthe planter on business connected with the lumber trade.\r\n\r\nFanny was a stranger to them and promised to remain such; for she\r\nacknowledged her presentation with a silent bow and retreated as far\r\nfrom the group as a decent concession to sociability would permit.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se with her pretty Creole tact was not long in bringing these\r\nseemingly incongruent elements into some degree of harmony. Mr. Duplan\r\nin his courteous and rather lordly way was presently imparting to Mrs.\r\nWorthington certain reminiscences of a visit to St. Louis twenty-five\r\nyears before, when he and Mrs. Duplan had rather hastily traversed\r\nthat interesting town during their wedding journey. Mr. Duplan\'s\r\nmanner had a singular effect upon Mrs. Worthington, who became\r\ndignified, subdued, and altogether unnatural in her endeavor to adjust\r\nherself to it.\r\n\r\nMr. Worthington seated himself beside Mrs. Duplan and was soon trying\r\nto glean information, in his eager short-sighted way, of psychological\r\ninterest concerning the negro race; such effort rather bewildering\r\nthat good lady, who could not bring herself to view the negro as an\r\ninteresting or suitable theme to be introduced into polite\r\nconversation.\r\n\r\nHosmer sat and talked good-naturedly to the little girls, endeavoring\r\nto dispel the shyness with which they seemed inclined to view each\r\nother--and Th\xe9r\xe8se crossed the room to join Fanny.\r\n\r\n"I hope you\'re feeling better," she ventured, "you should have let me\r\nhelp you while Mr. Hosmer was ill."\r\n\r\nFanny looked away, biting her lip, the sudden tears coming to her\r\neyes. She answered with unsteady voice, "Oh, I was able to look after\r\nmy husband myself, Mrs. Laferm."\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se reddened at finding herself so misunderstood. "I meant in your\r\nhousekeeping, Mrs. Hosmer; I could have relieved you of some of that\r\nworry, whilst you were occupied with your husband."\r\n\r\nFanny continued to look unhappy; her features taking on that peculiar\r\ndownward droop which Th\xe9r\xe8se had come to know and mistrust.\r\n\r\n"Are you going to New Orleans with Mrs. Worthington?" she asked, "she\r\ntold me she meant to try and persuade you."\r\n\r\n"No; I\'m not going. Why?" looking suspiciously in Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s face.\r\n\r\n"Well," laughed Th\xe9r\xe8se, "only for the sake of asking, I suppose. I\r\nthought you\'d enjoy Mardi-Gras, never having seen it."\r\n\r\n"I\'m not going anywheres unless David goes along," she said, with an\r\nimpertinent ring in her voice, and with a conviction that she was\r\nadministering a stab and a rebuke. She had come prepared to watch her\r\nhusband and Mrs. Lafirme, her heart swelling with jealous suspicion as\r\nshe looked constantly from one to the other, endeavoring to detect\r\nsigns of an understanding between them. Failing to discover such, and\r\nloth to be robbed of her morbid feast of misery, she set her failure\r\ndown to their pre-determined subtlety. Th\xe9r\xe8se was conscious of a\r\nchange in Fanny\'s attitude, and felt herself unable to account for it\r\notherwise than by whim, which she knew played a not unimportant r\xf4le\r\nin directing the manner of a large majority of women. Moreover, it was\r\nnot a moment to lose herself in speculation concerning this woman\'s\r\ncapricious behavior. Her guests held the first claim upon her\r\nattentions. Indeed, here was Mrs. Worthington even now loudly\r\ndemanding a pack of cards. "Here\'s a gentleman never heard of\r\nsix-handed euchre. If you\'ve got a pack of cards, Mrs. Laferm, I guess\r\nI can show him quick enough that it can be done."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I don\'t doubt Mrs. Worthington\'s ability to make any startling\r\nand pleasing revelations," rejoined the planter good humoredly, and\r\ngallantly following Mrs. Worthington who had risen with the view of\r\nputting into immediate effect her scheme of initiating these slow\r\npeople into the unsuspected possibilities of euchre; a game which,\r\nhowever adaptable in other ways, could certainly not be indulged in by\r\nseven persons. After each one proffering, as is usual on such\r\noccasions, his readiness to assume the character of on-looker, Mr.\r\nWorthington\'s claim to entire indifference, if not inability--confirmed\r\nby his wife--was accepted as the most sincere, and that gentleman was\r\nexcluded and excused.\r\n\r\nHe watched them as they seated themselves at table, even lending\r\nassistance, in his own awkward way, to range the chairs in place. Then\r\nhe followed the game for a while, standing behind Fanny to note the\r\noutcome of her reckless offer of "five on hearts," with only three\r\ntrumps in hand, and every indication of little assistance from her\r\npartners, Mr. Duplan and Belle Worthington.\r\n\r\nAt one end of the room was a long, low, well-filled book-case. Here\r\nhad been the direction of Mr. Worthington\'s secret and stolen glances\r\nthe entire evening. And now towards this point he finally transported\r\nhimself by gradual movements which he believed appeared unstudied and\r\nindifferent. He was confronted by a good deal of French--to him an\r\nunfamiliar language. Here a long row of Balzac; then, the Waverley\r\nNovels in faded red cloth of very old date. Racine, Moliere, Bulwer\r\nfollowing in more modern garb; Shakespeare in a compass that promised\r\nvery small type. His quick trained glance sweeping along the shelves,\r\ncontracted into a little frown of resentment while he sent his hand\r\nimpetuously through his scant locks, standing them quite on end.\r\n\r\nOn the very lowest shelf were five imposing volumes in dignified black\r\nand gold, bearing the simple inscription "Lives of the Saints--Rev. A.\r\nButler." Upon one of them, Mr. Worthington seized, opening it at\r\nhazard. He had fallen upon the history of St. Monica, mother of the\r\ngreat St. Austin--a woman whose habits it appears had been so closely\r\nguarded in her childhood by a pious nurse, that even the quenching of\r\nher natural thirst was permitted only within certain well defined\r\nbounds. This mentor used to say "you are now for drinking water, but\r\nwhen you come to be mistress of the cellar, water will be despised,\r\nbut the habit of drinking will stick by you." Highly interesting, Mr.\r\nWorthington thought, as he brushed his hair all down again the right\r\nway and seated himself the better to learn the fortunes of the good\r\nSt. Monica who, curiously enough, notwithstanding those early\r\nincentives to temperance, "insensibly contracted an inclination to\r\nwine," drinking "whole cups of it with pleasure as it came in her\r\nway." A "dangerous intemperance" which it finally pleased Heaven to\r\ncure through the instrumentality of a maid servant taunting her\r\nmistress with being a "wine bibber."\r\n\r\nMr. Worthington did not stop with the story of Saint Monica. He lost\r\nhimself in those details of asceticism, martyrdom, superhuman\r\npossibilities which man is capable of attaining under peculiar\r\nconditions of life--something he had not yet "gone into."\r\n\r\nThe voices at the card table would certainly have disturbed a man with\r\nless power of mind concentration. For Mrs. Worthington in this\r\nfamiliar employment was herself again--_con fuoco_. Here was Mr.\r\nDuplan in high spirits; his wife putting forth little gushes of\r\nbird-like exaltation as the fascinations of the game revealed\r\nthemselves to her. Even Hosmer and Th\xe9r\xe8se were drawn for the moment\r\nfrom their usual preoccupation. Fanny alone was the ghost of the\r\nfeast. Her features never relaxed from their settled gloom. She played\r\nat hap-hazard, listlessly throwing down the cards or letting them fall\r\nfrom her hands, vaguely asking what were trumps at inopportune\r\nmoments; showing that inattentiveness so exasperating to an eager\r\nplayer and which oftener than once drew a sharp rebuke from Belle\r\nWorthington.\r\n\r\n"Don\'t you wish we could play," said Ninette to her companion from her\r\ncomfortable perch beside the fire, and looking longingly towards the\r\ncard table.\r\n\r\n"Oh, no," replied Lucilla briefly, gazing into the fire, with hands\r\nfolded in her lap. Thin hands, showing up very white against the dull\r\ncolored "convent uniform" that hung in plain, severe folds about her\r\nand reached to her very ankles.\r\n\r\n"Oh, don\'t you? I play often at home when company comes. And I play\r\ncribbage and _vingt-et-un_ with papa and win lots of money from him."\r\n\r\n"That\'s wrong."\r\n\r\n"No, it isn\'t; papa wouldn\'t do it if it was wrong," she answered\r\ndecidedly. "Do you go to the convent?" she asked, looking critically\r\nat Lucilla and drawing a little nearer, so as to be confidential.\r\n"Tell me about it," she continued, when the other had replied\r\naffirmatively. "Is it very dreadful? you know they\'re going to send me\r\nsoon."\r\n\r\n"Oh, it\'s the best place in the world," corrected Lucilla as eagerly\r\nas she could.\r\n\r\n"Well, mamma says she was just as happy as could be there, but you see\r\nthat\'s so awfully long ago. It must have changed since then."\r\n\r\n"The convent never changes: it\'s always the same. You first go to\r\nchapel to mass early in the morning."\r\n\r\n"Ugh!" shuddered Ninette.\r\n\r\n"Then you have studies," continued Lucilla. "Then breakfast, then\r\nrecreation, then classes, and there\'s meditation."\r\n\r\n"Oh, well," interrupted Ninette, "I believe anything most would suit\r\nyou, and mamma when she was little; but if I don\'t like it--see here,\r\nif I tell you something will you promise never, never, to tell?"\r\n\r\n"Is it any thing wrong?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, no, not very; it isn\'t a real mortal sin. Will you promise?"\r\n\r\n"Yes," agreed Lucilla; curiosity getting something the better of her\r\npious scruples.\r\n\r\n"Cross your heart?"\r\n\r\nLucilla crossed her heart carefully, though a little reluctantly.\r\n\r\n"Hope you may die?"\r\n\r\n"Oh!" exclaimed the little convent girl aghast.\r\n\r\n"Oh, pshaw," laughed Ninette, "never mind. But that\'s what Polly\r\nalways says when she wants me to believe her: \'hope I may die, Miss\r\nNinette.\' Well, this is it: I\'ve been saving up money for the longest\r\ntime, oh ever so long. I\'ve got eighteen dollars and sixty cents, and\r\nwhen they send me to the convent, if I don\'t like it, I\'m going to run\r\naway." This last and startling revelation was told in a tragic whisper\r\nin Lucilla\'s ear, for Betsy was standing before them with a tray of\r\nchocolate and coffee that she was passing around.\r\n\r\n"I yeard you," proclaimed Betsy with mischievous inscrutable\r\ncountenance.\r\n\r\n"You didn\'t," said Ninette defiantly, and taking a cup of coffee.\r\n\r\n"Yas, I did, I yeard you," walking away.\r\n\r\n"See here, Betsy," cried Ninette recalling the girl, "you\'re not going\r\nto tell, are you?"\r\n\r\n"Dun know ef I isn\'t gwine tell. Dun know ef I isn\'t gwine tell Miss\r\nDuplan dis yere ver\' minute."\r\n\r\n"Oh Betsy," entreated Ninette, "I\'ll give you this dress if you don\'t.\r\nI don\'t want it any more."\r\n\r\nBetsy\'s eyes glowed, but she looked down unconcernedly at the pretty\r\ngown.\r\n\r\n"Don\'t spec it fit me. An\' you know Miss T\'r\xe8se ain\'t gwine let me go\r\nflyin\' roun\' wid my laigs stickin\' out dat away."\r\n\r\n"I\'ll let the ruffle down, Betsy," eagerly proposed Ninette.\r\n\r\n"Betsy!" called Th\xe9r\xe8se a little impatiently.\r\n\r\n"Yas, \'um--I ben waitin\' fu\' de cups."\r\n\r\nLucilla had made many an aspiration--many an "act" the while. This\r\nwhole evening of revelry, and now this last act of wicked conspiracy\r\nseemed to have tainted her soul with a breath of sin which she would\r\nnot feel wholly freed from, till she had cleansed her spirit in the\r\nwaters of absolution.\r\n\r\nThe party broke up at a late hour, though the Duplans had a long\r\ndistance to go, and, moreover, had to cross the high and turbid river\r\nto reach their carriage which had been left on the opposite bank,\r\nowing to the difficulty of the crossing.\r\n\r\nMr. Duplan took occasion of a moment aside to whisper to Hosmer with\r\nthe air of a connoisseur, "fine woman that Mrs. Worthington of yours."\r\n\r\nHosmer laughed at the jesting implication, whilst disclaiming it, and\r\nFanny looked moodily at them both, jealously wondering at the cause of\r\ntheir good humor.\r\n\r\nMrs. Duplan, under the influence of a charming evening passed in such\r\nagreeable and distinguished company, was full of amiable bustle in\r\nleaving and had many pleasant parting words to say to each, in her\r\npretty broken English.\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes, ma\'am," said Mrs. Worthington to that lady, who had taken\r\nadmiring notice of the beautiful silver "Holy Angels" medal that hung\r\nfrom Lucilla\'s neck and rested against the dark gown. "Lucilla takes\r\nafter Mr. Worthington as far as religion goes--kind of different\r\nthough, for I must say it ain\'t often he darkens the doors of a\r\nchurch."\r\n\r\nMrs. Worthington always spoke of her husband present as of a husband\r\nabsent. A peculiarity which he patiently endured, having no talent for\r\nrepartee, that he had at one time thought of cultivating. But that\r\ntime was long past.\r\n\r\nThe Duplans were the first to leave. Then Th\xe9r\xe8se stood for a while on\r\nthe veranda in the chill night air watching the others disappear\r\nacross the lawn. Mr. and Mrs. Worthington and Lucilla had all shaken\r\nhands with her in saying good night. Fanny followed suit limply and\r\ngrudgingly. Hosmer buttoned his coat impatiently and only lifted his\r\nhat to Th\xe9r\xe8se as he helped his wife down the stairs.\r\n\r\nPoor Fanny! she had already taken exception at that hand pressure\r\nwhich was to come and for which she watched, and now her whole small\r\nbeing was in a jealous turmoil--because there had been none.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXII\r\n\r\nTidings That Sting.\r\n\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se felt that the room was growing oppressive. She had been\r\nsitting all morning alone before the fire, passing in review a great\r\nheap of household linen that lay piled beside her on the floor,\r\nalternating this occupation with occasional careful and tender offices\r\nbestowed upon a wee lamb that had been brought to her some hours\r\nbefore, and that now lay wounded and half lifeless upon a pile of\r\ncoffee sacks before the blaze.\r\n\r\nA fire was hardly needed, except to dispel the dampness that had even\r\nmade its insistent way indoors, covering walls and furniture with a\r\nclammy film. Outside, the moisture was dripping from the glistening\r\nmagnolia leaves and from the pointed polished leaves of the live-oaks,\r\nand the sun that had come out with intense suddenness was drawing it\r\nsteaming from the shingled roof-tops.\r\n\r\nWhen Th\xe9r\xe8se, finally aware of the closeness of the room, opened the\r\ndoor and went out on the veranda, she saw a man, a stranger, riding\r\ntowards the house and she stood to await his approach. He belonged to\r\nwhat is rather indiscriminately known in that section of the State as\r\nthe "piney-woods" genus. A rawboned fellow, lank and long of leg; as\r\nungroomed with his scraggy yellow hair and beard as the scrubby little\r\nTexas pony which he rode. His big soft felt hat had done unreasonable\r\nservice as a head-piece; and the "store clothes" that hung upon his\r\nlean person could never in their remotest freshness have masqueraded\r\nunder the character of "all wool." He was in transit, as the bulging\r\nsaddle-bags that hung across his horse indicated, as well as the rough\r\nbrown blanket strapped behind him to the animal\'s back. He rode up\r\nclose to the rail of the veranda near which Th\xe9r\xe8se stood, and nodded\r\nto her without offering to raise or touch his hat. She was prepared\r\nfor the drawl with which he addressed her, and even guessed at what\r\nhis first words would be.\r\n\r\n"You\'re Mrs. Laferm I \'low?"\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se acknowledged her identity with a bow.\r\n\r\n"My name\'s Jimson; Rufe Jimson," he went on, settling himself on the\r\npony and folding his long knotty hands over the hickory switch that he\r\ncarried in guise of whip.\r\n\r\n"Do you wish to speak to me? won\'t you dismount?" Th\xe9r\xe8se asked.\r\n\r\n"I hed my dinner down to the store," he said taking her proposal as an\r\ninvitation to dine, and turning to expectorate a mouth full of tobacco\r\njuice before continuing. "Capital sardines them air," passing his hand\r\nover his mouth and beard in unctuous remembrance of the oily dainties.\r\n\r\n"I\'m just from Cornstalk, Texas, on mu way to Grant. An\' them roads as\r\nI\'ve traversed isn\'t what I\'d call the best in a fair and square\r\ntalk."\r\n\r\nHis manner bore not the slightest mark of deference. He spoke to\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se as he might have spoken to one of her black servants, or as he\r\nwould have addressed a princess of royal blood if fate had ever\r\nbrought him into such unlikely contact, so clearly was the sense of\r\nhuman equality native to him.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se knew her animal, and waited patiently for his business to\r\nunfold itself.\r\n\r\n"I reckon thar hain\'t no ford hereabouts?" he asked, looking at her\r\nwith a certain challenge.\r\n\r\n"Oh, no; its even difficult crossing in the flat," she answered.\r\n\r\n"Wall, I hed calc\'lated continooing on this near side. Reckon I could\r\nmake it?" challenging her again to an answer.\r\n\r\n"There\'s no road on this side," she said, turning away to fasten more\r\nsecurely the escaped branches of a rose-bush that twined about a\r\ncolumn near which she stood.\r\n\r\nWhether there were a road on this side or on the other side, or no\r\nroad at all, appeared to be matter of equal indifference to Mr.\r\nJimson, so far as his manner showed. He continued imperturbably "I\r\n\'lowed to stop here on a little matter o\' business. \'Tis some out o\'\r\nmu way; more\'n I\'d calc\'lated. You couldn\'t tell the ixact distance\r\nfrom here to Colfax, could you?"\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se rather impatiently gave him the desired information, and\r\nbegged that he would disclose his business with her.\r\n\r\n"Wall," he said, "onpleasant news \'ll keep most times tell you\'re\r\nready fur it. Thet\'s my way o\' lookin\' at it."\r\n\r\n"Unpleasant news for me?" she inquired, startled from her indifference\r\nand listlessness.\r\n\r\n"Rather onpleasant ez I take it. I hain\'t a makin\' no misstatement to\r\npersume thet Gr\xe9gor Sanchun was your nephew?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, yes," responded Th\xe9r\xe8se, now thoroughly alarmed, and approaching\r\nas close to Mr. Rufe Jimson as the dividing rail would permit, "What\r\nof him, please?"\r\n\r\nHe turned again to discharge an accumulation of tobacco juice into a\r\nthick border of violets, and resumed.\r\n\r\n"You see a hot-blooded young feller, ez wouldn\'t take no more \'an give\r\nno odds, stranger or no stranger in the town, he couldn\'t ixpect civil\r\ntreatment; leastways not from Colonel Bill Klayton. Ez I said to\r\nTozier--"\r\n\r\n"Please tell me as quickly as possible what has happened," demanded\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se with trembling eagerness; steadying herself with both hands on\r\nthe railing before her.\r\n\r\n"You see it all riz out o\' a little altercation \'twixt him and Colonel\r\nKlayton in the colonel\'s store. Some says he\'d ben drinkin\'; others\r\ndenies it. Howsomever they did hev words risin\' out o\' the colonel\r\naddressing your nephew under the title o\' \'Frenchy\'; which most takes\r\nez a insufficient cause for rilin\'."\r\n\r\n"He\'s dead?" gasped Th\xe9r\xe8se, looking at the dispassionate Texan with\r\nhorrified eyes.\r\n\r\n"Wall, yes," an admission which he seemed not yet willing to leave\r\nunqualified; for he went on "It don\'t do to alluz speak out open an\'\r\nabove boards, leastways not thar in Cornstalk. But I\'ll \'low to you,\r\nit\'s my opinion the colonel acted hasty. It\'s true \'nough, the young\r\nfeller hed drawed, but ez I said to Tozier, thet\'s no reason to\r\npersume it was his intention to use his gun."\r\n\r\nSo Gr\xe9goire was dead. She understood it all now. The manner of his\r\ndeath was plain to her as if she had seen it, out there in some\r\ndisorderly settlement. Killed by the hand of a stranger with whom\r\nperhaps the taking of a man\'s life counted as little as it had once\r\ncounted with his victim. This flood of sudden and painful intelligence\r\nstaggered her, and leaning against the column she covered her eyes\r\nwith both hands, for a while forgetting the presence of the man who\r\nhad brought the sad tidings.\r\n\r\nBut he had never ceased his monotonous unwinding. "Thar hain\'t no\r\nmanner o\' doubt, marm," he was saying, "thet he did hev the sympathy\r\no\' the intire community--ez far ez they was free to express\r\nit--barrin\' a few. Fur he was a likely young chap, that warn\'t no two\r\nopinions o\' that. Free with his money--alluz ready to set up fur a\r\nfriend. Here\'s a bit o\' writin\' thet\'ll larn you more o\' the\r\npertic\'lars," drawing a letter from his pocket, "writ by the Catholic\r\npriest, by name of O\'Dowd. He \'lowed you mought want proyer meetin\'s\r\nand sich."\r\n\r\n"Masses," corrected Th\xe9r\xe8se, holding out her hand for the letter. With\r\nthe other hand she was wiping away the tears that had gathered thick\r\nin her eyes.\r\n\r\n"Thar\'s a couple more little tricks thet he sont," continued Rufe\r\nJimson, apparently dislocating his joints to reach the depths of his\r\ntrouser pocket, from which he drew a battered pocket book wrapped\r\naround with an infinity of string. From the grimy folds of this\r\nreceptacle he took a small paper parcel which he placed in her hand.\r\nIt was partly unfastened, and as she opened it fully, the pent-up\r\ntears came blindingly--for before her lay a few curling rings of soft\r\nbrown hair, and a pair of scapulars, one of which was pierced by a\r\ntell-tale bullet hole.\r\n\r\n"Won\'t you dismount?" she presently asked again, this time a little\r\nmore kindly.\r\n\r\n"No, marm," said the Texan, jerking his hitherto patient pony by the\r\nbridle till it performed feats of which an impartial observer could\r\nscarcely have suspected it.\r\n\r\n"Don\'t reckon I could make Colfax before dark, do you?"\r\n\r\n"Hardly," she said, turning away, "I\'m much obliged to you, Mr.\r\nJimson, for having taken this trouble--if the flat is on the other\r\nside, you need only call for it."\r\n\r\n"Wall, good day, marm--I wish you luck," he added, with a touch of\r\ngallantry which her tears and sweet feminine presence had inspired.\r\nThen turning, he loped his horse rapidly forward, leaning well back in\r\nthe saddle and his elbows sawing the air.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIII\r\n\r\nMelicent Hears the News.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was talked about and wept about at Place-du-Bois, that Gr\xe9goire\r\nshould be dead. It seemed to them all so unbelievable. Yet, whatever\r\nhesitancy they had in accepting the fact of his death, was perforce\r\nremoved by the convincing proof of Father O\'Dowd\'s letter.\r\n\r\nNone could remember but sweetness and kindness of him. Even Nathan,\r\nwho had been one day felled to earth by a crowbar in Gr\xe9goire\'s hand,\r\nhad come himself to look at that deed as not altogether blamable in\r\nlight of the provocation that had called it forth.\r\n\r\nFanny remembered those bouquets which had been daily offered to her\r\nforlornness at her arrival; and the conversations in which they had\r\nunderstood each other so well. The conviction that he was gone away\r\nbeyond the possibility of knowing him further, moved her to tears.\r\nHosmer, too, was grieved and shocked, without being able to view the\r\nevent in the light of a calamity.\r\n\r\nNo one was left unmoved by the tidings which brought a lowering cloud\r\neven upon the brow of Aunt Belindy, to rest there the whole day. Deep\r\nwere the mutterings she hurled at a fate that could have been so\r\nshort-sighted as to remove from earth so bright an ornament as\r\nGr\xe9goire. Her grief further spent much of itself upon the inoffensive\r\nBetsy, who, for some inscrutable reason was for twenty-four hours\r\ndebarred entrance to the kitchen.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se seated at her desk, devoted a morning to the writing of\r\nletters, acquainting various members of the family with the unhappy\r\nintelligence. She wrote first to Madame Santien, living now her lazy\r\nlife in Paris, with eyes closed to the duties that lay before her and\r\nheart choked up with an egoism that withered even the mother\r\ninstincts. It was very difficult to withhold the reproach which she\r\nfelt inclined to deal her; hard to refrain from upbraiding a\r\nselfishness which for a life-time had appeared to Th\xe9r\xe8se as criminal.\r\n\r\nIt was a matter less nice, less difficult, to write to the\r\nbrothers--one up on the Red River plantation living as best he could;\r\nthe other idling on the New Orleans streets. But it was after all a\r\nshort and simple story to tell. There was no lingering illness to\r\ndescribe; no moment even of consciousness in which harrowing last\r\nwords were to be gathered and recorded. Only a hot senseless quarrel\r\nto be told about; the speeding of a bullet with very sure aim,\r\nand--quick death.\r\n\r\nOf course, masses must be said. Father O\'Dowd was properly instructed.\r\nP\xe8re Antoine in Centerville was addressed on the subject. The Bishop\r\nof Natchitoches, respectfully asked to perform this last sad office\r\nfor the departed soul. And the good old priest and friend at the New\r\nOrleans Cathedral, was informed of her desires. Not that Th\xe9r\xe8se held\r\nvery strongly to this saying of masses for the dead; but it had been a\r\ncustom holding for generations in the family and which she was not\r\ndisposed to abandon now, even if she had thought of it.\r\n\r\nThe last letter was sent to Melicent. Th\xe9r\xe8se made it purposely short\r\nand pointed, with a bare statement of facts--a dry, unemotional\r\ntelling, that sounded heartless when she read it over; but she let it\r\ngo.\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\nMelicent was standing in her small, quaint sitting-room, her back to\r\nthe fire, and her hands clasped behind her. How handsome was this\r\nMelicent! Pouting now, and with eyes half covered by the dark shaded\r\nlids, as they gazed moodily out at the wild snowflakes that were\r\nhurrying like crazy things against the warm window pane and meeting\r\ntheir end there. A loose tea-gown clung in long folds about her. A\r\ndull colored thing, save for the two broad bands of sapphire plush\r\nhanging straight before, from throat to toe. Melicent was plainly\r\ndejected; not troubled, nor sad, only dejected, and very much bored; a\r\ncondition that had made her yawn several times while she looked at the\r\nfalling snow.\r\n\r\nShe was philosophizing a little. Wondering if the world this morning\r\nwere really the unpleasant place that it appeared, or if these\r\nconditions of unpleasantness lay not rather within her own mental\r\nvision; a train of thought that might be supposed to have furnished\r\nher some degree of entertainment had she continued in its pursuit. But\r\nshe chose rather to dwell on her causes of unhappiness, and thus\r\nconvince herself that that unhappiness was indeed outside of her and\r\naround her and not by any possibility to be avoided or circumvented.\r\nThere lay now a letter in her desk from David, filled with admonitions\r\nif not reproof which she felt to be not entirely unjust, on the\r\ndisagreeable subject of Expenses. Looking around the pretty room she\r\nconceded to herself that here had been temptations which she could not\r\nreasonably have been expected to withstand. The temptation to lodge\r\nherself in this charming little flat; furnish it after her own liking;\r\nand install that delightful little old poverty-stricken English woman\r\nas keeper of Proprieties, with her irresistible white starched caps\r\nand her altogether delightful way of inquiring daily after that "poor,\r\ndear, kind Mr. Hosmer." It had all cost a little more than she had\r\nforeseen. But the worst of it, the very worst of it was, that she had\r\nalready begun to ask herself if, for instance, it were not very\r\nirritating to see every day, that same branching palm, posing by the\r\nwindow, in that same yellow jardini\xe8re. If those draperies that\r\nconfronted her were not becoming positively offensive in the monotony\r\nof their solemn folds. If the cuteness and quaintness of the\r\npoverty-stricken little English woman were not after all a source of\r\nentertainment that she would willingly forego on occasion. The answer\r\nto these questions was a sigh that ended in another yawn.\r\n\r\nThen Melicent threw herself into a low easy chair by the table, took\r\nup her visiting book, and bending lazily with her arms resting on her\r\nknees, began to turn over its pages. The names which she saw there\r\nrecalled to her mind an entertainment at which she had assisted on the\r\nprevious afternoon. A progressive euchre party; and the remembrance of\r\nwhat she had there endured now filled her soul with horror.\r\n\r\nShe thought of those hundred cackling women--of course women are never\r\ncackling, it was Melicent\'s exaggerated way of expressing\r\nherself--packed into those small overheated rooms, around those\r\ntwenty-five little tables; and how by no chance had she once found\r\nherself with a congenial set. And how that Mrs. Van Wycke had cheated!\r\nIt was plain to Melicent that she had taken advantage of having fat\r\nMiss Bloomdale for a partner, who went to euchre parties only to show\r\nher hands and rings. And little Mrs. Brinke playing against her.\r\nLittle Mrs. Brinke! A woman who only the other day had read an\r\noriginal paper entitled: "An Hour with Hegel" before her philosophy\r\nclass; who had published that dry mystical affair "Light on the\r\nInscrutable in Dante." How could such a one by any possibility be\r\nsupposed to observe the disgusting action of Mrs. Van Wycke in\r\nthrowing off on her partner\'s trump and swooping down on the last\r\ntrick with her right bower? Melicent would have thought it beneath her\r\nto more than look her contempt as Mrs. Van Wycke rose with a\r\ntriumphant laugh to take her place at a higher table, dragging the\r\nplastic Bloomdale with her. But she did mutter to herself now, "nasty\r\nthief."\r\n\r\n"Johannah," Melicent called to her maid who sat sewing in the next\r\nroom.\r\n\r\n"Yes, Miss."\r\n\r\n"You know Mrs. Van Wycke?"\r\n\r\n"Mrs. Van Wycke, Miss? the lady with the pinted nose that I caught\r\na-feeling of the curtains?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, when she calls again I\'m not at home. Do you understand? not at\r\nhome."\r\n\r\n"Yes, Miss."\r\n\r\nIt was gratifying enough to have thus summarily disposed of Mrs. Van\r\nWycke; but it was a source of entertainment which was soon ended.\r\nMelicent continued to turn over the pages of her visiting book during\r\nwhich employment she came to the conclusion that these people whom she\r\nfrequented were all very tiresome. All, all of them, except Miss Drake\r\nwho had been absent in Europe for the past six months. Perhaps Mrs.\r\nManning too, who was so seldom at home when Melicent called. Who when\r\nat home, usually rushed down with her bonnet on, breathless with "I\r\ncan only spare you a moment, dear. It\'s very sweet of you to come."\r\nShe was always just going to the "Home" where things had got into such\r\na muddle whilst she was away for a week. Or it was that "Hospital"\r\nmeeting where she thought certain members were secretly conniving at\r\nher removal from the presidency which she had held for so many years.\r\nShe was always reading minutes at assemblages which Melicent knew\r\nnothing about; or introducing distinguished guests to Guild room\r\nmeetings. Altogether Melicent saw very little of Mrs. Manning.\r\n\r\n"Johannah, don\'t you hear the bell?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, Miss," said Johannah, coming into the room and depositing a gown\r\non which she had been working, on the back of a chair. "It\'s that\r\npostman," she said, as she fastened her needle to the bosom of her\r\ndress. "And such a one as he is, thinking that people must fly when he\r\nso much as touches the bell, and going off a writing of \'no answer to\r\nbell,\' and me with my hand on the very door-knob."\r\n\r\n"I notice that always happens when I\'m out, Johannah; he\'s ringing\r\nagain."\r\n\r\nIt was Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s letter, and as Melicent turned it about and looked\r\ncritically at the neatly written address, it was not without a hope\r\nthat the reading of it might furnish her a moment\'s diversion. She did\r\nnot faint. The letter did not "fall from her nerveless clasp." She\r\nrather held it very steadily. But she grew a shade paler and looked\r\nlong into the fire. When she had read it three times she folded it\r\nslowly and carefully and locked it away in her desk.\r\n\r\n"Johannah."\r\n\r\n"Yes, Miss."\r\n\r\n"Put that gown away; I shan\'t need it."\r\n\r\n"Yes, Miss; and all the beautiful passmantry that you bought?"\r\n\r\n"It makes no difference, I shan\'t use it. What\'s become of that black\r\ncamel\'s-hair that Mrs. Gauche spoiled so last winter?"\r\n\r\n"It\'s laid away, Miss, the same in the cedar chest as the day it came\r\nhome from her hands and no more fit, that I\'d be a shame meself and no\r\nclaims to a dress-maker. And there\'s many a lady that she never would\r\nhave seen a cent, let alone making herself pay for the spiling of it."\r\n\r\n"Well, well, Johannah, never mind. Get it out, we\'ll see what can be\r\ndone with it. I\'ve had some painful news, and I shall wear mourning\r\nfor a long, long time."\r\n\r\n"Oh, Miss, it\'s not Mr. David! nor yet one of those sweet relations in\r\nUtica? leastways not I hope that beautiful Miss Gertrude, with such\r\nhair as I never see for the goldness of it and not dyed, except me\r\ncousin that\'s a nun, that her mother actually cried when it was cut\r\noff?"\r\n\r\n"No, Johannah; only a very dear friend."\r\n\r\nThere were a few social engagements to be cancelled; and regrets to be\r\nsent out, which she attended to immediately. Then she turned again to\r\nlook long into the fire. That crime for which she had scorned him, was\r\nwiped out now by expiation. For a long time--how long she could not\r\nyet determine--she would wrap herself in garb of mourning and move\r\nabout in sorrowing--giving evasive answer to the curious who\r\nquestioned her. Now might she live again through those summer months\r\nwith Gr\xe9goire--those golden afternoons in the pine woods--whose aroma\r\neven now came back to her. She might look again into his loving brown\r\neyes; feel beneath her touch the softness of his curls. She recalled a\r\nday when he had said, "Neva to see you--my God!" and how he had\r\ntrembled. She recalled--strangely enough and for the first time--that\r\none kiss, and a little tremor brought the hot color to her cheek.\r\n\r\nWas she in love with Gr\xe9goire now that he was dead? Perhaps. At all\r\nevents, for the next month, Melicent would not be bored.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIV\r\n\r\nA Step Too Far.\r\n\r\n\r\nWho of us has not known the presence of Misery? Perhaps as those\r\nfortunate ones whom he has but touched as he passed them by. It may be\r\nthat we see but a promise of him as we look into the prophetic faces\r\nof children; into the eyes of those we love, and the awfulness of\r\nlife\'s possibilities presses into our souls. Do we fly him? hearing\r\nhim gain upon us panting close at our heels, till we turn from the\r\ndesperation of uncertainty to grapple with him? In close scuffle we\r\nmay vanquish him. Fleeing, we may elude him. But what if he creep into\r\nthe sanctuary of our lives, with his subtle omnipresence, that we do\r\nnot see in all its horror till we are disarmed; thrusting the burden\r\nof his companionship upon us to the end! However we turn he is there.\r\nHowever we shrink he is there. However we come or go, or sleep or wake\r\nhe is before us. Till the keen sense grows dull with apathy at looking\r\non him, and he becomes like the familiar presence of sin.\r\n\r\nInto such callousness had Hosmer fallen. He had ceased to bruise his\r\nsoul in restless endeavor of resistance. When the awful presence bore\r\ntoo closely upon him, he would close his eyes and brave himself to\r\nendurance. Yet Fate might have dealt him worse things.\r\n\r\nBut a man\'s misery is after all his own, to make of it what he will or\r\nwhat he can. And shall we be fools, wanting to lighten it with our\r\nplatitudes?\r\n\r\nMy friend, your trouble I know weighs. That you should be driven by\r\nearthly needs to drag the pinioned spirit of your days through rut and\r\nmire. But think of the millions who are doing the like. Or is it your\r\nboy, that part of your own self and that other dearer self, who is\r\nwalking in evil ways? Why, I know a man whose son was hanged the other\r\nday; hanged on the gibbet; think of it. If you be quivering while the\r\nsurgeon cuts away that right arm, remember the poor devil in the\r\nhospital yesterday who had both his sawed off.\r\n\r\nOh, have done, with your mutilated men and your sons on gibbets! What\r\nare they to me? My hurt is greater than all, because it is my own. If\r\nit be only that day after day I must look with warm entreaty into eyes\r\nthat are cold. Let it be but that peculiar trick of feature which I\r\nhave come to hate, seen each morning across the breakfast table. That\r\nrecurrent pin-prick: it hurts. The blow that lays the heart in twain:\r\nit kills. Let be mine which will; it is the one that counts.\r\n\r\nIf Misery kill a man, that ends it. But Misery seldom deals so\r\nsummarily with his victims. And while they are spared to earth, we\r\nfind them usually sustaining life after the accepted fashion.\r\n\r\nHosmer was seated at table, having finished his breakfast. He had also\r\nfinished glancing over the contents of a small memorandum book, which\r\nhe replaced in his pocket. He then looked at his wife sitting opposite\r\nhim, but turned rather hastily to gaze with a certain entreaty into\r\nthe big kind eyes of the great shaggy dog who stood--the shameless\r\nbeggar--at his side.\r\n\r\n"I knew there was something wrong," he said abruptly, with his eyes\r\nstill fixed on the dog, and his fingers thrust into the animal\'s\r\nmatted wool, "Where\'s the mail this morning?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know if that stupid boy\'s gone for it or not. I told him. You\r\ncan\'t depend on any one in a place like this."\r\n\r\nFanny had scarcely touched the breakfast before her, and now pushed\r\naside her cup still half filled with coffee.\r\n\r\n"Why, how\'s that? Sampson seems to do the right thing."\r\n\r\n"Yes, Sampson; but he ain\'t here. That boy of Minervy\'s been doing his\r\nwork all morning."\r\n\r\nMinervy\'s boy was even now making his appearance, carrying a good\r\nsized bundle of papers and letters, with which he walked boldly up to\r\nHosmer, plainly impressed with the importance of this new r\xf4le.\r\n\r\n"Well, colonel; so you\'ve taken Sampson\'s place?" Hosmer observed,\r\nreceiving the mail from the boy\'s little black paws.\r\n\r\n"My name\'s Major, suh. Maje; dats my name. I ain\'t tuck Sampson\'s\r\nplace: no, suh."\r\n\r\n"Oh, he\'s having a day off--" Hosmer went on, smiling quizzingly at\r\nthe dapper little darkey, and handing him a red apple from the dish of\r\nfruit standing in the center of the table. Maje received it with a\r\nvery unmilitary bob of acknowledgment.\r\n\r\n"He yonda home \'cross de riva, suh. He ben too late fu\' kotch de\r\nflat\'s mornin\' An\' he holla an\' holla. He know dey warn\'t gwine cross\r\ndat flat \'gin jis\' fu\' Sampson."\r\n\r\nHosmer had commenced to open his letters. Fanny with her elbows on the\r\ntable, asked the boy--with a certain uneasiness in her voice--"Ain\'t\r\nhe coming at all to-day? Don\'t he know all the work he\'s got to do?\r\nHis mother ought to make him."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t reckon. Dat away Sampson: he git mad he stay mad," with which\r\nassurance Maje vanished through the rear door, towards the region of\r\nthe kitchen, to seek more substantial condiments than the apple which\r\nhe still clutched firmly.\r\n\r\nOne of the letters was for Fanny, which her husband handed her. When\r\nhe had finished reading his own, he seemed disposed to linger, for he\r\ntook from the fruit dish the mate to the red apple he had given Maje,\r\nand commenced to peel it with his clasp knife.\r\n\r\n"What has our friend Belle Worthington to say for herself?" he\r\ninquired good humoredly. "How does she get on with those Creoles down\r\nthere?"\r\n\r\n"You know as well as I do, Belle Worthington ain\'t going to mix with\r\nCreoles. She can\'t talk French if she wanted to. She says Muddy-Graw\r\ndon\'t begin to compare with the Veiled Prophets. It\'s just what I\r\nthought--with their \'Muddy-Graw,\'\xa0" Fanny added, contemptuously.\r\n\r\n"Coming from such high authority, we\'ll consider that verdict a final\r\nclincher," Hosmer laughed a little provokingly.\r\n\r\nFanny was looking again through the several sheets of Belle\r\nWorthington\'s letter. "She says if I\'ll agree to go back with her,\r\nshe\'ll pass this way again."\r\n\r\n"Well, why don\'t you? A little change wouldn\'t hurt."\r\n\r\n"\xa0\'Tain\'t because I want to stay here, Lord knows. A God-forsaken\r\nplace like this. I guess you\'d be glad enough," she added, with voice\r\nshaking a little at her own boldness.\r\n\r\nHe closed his knife, placed it in his pocket, and looked at his wife,\r\ncompletely puzzled.\r\n\r\nThe power of speech had come to her, for she went on, in an unnatural\r\ntone, however, and fumbling nervously with the dishes before her. "I\'m\r\nfool enough about some things, but I ain\'t quite such a fool as that."\r\n\r\n"What are you talking about, Fanny?"\r\n\r\n"That woman wouldn\'t ask anything better than for me to go to St.\r\nLouis."\r\n\r\nHosmer was utterly amazed. He leaned his arms on the table, clasping\r\nhis hands together and looked at his wife.\r\n\r\n"That woman? Belle Worthington? What _do_ you mean, any way?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t mean Belle Worthington," she said excitedly, with two deep\r\nred spots in her cheeks. "I\'m talking about Mrs. Laferm."\r\n\r\nHe thrust his hand into his pockets and leaned back in his chair. No\r\namazement now, but very pale, and with terrible concentration of\r\nglance.\r\n\r\n"Well, then, don\'t talk about Mrs. Lafirme," he said very slowly, not\r\ntaking his eyes from her face.\r\n\r\n"I will talk about her, too. She ain\'t worth talking about," she\r\nblurted incoherently. "It\'s time for somebody to talk about a woman\r\npassing herself off for a saint, and trying to take other women\'s\r\nhusbands--"\r\n\r\n"Shut up!" cried Hosmer maddened with sudden fury, and rising\r\nviolently from his chair.\r\n\r\n"I won\'t shut up," Fanny cried excitedly back at him; rising also.\r\n"And what\'s more I won\'t stay here and have you making love under my\r\nvery eyes to a woman that\'s no better than she ought to be."\r\n\r\nShe meant to say more, but Hosmer grasped her arm with such a grasp,\r\nthat had it been her throat she would never have spoken more. The\r\nother hand went to his pocket, with fingers clutching the clasp knife\r\nthere.\r\n\r\n"By heaven--I\'ll--kill you!" every word weighted with murder, panted\r\nclose in her terrified face. What she would have uttered died upon her\r\npale lips, when her frightened eyes beheld the usually calm face of\r\nher husband distorted by a passion of which she had not dreamed.\r\n\r\n"David," she faltered, "let go my arm."\r\n\r\nHer voice broke the spell that held him, and brought him again to his\r\nsenses. His fingers slowly relaxed their tense hold. A sigh that was\r\nsomething between a moan and a gasp came with his deliverance and\r\nshook him. All the horror now was in his own face as he seized his hat\r\nand hurried speechless away.\r\n\r\nFanny remained for a little while dazed. Hers was not the fine nature\r\nthat would stay cruelly stunned after such a scene. Her immediate\r\nterror being past, the strongest resultant emotion was one of\r\nself-satisfaction at having spoken out her mind.\r\n\r\nBut there was a stronger feeling yet, moving and possessing her;\r\ncrowding out every other. A pressing want that only Sampson\'s coming\r\nwould relieve, and which bade fair to drive her to any extremity if it\r\nwere not appeased.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXV\r\n\r\nA Fateful Solution.\r\n\r\n\r\nHosmer passed the day with a great pain at his heart. His hasty and\r\nviolent passion of the morning had added another weight for his spirit\r\nto drag about, and which he could not cast off. No feeling of\r\nresentment remained with him; only wonder at his wife\'s misshapen\r\nknowledge and keen self-rebuke of his own momentary forgetfulness.\r\nEven knowing Fanny as he did, he could not rid himself of the haunting\r\ndread of having wounded her nature cruelly. He felt much as a man who\r\nin a moment of anger inflicts an irreparable hurt upon some small,\r\nweak, irresponsible creature, and must bear regret for his madness.\r\nThe only reparation that lay within his power--true, one that seemed\r\ninadequate--was an open and manly apology and confession of wrong. He\r\nwould feel better when it was made. He would perhaps find relief in\r\ndiscovering that the wound he had inflicted was not so deep--so\r\ndangerous as he feared.\r\n\r\nWith such end in view he came home early in the afternoon. His wife\r\nwas not there. The house was deserted. Even the servants had\r\ndisappeared. It took but a moment for him to search the various rooms\r\nand find them one after the other, unoccupied. He went out on the\r\nporch and looked around. The raw air chilled him. The wind was blowing\r\nviolently, bringing dashes of rain along with it from massed clouds\r\nthat hung leaden between sky and earth. Could she have gone over to\r\nthe house? It was unlikely, for he knew her to have avoided Mrs.\r\nLafirme of late, with a persistence that had puzzled him to seek its\r\ncause, which had only fully revealed itself in the morning Yet, where\r\nelse could she be? An undefined terror was laying hold of him. His\r\nsensitive nature, in exaggerating its own heartlessness, was blindly\r\noverestimating the delicacy of hers. To what may he not have driven\r\nher? What hitherto untouched chord may he not have started into\r\npainful quivering? Was it for him to gauge the endurance of a woman\'s\r\nspirit? Fanny was not now the wife whom he hated; his own act of the\r\nmorning had changed her into the human being, the weak creature whom\r\nhe had wronged.\r\n\r\nIn quitting the house she must have gone unprepared for the inclement\r\nweather, for there hung her heavy wrap in its accustomed place, with\r\nher umbrella beside it. He seized both and buttoning his own great\r\ncoat about him, hurried away and over to Mrs. Lafirme\'s. He found that\r\nlady in the sitting-room.\r\n\r\n"Isn\'t Fanny here?" he asked abruptly, with no word of greeting.\r\n\r\n"No," she answered looking up at him, and seeing the evident\r\nuneasiness in his face. "Isn\'t she at home? Is anything wrong?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, everything is wrong," he returned desperately, "But the immediate\r\nwrong is that she has disappeared--I must find her."\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se arose at once and called to Betsy who was occupied on the\r\nfront veranda.\r\n\r\n"Yas, um," the girl answered to her mistress\' enquiry. "I seed ma\'am\r\nHosma goin\' to\'ads de riva good hour \'go. She mus\' crost w\'en Nathan\r\ntuck dat load ova. I yain\'t seed \'er comin\' back yit."\r\n\r\nHosmer left the house hastily, hardly reassured by Betsy\'s\r\ninformation. Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s glance--speculating and uneasy--followed his\r\nhurrying figure till it disappeared from sight.\r\n\r\nThe crossing was an affair of extreme difficulty, and which Nathan was\r\nreluctant to undertake until he should have gathered a "load" that\r\nwould justify him in making it. In his estimation, Hosmer did not meet\r\nsuch requirement, even taken in company with the solitary individual\r\nwho had been sitting on his horse with Egyptian patience for long\r\nunheeded moments, the rain beating down upon his back, while he waited\r\nthe ferryman\'s pleasure. But Nathan\'s determination was not proof\r\nagainst the substantial inducements which Hosmer held out to him; and\r\nsoon they were launched, all hands assisting in the toilsome passage.\r\n\r\nThe water, in rising to an unaccustomed height, had taken on an added\r\nand tremendous swiftness. The red turbid stream was eddying and\r\nbulging and hurrying with terrific swiftness between its shallow\r\nbanks, striking with an immensity of power against the projection of\r\nland on which stood Marie Louise\'s cabin, and rebounding in great\r\ncircling waves that spread and lost themselves in the seething\r\nturmoil. The cable used in crossing the unwieldly flat had long been\r\nsubmerged and the posts which held it wrenched from their fastenings.\r\nThe three men, each with his long heavy oar in hand began to pull up\r\nstream, using a force that brought the swelling veins like iron\r\ntracings upon their foreheads where the sweat had gathered as if the\r\nday were midsummer. They made their toilsome way by slow inches, that\r\nfinally landed them breathless and exhausted on the opposite side.\r\n\r\nWhat could have been the inducement to call Fanny out on such a day\r\nand such a venture? The answer came only too readily from Hosmer\'s\r\nreproaching conscience. And now, where to seek her? There was nothing\r\nto guide him; to indicate the course she might have taken. The rain\r\nwas falling heavily and in gusts and through it he looked about at the\r\nsmall cabins standing dreary in their dismantled fields. Marie\r\nLouise\'s was the nearest at hand and towards it he directed his steps.\r\n\r\nThe big good-natured negress had seen his approach from the window,\r\nfor she opened the door to him before he had time to knock, and\r\nentering he saw Fanny seated before the fire holding a pair of very\r\nwet smoking feet to dry. His first sensation was one of relief at\r\nfinding her safe and housed. His next, one of uncertainty as to the\r\nkind and degree of resentment which he felt confident must now show\r\nitself. But this last was soon dispelled, for turning, she greeted him\r\nwith a laugh. He would have rather a blow. That laugh said so many\r\nthings--too many things. True, it removed the dread which had been\r\nhaunting him all day, but it shattered what seemed to have been now\r\nhis last illusion regarding this woman. That unsounded chord which he\r\nfeared he had touched was after all but one in harmony with the rest\r\nof her common nature. He saw too at a glance that her dominant passion\r\nhad been leading and now controlled her. And by one of those rapid\r\ntrains of thought in which odd and detached fancies, facts,\r\nimpressions and observations form themselves into an orderly sequence\r\nleading to a final conviction--all was made plain to him that before\r\nhad puzzled him. She need not have told him her reason for crossing\r\nthe river, he knew it. He dismissed at once the attitude with which he\r\nhad thought to approach her. Here was no forgiveness to be asked of\r\ndulled senses. No bending in expiation of faults committed. He was\r\nhere as master.\r\n\r\n"Fanny, what does this mean?" he asked in cold anger; with no heat\r\nnow, no passion.\r\n\r\n"Yaas, me tell madame, she goin\' fur ketch cole si she don\' mine out.\r\nDat not fur play dat kine wedder, no. Teck chair, M\'sieur; dry\r\nyou\'se\'f leet beet. Me mek you one cup coffee."\r\n\r\nHosmer declined the good Marie Louise\'s kind proffer of coffee, but he\r\nseated himself and waited for Fanny to speak.\r\n\r\n"You know if you want a thing done in this place, you\'ve got to do it\r\nyourself. I\'ve heard you say it myself, time and time again about\r\nthose people at the mill," she said.\r\n\r\n"Could it have been so urgent as to call you out on a day like this,\r\nand with such a perilous crossing? Couldn\'t you have found some one\r\nelse to come for you?"\r\n\r\n"Who? I\'d like to know. Just tell me who? It\'s nothing to you if we\'re\r\nwithout servants, but I\'m not going to stand it. I ain\'t going to let\r\nSamp_son_ act like that without knowing what he means," said Fanny\r\nsharply.\r\n\r\n"Dat Samp_son_, he one leet dev\'," proffered Marie Louise, with\r\nlaudable design of shifting blame upon the easy shoulders of Sampson,\r\nin event of the domestic jar which she anticipated. "No use try do\r\nnuttin\' \'id Sampson, M\'sieur."\r\n\r\n"I had to know something, one way or the other," Fanny said in a tone\r\nwhich carried apology, rather by courtesy than by what she considered\r\ndue.\r\n\r\nHosmer walked to the window where he looked out upon the dreary,\r\ndesolate scene, little calculated to cheer him. The river was just\r\nbelow; and from this window he could gaze down upon the rushing\r\ncurrent as it swept around the bend further up and came striking\r\nagainst this projection with a force all its own. The rain was falling\r\nstill; steadily, blindingly, with wild clatter against the shingled\r\nroof so close above their heads. It coursed in little swift rivulets\r\ndown the furrows of the almost perpendicular banks. It mingled in a\r\ndemon dance with the dull, red water. There was something inviting to\r\nHosmer in the scene. He wanted to be outside there making a part of\r\nit. He wanted to feel that rain and wind beating upon him. Within, it\r\nwas stifling, maddening; with his wife\'s presence there, charging the\r\nroom with an atmosphere of hate that was possessing him and beginning\r\nto course through his veins as it had never done before.\r\n\r\n"Do you want to go home?" he asked bluntly, turning half around.\r\n\r\n"You must be crazy," she replied, with a slow, upward glance out the\r\nwindow, then down at her feet that were still poised on the low stool\r\nthat Marie Louise had placed for her.\r\n\r\n"You\'d better come." He could not have said what moved him, unless it\r\nwere recklessness and defiance.\r\n\r\n"I guess you\'re dreaming, or something, David. You go on home if you\r\nwant. Nobody asked you to come after me any way. I\'m able to take care\r\nof myself, I guess. Ain\'t you going to take the umbrella?" she added,\r\nseeing him start for the door empty handed.\r\n\r\n"Oh, it doesn\'t matter about the rain," he answered without a look\r\nback as he went out and slammed the door after him.\r\n\r\n"M\'sieur look lak he not please," said Marie Louise, with plain regret\r\nat the turn of affairs. "You see he no lak you go out in dat kine\r\nwedder, me know dat."\r\n\r\n"Oh, bother," was Fanny\'s careless reply. "This suits me well enough;\r\nI don\'t care how long it lasts."\r\n\r\nShe was in Marie Louise\'s big rocker, balancing comfortably back and\r\nforth with a swing that had become automatic.
