She felt "good," as she\r\nwould have termed it herself; her visit to Sampson\'s hut having not\r\nbeen without results tending to that condition. The warmth of the room\r\nwas very agreeable in contrast to the bleakness of out-doors. She felt\r\nfree and moved to exercise a looseness of tongue with the amiable old\r\nnegress which was not common with her. The occurrences of the morning\r\nwere gradually withdrawing themselves into a distant perspective that\r\nleft her in the attitude of a spectator rather than that of an actor.\r\nAnd she laughed and talked with Marie Louise, and rocked, and rocked\r\nherself on into drowsiness.\r\n\r\nHosmer had no intention of returning home without his wife. He only\r\nwanted to be out under the sky; he wanted to breathe, to use his\r\nmuscles again. He would go and help cross the flat if need be; an\r\noccupation that promised him relief in physical effort. He joined\r\nNathan, whom he found standing under a big live-oak, disputing with an\r\nold colored woman who wanted to cross to get back to her family before\r\nsupper time.\r\n\r\n"You didn\' have no call to come ova in de fus\' place," he was saying\r\nto her, "you womens is alluz runnin\' back\'ards and for\'ards like\r\nskeard rabbit in de co\'n fiel\'."\r\n\r\n"I don\' stan\' no sich talk is dat f\'om you. Ef you kiant tin\' to yo\'\r\nbusiness o\' totin\' folks w\'en dey wants, you betta quit. You done\r\ncheat Mose out o\' de job, anyways; we all knows dat."\r\n\r\n"Mine out, woman, you gwine git hu\'t. Jis\' le\'me see Mose han\'le dat\r\n\'ar flat onct: Jis\' le\'me. He lan\' you down to de Mouf \'fo\' you knows\r\nit."\r\n\r\n"Let me tell you, Nathan," said Hosmer, looking at his watch, "say you\r\nwait a quarter of an hour and if no one else comes, we\'ll cross Aunt\r\nAgnes anyway."\r\n\r\n"Dat \'nudda t\'ing ef you wants to go back, suh."\r\n\r\nAunt Agnes was grumbling now at Hosmer\'s proposal that promised to\r\nkeep her another quarter of an hour from her expectant family, when a\r\nbig lumbering creaking wagon drove up, with its load of baled cotton\r\nall covered with tarpaulins.\r\n\r\n"Dah!" exclaimed Nathan at sight of the wagon, "ef I\'d \'a listened to\r\nyo\' jawin\'--what?"\r\n\r\n"Ef you\'d listen to me, you\'d \'tin\' to yo\' business betta \'an you\r\ndoes," replied Aunt Agnes, raising a very battered umbrella over her\r\ngrotesquely apparelled figure, as she stepped from under the shelter\r\nof the tree to take her place in the flat.\r\n\r\nBut she still met with obstacles, for the wagon must needs go first.\r\nWhen it had rolled heavily into place with much loud and needless\r\nswearing on the part of the driver who, being a white man, considered\r\nHosmer\'s presence no hindrance, they let go the chain, and once again\r\npulled out. The crossing was even more difficult now, owing to the\r\nextra weight of the wagon.\r\n\r\n"I guess you earn your money, Nathan," said Hosmer bending and\r\nquivering with the efforts he put forth.\r\n\r\n"Yas, suh, I does; an\' dis job\'s wuf mo\' \'an I gits fu\' it."\r\n\r\n"All de same you done lef\' off wurking crap sence you start it,"\r\nmumbled Aunt Agnes.\r\n\r\n"You gwine git hu\'t, woman; I done tole you dat; don\' wan\' listen,"\r\nreturned Nathan with halting breath.\r\n\r\n"Who gwine hu\'t me?"\r\n\r\nWhether from tardy gallantry or from pre-occupation with his arduous\r\nwork, Nathan offered no reply to this challenge, and his silence left\r\nAunt Agnes in possession of the field.\r\n\r\nThey were in full mid-stream. Hosmer and the teamster were in the fore\r\nend of the boat; Nathan in the rear, and Aunt Agnes standing in the\r\ncenter between the wagon and the protecting railing, against which she\r\nleaned her clasped hands that still upheld the semblance of umbrella.\r\n\r\nThe ill-mated horses stood motionless, letting fall their dejected\r\nheads with apathetic droop. The rain was dripping from their\r\nglistening coats, and making a great patter as it fell upon the\r\ntarpaulins covering the cotton bales.\r\n\r\nSuddenly came an exclamation: "Gret God!" from Aunt Agnes, so genuine\r\nin its amazement and dismay, that the three men with one accord looked\r\nquickly up at her, then at the point on which her terrified gaze was\r\nfixed. Almost on the instant of the woman\'s cry, was heard a shrill,\r\npiercing, feminine scream.\r\n\r\nWhat they saw was the section of land on which stood Marie Louise\'s\r\ncabin, undermined--broken away from the main body and gradually\r\ngliding into the water. It must have sunk with a first abrupt wrench,\r\nfor the brick chimney was shaken from its foundation, the smoke\r\nissuing in dense clouds from its shattered sides, the house toppling\r\nand the roof caving. For a moment Hosmer lost his senses. He could but\r\nlook, as if at some awful apparition that must soon pass from sight\r\nand leave him again in possession of his reason. The leaning house was\r\nhalf submerged when Fanny appeared at the door, like a figure in a\r\ndream; seeming a natural part of the awfulness of it. He only gazed\r\non. The two negroes uttered loud lamentations.\r\n\r\n"Pull with the current!" cried the teamster, first to regain his\r\npresence of mind. It had needed but this, to awaken Hosmer to the\r\nsituation.\r\n\r\n"Leave off," he cried at Nathan, who was wringing his hands. "Take\r\nhold that oar or I\'ll throw you overboard." The trembling ashen negro\r\nobeyed on the instant.\r\n\r\n"Hold fast--for God\'s sake--hold fast!" he shouted to Fanny, who was\r\nclinging with swaying figure to the door post. Of Marie Louise there\r\nwas no sign.\r\n\r\nThe caved bank now remained fixed; but Hosmer knew that at any instant\r\nit was liable to disappear before his riveted gaze.\r\n\r\nHow heavy the flat was! And the horses had caught the contagion of\r\nterror and were plunging madly.\r\n\r\n"Whip those horses and their load into the river," called Hosmer,\r\n"we\'ve got to lighten at any price."\r\n\r\n"Them horses an\' cotton\'s worth money," interposed the alarmed\r\nteamster.\r\n\r\n"Force them into the river, I say; I\'ll pay you twice their value."\r\n\r\n"You \'low to pay fur the cotton, too?"\r\n\r\n"Into the river with them or I\'ll brain you!" he cried, maddened at\r\nthe weight and delay that were holding them back.\r\n\r\nThe frightened animals seemed to ask nothing more than to plunge into\r\nthe troubled water; dragging their load with them.\r\n\r\nThey were speeding rapidly towards the scene of catastrophe; but to\r\nHosmer they crawled--the moments were hours. "Hold on! hold fast!" he\r\ncalled again and again to his wife. But even as he cried out, the\r\ndetached section of earth swayed, lurched to one side--plunged to the\r\nother, and the whole mass was submerged--leaving the water above it in\r\nwild agitation.\r\n\r\nA cry of horror went up from the spectators--all but Hosmer. He cast\r\naside his oar--threw off his coat and hat; worked an instant without\r\navail at his wet clinging boots, and with a leap was in the water,\r\nswimming towards the spot where the cabin had gone down. The current\r\nbore him on without much effort of his own. The flat was close up with\r\nhim; but he could think of it no longer as a means of rescue. Detached\r\npieces of timber from the ruined house were beginning to rise to the\r\nsurface. Then something floating softly on the water: a woman\'s dress,\r\nbut too far for him to reach it.\r\n\r\nWhen Fanny appeared again, Hosmer was close beside her. His left arm\r\nwas quickly thrown about her. She was insensible, and he remembered\r\nthat it was best so, for had she been in possession of her reason, she\r\nmight have struggled and impeded his movements. He held her\r\nfast--close to him and turned to regain the shore. Another horrified\r\nshriek went up from the occupants of the flat-boat not far away, and\r\nHosmer knew no more--for a great plunging beam struck him full upon\r\nthe forehead.\r\n\r\nWhen consciousness came back to him, he found that he lay extended in\r\nthe flat, which was fastened to the shore. The confused sound of many\r\nvoices mingled with a ringing din that filled his ears. A warm stream\r\nwas trickling down over his cheek. Another body lay beside him. Now\r\nthey were lifting him. Th\xe9r\xe8se\'s face was somewhere--very near, he saw\r\nit dimly and that it was white--and he fell again into insensibility.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVI\r\n\r\nTo Him Who Waits.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe air was filled with the spring and all its promises. Full with the\r\nsound of it, the smell of it, the deliciousness of it. Such sweet air;\r\nsoft and strong, like the touch of a brave woman\'s hand. The air of an\r\nearly March day in New Orleans. It was folly to shut it out from nook\r\nor cranny. Worse than folly the lady thought who was making futile\r\nendeavors to open the car window near which she sat. Her face had\r\ngrown pink with the effort. She had bit firmly into her red nether\r\nlip, making it all the redder; and then sat down from the\r\nunaccomplished feat to look ruefully at the smirched finger tips of\r\nher Parisian gloves. This flavor of Paris was well about her; in the\r\nfolds of her graceful wrap that set to her fine shoulders. It was\r\nplainly a part of the little black velvet toque that rested on her\r\nblonde hair. Even the umbrella and one small valise which she had just\r\nlaid on the seat opposite her, had Paris written plain upon them.\r\n\r\nThese were impressions which the little grey-garbed conventional\r\nfigure, some seats removed, had been noting since the striking lady\r\nhad entered the car. Points likely to have escaped a man, who--unless\r\na minutely observant one,--would only have seen that she was handsome\r\nand worthy of an admiration that he might easily fancy rising to\r\ndevotion.\r\n\r\nBeside herself and the little grey-garbed figure was an interesting\r\nfamily group at the far end of the car. A husband, but doubly a\r\nfather, surrounded and sat upon by a small band of offspring. A\r\nwife--presumably a mother--absorbed with the view of the outside world\r\nand the elaborate gold chain that hung around her neck.\r\n\r\nThe presence of a large valise, an overcoat, a cane and an umbrella\r\ndisposed on another seat, bespoke a further occupant, likely to be at\r\npresent in the smoking car.\r\n\r\nThe train pushed out from the dep\xf4t. The porter finally made tardy\r\nhaste to the assistance of the lady who had been attempting to open\r\nthe window, and when the fresh morning air came blowing in upon her\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se leaned back in her seat with a sigh of content.\r\n\r\nThere was a full day\'s journey before her. She would not reach\r\nPlace-du-Bois before dark, but she did not shrink from those hours\r\nthat were to be passed alone. She rather welcomed the quiet of them\r\nafter a visit to New Orleans full of pleasant disturbances. She was\r\neager to be home again. She loved Place-du-Bois with a love that was\r\nreal; that had grown deep since it was the one place in the world\r\nwhich she could connect with the presence of David Hosmer. She had\r\noften wondered--indeed was wondering now--if the memory of those\r\nhappenings to which he belonged would ever grow strange and far away\r\nto her. It was a trick of memory with which she indulged herself on\r\noccasion, this one of retrospection. Beginning with that June day when\r\nshe had sat in the hall and watched the course of a white sunshade\r\nover the tops of the bending corn.\r\n\r\nSuch idle thoughts they were with their mingling of bitter and\r\nsweet--leading nowhere. But she clung to them and held to them as if\r\nto a refuge which she might again and again return to.\r\n\r\nThe picture of that one terrible day of Fanny\'s death, stood out in\r\nsharp prominent lines; a touch of the old agony always coming back as\r\nshe remembered how she had believed Hosmer dead too--lying so pale and\r\nbleeding before her. Then the parting which had held not so much of\r\nsorrow as of awe and bewilderment in it: when sick, wounded and broken\r\nhe had gone away at once with the dead body of his wife; when the two\r\nhad clasped hands without words that dared be uttered.\r\n\r\nBut that was a year ago. And Th\xe9r\xe8se thought many things might come\r\nabout in a year. Anyhow, might not such length of time be hoped to rub\r\nthe edge off a pain that was not by its nature lasting?\r\n\r\nThat time of acute trouble seemed to have thrown Hosmer back upon his\r\nold diffidence. The letter he wrote her after a painful illness which\r\nprostrated him on his arrival in St. Louis, was stiff and formal, as\r\nmen\'s letters are apt to be, though it had breathed an untold story of\r\nloyalty which she had felt at the time, and still cherished. Other\r\nletters--a few--had gone back and forth between them, till Hosmer had\r\ngone away to the sea-shore with Melicent, to recuperate, and June\r\ncoming, Th\xe9r\xe8se had sailed from New Orleans for Paris, whither she had\r\npassed six months.\r\n\r\nThings had not gone well at Place-du-Bois during her absence, the\r\nimpecunious old kinsman whom she had left in charge, having a decided\r\npreference for hunting the _Gros-Bec_ and catching trout in the lake\r\nto supervising the methods of a troublesome body of blacks. So Th\xe9r\xe8se\r\nhad had much to engage her thoughts from the morbid channel into which\r\nthose of a more idle woman might have drifted.\r\n\r\nShe went occasionally enough to the mill. There at least she was\r\nalways sure to hear Hosmer\'s name--and what a charm the sound of it\r\nhad for her. And what a delight it was to her eyes when she caught\r\nsight of an envelope lying somewhere on desk or table of the office,\r\naddressed in his handwriting. That was a weakness which she could not\r\npardon herself; but which staid with her, seeing that the same\r\ntrifling cause never failed to awaken the same unmeasured delight. She\r\nhad even trumped up an excuse one day for carrying off one of Hosmer\'s\r\nbusiness letters--indeed of the dryest in substance, and which, when\r\nhalf-way home, she had torn into the smallest bits and scattered to\r\nthe winds, so overcome was she by a sense of her own absurdity.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se had undergone the ordeal of having her ticket scrutinized,\r\ncommented upon and properly punched by the suave conductor. The little\r\nconventional figure had given over the contemplation of Parisian\r\nstyles and betaken herself to the absorbing pages of a novel which she\r\nread through smoked glasses. The husband and father had peeled and\r\ndistributed his second outlay of bananas amongst his family. It was at\r\nthis moment that Th\xe9r\xe8se, looking towards the door, saw Hosmer enter\r\nthe car.\r\n\r\nShe must have felt his presence somewhere near; his being there and\r\ncoming towards her was so much a part of her thoughts. She held out\r\nher hand to him and made place beside her, as if he had left her but a\r\nhalf hour before. All the astonishment was his. But he pressed her\r\nhand and took the seat she offered him.\r\n\r\n"You knew I was on the train?" he asked.\r\n\r\n"Oh, no, how should I?"\r\n\r\nThen naturally followed question and answer.\r\n\r\nYes, he was going to Place-du-Bois.\r\n\r\nNo, the mill did not require his presence; it had been very well\r\nmanaged during his absence.\r\n\r\nYes, she had been to New Orleans. Had had a very agreeable visit.\r\nBeautiful weather for city dwellers. But such dryness. So disastrous\r\nto the planters.\r\n\r\nYes--quite likely there would be rain next month: there usually was in\r\nApril. But indeed there was need of more than April showers for that\r\nstiff land--that strip along the bayou, if he remembered? Oh, he\r\nremembered quite well, but for all that he did not know what she was\r\ntalking about. She did not know herself. Then they grew silent; not\r\nfrom any feeling of the absurdity of such speech between them, for\r\neach had but listened to the other\'s voice. They became silently\r\nabsorbed by the consciousness of each other\'s nearness. She was\r\nlooking at his hand that rested on his knee, and thinking it fuller\r\nthan she remembered it before. She was aware of some change in him\r\nwhich she had not the opportunity to define; but this firmness and\r\nfullness of the hand was part of it. She looked up into his face then,\r\nto find the same change there, together with a new content. But what\r\nshe noted beside was the dull scar on his forehead, coming out like a\r\nred letter when his eyes looked into her own. The sight of it was like\r\na hurt. She had forgotten it might be there, telling its story of pain\r\nthrough the rest of his life.\r\n\r\n"Th\xe9r\xe8se," Hosmer said finally, "won\'t you look at me?"\r\n\r\nShe was looking from the window. She did not turn her head, but her\r\nhand went out and met his that was on the seat close beside her. He\r\nheld it firmly; but soon with an impatient movement drew down the\r\nloose wristlet of her glove and clasped his fingers around her warm\r\nwrist.\r\n\r\n"Th\xe9r\xe8se," he said again; but more unsteadily, "look at me."\r\n\r\n"Not here," she answered him, "not now, I mean." And presently she\r\ndrew her hand away from him and held it for a moment pressed firmly\r\nover her eyes. Then she looked at him with brave loving glance.\r\n\r\n"It\'s been so long," she said, with the suspicion of a sigh.\r\n\r\n"Too long," he returned, "I couldn\'t have borne it but for you--the\r\nthought of you always present with me; helping me to take myself out\r\nof the past. That was why I waited--till I could come to you free.\r\nHave you an idea, I wonder, how you have been a promise, and can be\r\nthe fulfillment of every good that life may give to a man?"\r\n\r\n"No, I don\'t know," she said a little hopelessly, taking his hand\r\nagain, "I have seen myself at fault in following what seemed the only\r\nright. I feel as if there were no way to turn for the truth. Old\r\nsupports appear to be giving way beneath me. They were so secure\r\nbefore. It commenced, you remember--oh, you know when it must have\r\nbegun. But do you think, David, that it\'s right we should find our\r\nhappiness out of that past of pain and sin and trouble?"\r\n\r\n"Th\xe9r\xe8se," said Hosmer firmly, "the truth in its entirety isn\'t given\r\nto man to know--such knowledge, no doubt, would be beyond human\r\nendurance. But we make a step towards it, when we learn that there is\r\nrottenness and evil in the world, masquerading as right and\r\nmorality--when we learn to know the living spirit from the dead\r\nletter. I have not cared to stop in this struggle of life to question.\r\nYou, perhaps, wouldn\'t dare to alone. Together, dear one, we will work\r\nit out. Be sure there is a way--we may not find it in the end, but we\r\nwill at least have tried."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVII\r\n\r\nConclusion.\r\n\r\n\r\nOne month after their meeting on the train, Hosmer and Th\xe9r\xe8se had\r\ngone together to Centerville where they had been made one, as the\r\nsaying goes, by the good P\xe8re Antoine; and without more ado, had\r\ndriven back to Place-du-Bois: Mr. and Mrs. Hosmer. The event had\r\ncaused more than the proverbial nine days\' talk. Indeed, now, two\r\nmonths after, it was still the absorbing theme that occupied the\r\ndwellers of the parish: and such it promised to remain till supplanted\r\nby something of sufficient dignity and importance to usurp its place.\r\n\r\nBut of the opinions, favorable and other, that were being exchanged\r\nregarding them and their marriage, Hosmer and Th\xe9r\xe8se heard little and\r\nwould have cared less, so absorbed were they in the overmastering\r\nhappiness that was holding them in thralldom. They could not yet bring\r\nthemselves to look at it calmly--this happiness. Even the intoxication\r\nof it seemed a thing that promised to hold. Through love they had\r\nsought each other, and now the fulfillment of that love had brought\r\nmore than tenfold its promise to both. It was a royal love; a generous\r\nlove and a rich one in its revelation. It was a magician that had\r\ntouched life for them and changed it into a glory. In giving them to\r\neach other, it was moving them to the fullness of their own\r\ncapabilities. Much to do in two little months; but what cannot love\r\ndo?\r\n\r\n"Could it give a woman more than this?" Th\xe9r\xe8se was saying softly to\r\nherself. Her hands were clasped as in prayer and pressed together\r\nagainst her bosom. Her head bowed and her lips touching the\r\nintertwined fingers. She spoke of her own emotion; of a certain sweet\r\nturmoil that was stirring within her, as she stood out in the soft\r\nJune twilight waiting for her husband to come. Waiting to hear the new\r\nring in his voice that was like a song of joy. Waiting to see that new\r\nstrength and courage in his face, of whose significance she lost\r\nnothing. To see the new light that had come in his eyes with\r\nhappiness. All gifts which love had given her.\r\n\r\n"Well, at last," she said, going to the top of the steps to meet him\r\nwhen he came. Her welcome was in her eyes.\r\n\r\n"At last," he echoed, with a sigh of relief; pressing her hand which\r\nshe held out to him and raising it to his lips.\r\n\r\nHe did not let it go, but passed it through his arm, and together they\r\nturned to walk up and down the veranda.\r\n\r\n"You didn\'t expect me at noon, did you?" he asked, looking down at\r\nher.\r\n\r\n"No; you said you\'d be likely not to come; but I hoped for you all the\r\nsame. I thought you\'d manage it some way."\r\n\r\n"No," he answered her, laughing, "my efforts failed. I used even\r\nstrategy. Held out the temptation of your delightful Creole dishes and\r\nall that. Nothing was of any avail. They were all business and I had\r\nto be all business too, the whole day long. It was horribly stupid."\r\n\r\nShe pressed his arm significantly.\r\n\r\n"And do you think they will put all that money into the mill, David?\r\nInto the business?"\r\n\r\n"No doubt of it, dear. But they\'re shrewd fellows: didn\'t commit\r\nthemselves in any way. Yet I could see they were impressed. We rode\r\nfor hours through the woods this morning and they didn\'t leave a stick\r\nof timber unscrutinized. We were out on the lake, too, and they were\r\nlike ferrets into every cranny of the mill."\r\n\r\n"But won\'t that give you more to do?"\r\n\r\n"No, it will give me less: division of labor, don\'t you see? It will\r\ngive me more time to be with you."\r\n\r\n"And to help with the plantation," his wife suggested.\r\n\r\n"No, no, Madame Th\xe9r\xe8se," he laughed, "I\'ll not rob you of your\r\noccupation. I\'ll put no bungling hand into your concerns. I know a\r\nsound piece of timber when I see it; but I should hardly be able to\r\ntell a sample of Sea Island cotton from the veriest low middling."\r\n\r\n"Oh, that\'s absurd, David. Do you know you\'re getting to talk such\r\nnonsense since we\'re married; you remind me sometimes of Melicent."\r\n\r\n"Of Melicent? Heaven forbid! Why, I have a letter from her," he said,\r\nfeeling in his breast pocket. "The size and substance of it have\r\nactually weighted my pocket the whole day."\r\n\r\n"Melicent talking weighty things? That\'s something new," said Th\xe9r\xe8se\r\ninterested.\r\n\r\n"Is Melicent ever anything else than new?" he enquired.\r\n\r\nThey went and sat together on the bench at the corner of the veranda,\r\nwhere the fading Western light came over their shoulders. A quizzical\r\nsmile came into his eyes as he unfolded his sister\'s letter--with\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se still holding his arm and sitting very close to him.\r\n\r\n"Well," he said, glancing over the first few pages--his wife\r\nfollowing--"she\'s given up her charming little flat and her quaint\r\nlittle English woman: concludes I was right about the expense, etc.,\r\netc. But here comes the gist of the matter," he said, reading from the\r\nletter--"\xa0\'I know you won\'t object to the trip, David, I have my heart\r\nso set on it. The expense will be trifling, seeing there are four of\r\nus to divide carriage hire, restaurant and all that: and it counts.\r\n\r\n"\xa0\'If you only knew Mrs. Griesmann I\'d feel confident of your consent.\r\nYou\'d be perfectly fascinated with her. She\'s one of those highly\r\ngifted women who knows everything. She\'s very much interested in me.\r\nThinks to have found that I have a quick comprehensive intellectualism\r\n(she calls it) that has been misdirected. I think there is something\r\nin that, David; you know yourself I never did care really for society.\r\nShe says it\'s impossible to ever come to a true knowledge of life as\r\nit is--which should be every one\'s aim--without studying certain\r\nfundamental truths and things.\'\xa0"\r\n\r\n"Oh," breathed Th\xe9r\xe8se, overawed.\r\n\r\n"But wait--but listen," said Hosmer, "\xa0\'Natural History and all\r\nthat--and we\'re going to take that magnificent trip through the\r\nWest--the Yosemite and so forth. It appears the flora of California is\r\nespecially interesting and we\'re to carry those delicious little tin\r\nboxes strapped over our shoulders to hold specimens. Her son and\r\ndaughter are both, in their way, striking. He isn\'t handsome; rather\r\nthe contrary; but so serene and collected--so intensely bitter--his\r\nmother tells me he\'s a pessimist. And the daughter really puts me to\r\nshame, child as she is, with the amount of her knowledge. She labels\r\nall her mother\'s specimens in Latin. Oh, I feel there\'s so much to be\r\nlearned. Mrs. Griesmann thinks I ought to wear glasses during the\r\ntrip. Says we often require them without knowing it ourselves--that\r\nthey are so restful. She has some theory about it. I\'m trying a pair,\r\nand see a great deal better through them than I expected to. Only they\r\ndon\'t hold on very well, especially when I laugh.\r\n\r\n"\xa0\'Who do you suppose seized on to me in Vandervoort\'s the other day,\r\nbut that impertinent Mrs. Belle Worthington! Positively took me by the\r\ncoat and commenced to gush about dear sister Th\xe9r\xe8se. She said: "I\r\ntell you what, my dear--" called me my dear at the highest pitch, and\r\nthat odious Mrs. Van Wycke behind us listening and pretending to\r\nexamine a lace handkerchief. "That Mrs. Lafirme\'s a trump," she\r\nsaid--"too good for most any man. Hope you won\'t take offense, but I\r\nmust say, your brother David\'s a perfect stick--it\'s what I always\r\nsaid." Can you conceive of such shocking impertinence?\'\r\n\r\n"Well; Belle Worthington does possess the virtue of candor," said\r\nHosmer amused and folding the letter. "That\'s about all there is,\r\nexcept a piece of scandal concerning people you don\'t know; that\r\nwouldn\'t interest you."\r\n\r\n"But it would interest me," Th\xe9r\xe8se insisted, with a little wifely\r\nresentment that her husband should have a knowledge of people that\r\nexcluded her.\r\n\r\n"Then you shall hear it," he said, turning to the letter again. "Let\'s\r\nsee--\'conceive--shocking impertinence--\' oh, here it is.\r\n\r\n"\xa0\'Don\'t know if you have learned the horrible scandal; too dreadful\r\nto talk about. I shall send you the paper. I always knew that Lou\r\nDawson was a perfidious creature--and Bert Rodney! You never did like\r\nhim, David; but he was always so much the gentleman in his\r\nmanners--you must admit that. Who could have dreamed it of him. Poor\r\nMrs. Rodney is after all the one to be pitied. She is utterly\r\nprostrated. Refuses to see even her most intimate friends. It all came\r\nof those two vile wretches thinking Jack Dawson out of town when he\r\nwasn\'t; for he was right there following them around in their\r\nperambulations. And the outcome is that Mr. Rodney has his beauty\r\nspoiled they say forever; the shot came very near being fatal. But\r\npoor, poor Mrs. Rodney!\r\n\r\n"\xa0\'Well, good-bye, you dearest David mine. How I wish you both knew\r\nMrs. Griesmann. Give that sweet sister Th\xe9r\xe8se as many kisses as she\r\nwill stand for me.\r\n\r\n                                   Melicent.\'\xa0"\r\n\r\nThis time Hosmer put the letter into his pocket, and Th\xe9r\xe8se asked\r\nwith a little puzzled air: "What do you suppose is going to become of\r\nMelicent, anyway, David?"\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know, love, unless she marries my friend Homeyer."\r\n\r\n"Now, David, you are trying to mystify me. I believe there\'s a streak\r\nof perversity in you after all."\r\n\r\n"Of course there is; and here comes Mandy to say that \'suppa\'s gittin\'\r\ncole.\'\xa0"\r\n\r\n"Aunt B\'lindy \'low suppa on de table gittin\' cole," said Mandy,\r\nretreating at once from the fire of their merriment.\r\n\r\nTh\xe9r\xe8se arose and held her two hands out to her husband.\r\n\r\nHe took them but did not rise; only leaned further back on the seat\r\nand looked up at her.\r\n\r\n"Oh, supper\'s a bore; don\'t you think so?" he asked.\r\n\r\n"No, I don\'t," she replied. "I\'m hungry, and so are you. Come, David."\r\n\r\n"But look, Th\xe9r\xe8se, just when the moon has climbed over the top of\r\nthat live-oak? We can\'t go now. And then Melicent\'s request; we must\r\nthink about that."\r\n\r\n"Oh, surely not, David," she said, drawing back.\r\n\r\n"Then let me tell you something," and he drew her head down and\r\nwhispered something in her pink ear that he just brushed with his\r\nlips. It made Th\xe9r\xe8se laugh and turn very rosy in the moonlight.\r\n\r\nCan that be Hosmer? Is this Th\xe9r\xe8se? Fie, fie. It is time we were\r\nleaving them.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of the Project Gutenberg EBook of At Fault, by Kate Chopin\r\n\r\n*** END OF THIS PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK AT FAULT ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 23810-8.txt or 23810-8.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n        http://www.gutenberg.org/2/3/8/1/23810/\r\n\r\nProduced by Bernard Koloski\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions\r\nwill be renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from public domain print editions means that no\r\none owns a United States copyright in these works, so the Foundation\r\n(and you!) can copy and distribute it in the United States without\r\npermission and without paying copyright royalties.
